Four Steps to Oil: A Design Argument from Animal Behavior
Lists four points about an animal's need for oil and its fit with a flask.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple four-point observation about an animal's behavior can serve as a rational argument for purposeful design.

Core Idea
An animal's steps toward oil reveal purposeful design.

This passage presents a concrete example of means-end reasoning from the natural world. By examining how an animal successfully extracts oil from a flask, the author argues that the animal's actions are rationally guided toward a goal. This is a classic illustration used in arguments for divine existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the sequential logic from need to action.
This is a compressed argument – each point builds on the last.
2
Identify the four points
List the four observations in order. Notice how the third point (tail enters) resolves the problem posed by the second (head does not enter).
The animal's purpose is to obtain oil.
3
Reflect on the argument's significance
Consider why the author lists these points. The implicit conclusion is that the animal's behavior is not random but guided toward a goal – supporting design.
This is a micro-argument for design that can be scaled up to the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإحداها : أنها محتاجة إلى الدهن . وثانيها : أن رأسها لا تدخل في القارورة . وثالثها : أن ذنبها يدخل . ورابعها : أن المقصود حاصل بهذا الطريق ، فوجب الإقدام عليه
One of them: that it needs oil. Second: that its head does not enter the flask. Third: that its tail enters. Fourth: that the purpose is achieved by this method, so it must proceed.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents a four-point argument about a particular animal (unidentified from the preceding text). The author lists successive observations: first, the animal needs oil; second, its head does not fit into the flask containing the oil; third, its tail does enter; fourth, since the purpose (presumably extracting the oil) is achieved through this method, the animal necessarily proceeds with the action. The implicit conclusion is that the animal's behavior is rationally guided toward a goal, demonstrating design.

Key Terms
دهن
قارورة
رأس
ذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The animal's behavior is purely instinctual without any purpose or design.
Correction: The author argues that the animal's actions are rationally guided by the goal of obtaining oil, as shown by the step-by-step reasoning in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the four points, why does the animal proceed with the action?
A) Because it is forced by nature
B) Because the purpose is achieved by this method
C) Because its head fits the flask
D) Because it desires the oil

Key Takeaway
The animal's need for oil, combined with the fit of its tail, demonstrates that the animal's actions are purposive and rationally guided – a microcosm of design.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the simple, purposeful action of an animal retrieving oil, do you see it as a sign of a careful Creator, or do you dismiss it as mere instinct? How does your answer shape your daily awareness of God's presence?
?
The animal's head didn't fit, but its tail did—showing a designed solution. Think of a recent obstacle in your life where a different, unexpected path achieved the goal. Did you see that as divine guidance, or just luck? How can you train yourself to recognize design in your own circumstances?
?
If an animal's tail fitting a flask is enough to demonstrate purpose, what does the intricate design of your own body and abilities say about the One who made you? Are you using those gifts for the purpose they were given, or are you living as if they are accidental?

Bees and the Hexagon: Divine Inspiration in Nature
Argues that bees' hexagonal cells demonstrate divine inspiration and surpass human ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometry of bees' hexagonal cells points to divine inspiration and surpasses unaided human ability.

Core Idea
The hexagon's unique geometry shows bees are guided by God, not blind instinct.

In this passage, a classical theologian uses the familiar example of a beehive to argue for divine design. He points out two remarkable facts: the hexagon is the most efficient shape for filling a space, and bees construct it without tools. These observations challenge the idea that nature operates by mere chance or instinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: first the shape classification, then the two benefits, then the conclusion about inspiration.
2
Understand the shape classification
The author divides shapes into two types: those that fill a circle and those that don't. Among those that fill, some have narrow angles (waste); among those that don't, some have wide angles (gaps). Only the hexagon has both benefits.
This is a geometric argument.
3
Identify the two wonders
The first wonder is that bees choose the optimal shape. The second wonder is that they build it without tools, which humans need. Both point to divine inspiration.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: If bees acted by instinct alone, would they choose the most efficient shape? The author says no—this requires divine guidance.
This is an argument from design (teleological argument).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن النحل يبني البيوت المسدسة . وهذا الشكل فيه منفعتان لا تحصلان إلا من المسدس . وتقريره : أن الأشكال على قسمين : منها أشكال متى ضم بعضها إلى بعض ، امتلأت القرصة منها، ومنها : أشكال ليست كذلك فالقسم الأول : كالمثلثات والمربعات فإنهما وإن امتلأت الفرصة منهما ، إلا أن زواياهما ضيقة ، فتبقى معطلة . وأما المسبع والمثمن وغيرهما ، فزواياهم وإن كانت واسعة ، إلا أنه لا تملأ القرصة منهم ، بل يبقى بينهم فضاء [ معطل ] وأما الشكل المستجمع لكلتي المنفعتين ، فليس إلا المسدس . وذلك لأن زواياه واسعة ، فلا يبقى شيء من الجوانب فيه معطلاً ، وإذا ضمت المسدسات بعضها إلى بعض ، لم يبق فيما بينها فرجة ضائعة . وإذا ثبت أن الشكل الموصوف بهاتين الصفتين هو المسدس ، لا جرم اختارت النحل بناء بيوتها على هذا الشكل ، ولولا أنه تعالى أعطاها من الإلهام والذكاء ، وإلا لما حصل هذا الأمر . وفيه أعجوة ثانية. وهي : أن البشر لا يقدرون على بناء البيت المسدس إلا بالمسطر والفرجار . والنحل يبني تلك البيوت من غير حاجة إلى شيء من الآلات والأدوات . واعلم : أن عجائب أحوال النحل في رئاسته ، وفي تدبيره لأحوال الرعية ، وفي كيفية خدمة الرعية لذلك الرئيس : كثيرة ، مذكورة في كتاب الحيوان .
The second: That the bee builds hexagonal houses. And this shape has two benefits that are only obtained from the hexagon. Its explanation: Shapes are of two types: shapes which, when joined together, fill the circle, and shapes which are not like that. The first type: like triangles and squares, for although the circle is filled by them, their angles are narrow, so they remain wasted. As for the heptagon, octagon and others, their angles are wide, but they do not fill the circle; rather a wasted space remains between them. And the shape that combines both benefits is none other than the hexagon. This is because its angles are wide, so nothing of the sides is wasted, and when hexagons are joined together, no gap is left between them. And when it is established that the shape described by these two attributes is the hexagon, inevitably the bees chose to build their houses on this shape. And if God Most High had not given them inspiration and intelligence, this would not have happened. And in it is a second wonder: that humans cannot build a hexagonal house except with a ruler and compass. But the bees build those houses without need for any tools or instruments. And know: that the wonders of the state of bees in their leadership, and in their management of the subjects, and in the manner of service of the subjects to that leader, are many, mentioned in the Book of Animals.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an argument from design, presenting two wonders of bees. First, the author divides shapes into two classes: those that fill a circle (like triangles, squares) and those that don't (heptagon, octagon). Among those that fill, triangles and squares have narrow angles leaving wasted space; among those with wide angles, they leave gaps. The hexagon uniquely combines both benefits: it fills the circle completely and has wide angles, so no space is wasted. Since the hexagon is the optimal shape, bees must have chosen it through divine inspiration, not mere instinct. Second, humans require tools (ruler and compass) to construct hexagons, but bees do so without any instruments, demonstrating a superior intelligence granted by God. The passage concludes with a note that many more wonders of bees are recorded in the Book of Animals.

Key Terms
مسدس
قرصة
زوايا
إلهام
مسطر
فرجار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bees build hexagonal cells by blind instinct.
Correction: The passage argues that the hexagon's optimal geometry and the bees' ability to build it without tools require divine inspiration, not mere instinct.

Checkpoint
Why does the hexagon combine both benefits mentioned in the passage?
A) Because it has narrow angles and fills the circle
B) Because it has wide angles and fills the circle without gaps
C) Because it is the easiest shape to construct
D) Because it requires no tools

Key Takeaway
The hexagonal structure of beehives demonstrates divine inspiration, as it is the most efficient shape and requires no tools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design of a beehive, do you truly see it as a sign of God's direct guidance, or do you subconsciously attribute it to mere coincidence or instinct? In what area of your life are you still failing to see God's hand, attributing success to your own ability? Commit today to recognizing one blessing as coming solely from Him.
?
Bees build perfect hexagons without any tools, yet you, with all your resources, cannot match their precision without instruments. How does that humbling truth sit with your pride? Will you let it drive you to worship the One who taught the bee, or will you remain arrogant? Identify one area where you feel self-sufficient and ask God to remind you of your dependence.
?
If a simple bee is guided by divine inspiration to perform a task that humans cannot replicate without tools, what does that say about the more complex systems in your own body—your heartbeat, your thoughts, your very existence? Are you living as if these are just biological processes, or do you see the constant divine craftsmanship? Write down one specific sign of God in your daily life that you will consciously acknowledge tomorrow.

The Ant's Wisdom in Preparing and Preserving Food
Argues that ants store food and split grains with foresight and adaptation to moisture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ant's behavior of storing food and its three specific actions demonstrate intelligent design, pointing to divine guidance.

Core Idea
Ants exhibit rational foresight and problem-solving, not mere instinct.

This lesson examines the ant as a sign of divine wisdom. The ant's activities—storing food for future need, splitting grains to prevent spoilage, drying damp items, and predicting weather—reveal remarkable intelligence. The author argues that such purposeful behavior cannot be accidental and indicates a guiding Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: a general statement about storing food, then three enumerated wonders.
The translation includes a bracketed phrase [ في الشمس ] which is a variant.
2
Identify the three wonders
List the three specific behaviors: splitting grains, drying in the sun, and moving belongings before rain. Understand why each is considered a wonder.
3
Reflect on the argument
Consider why the author uses the ant's intelligence as evidence for design. What does this imply about the source of such intelligence?
This connects to broader kalam arguments for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن النملة تسعى في إعداد الذخيرة لنفسها ، وما ذاك إلا لعلمها بأنها قد تحتاج في الأزمنة المستقبلة إلى الغذاء ولا تكون قادرة على تحصيله في تلك الأوقات ، فوجب السعي في تحصيله في هذا الوقت، الذي حصلت فيه القدرة على تحصيل الذخيرة . ومن عجائب أحوالها أمور ثلاثة : أحدها : إنها إذا أحست بنداوة المكان ، فإنها تشق الحبة نصفين لعلمها بأن الحبة لو بقيت سليمة ، ووصلت إليها النداوة لنبت منها النبات فتفسد الحبة على النملة . أما إذا صارت مشقوقة نصفين لم تنبت . وثانيها : إنه إذا وصلت النداوة إلى تلك الأشياء ، ثم طلعت الشمس ، فإنها تخرج تلك الأشياء من جحرها ، وتضعها [ في الشمس ] حتى تجف . وثالثها : إن النملة إذا أخذت في نقل متاعها إلى داخل الجحر أنذر ذلك بنزول الأمطار وهبوب الرياح. وهذه الأحوال تدل على حصول ذكاء عظيم لهذا الحيوان الصغير .
The third: That the ant strives to prepare provisions for itself, and that is only because it knows that it may need food in future times and will not be able to obtain it at those times, so it must strive to obtain it at this time, when it has the ability to obtain provisions. And of the wonders of its states are three things: One of them: that when it senses the dampness of a place, it splits the grain in half, because it knows that if the grain remained whole and moisture reached it, a plant would sprout from it and spoil the grain for the ant. But if it is split in half, it does not sprout. The second: that when moisture reaches those things, then the sun rises, it takes those things out of its hole and puts them [in the sun] until they dry. The third: that when the ant begins to move its belongings into its hole, that warns of the coming of rain and the blowing of winds. And these states indicate the attainment of great intelligence for this small animal.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents the ant as an argument for design, focusing on its intelligence. First, the ant stores food for future need, knowing it cannot obtain it later—a rational calculation. Then three specific wonders: (1) Sensing dampness, it splits grains to prevent sprouting; (2) It dries damp items in the sun; (3) Its movement of belongings into the hole predicts rain and wind. All these indicate that the ant possesses great intelligence, which the author implies comes from divine guidance.

Key Terms
ذخيرة
نملة
نداوة
شق
جب
شمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ants act purely by instinct without any intelligence.
Correction: The passage argues that ants display rational foresight and problem-solving, indicating intelligence beyond blind instinct.

Checkpoint
Why does the ant split a grain in half when it senses dampness?
A) To eat it easily
B) To prevent it from sprouting and spoiling
C) To make it lighter to carry
D) To share it with other ants

Key Takeaway
The ant's behaviors—storing food, splitting grains, drying items, and predicting rain—demonstrate intelligent design pointing to divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ant splits a grain to prevent future spoilage. Are you as careful to prepare your spiritual provisions for the afterlife (e.g., deeds, repentance) as the ant is for its food? What one concrete change will you make today to store up good deeds?
?
When the ant senses dampness, it acts immediately to prevent decay. Is there any 'spiritual dampness' (sins, distractions, bad habits) in your life that you have been neglecting? What step will you take today to dry your heart in the 'sun' of good deeds?
?
The ant's movement warns others of rain. Do your actions warn those around you of spiritual dangers (e.g., heedlessness, sin), or do you remain silent out of comfort? What specific warning do you need to give to someone this week, and how will you deliver it with love?

Spider's Web: Evidence of Intelligent Design
Argues that the spider's web construction shows thoughtful design comparable to human reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the spider's web construction is presented as evidence of thoughtful intelligence, comparable to human reasoning, and how this points to divine wisdom.

Core Idea
The spider's web shows intelligent design that rivals human thought.

In this passage, the author presents the fourth argument for divine wisdom from the animal kingdom: the spider. At first glance, a spider web might seem like simple instinct, but the author insists it involves thoughtful planning. Let's see how the spider's actions are compared to human intellectual processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the author's direct claim.
Notice the phrase 'after it has thought' – this is the core of the argument.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that the spider weaves its web only after thinking about its proper arrangement to catch flies. This is not random action.
Think about what 'thinking' means for a spider – the author compares it to human ideas.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that these actions are intellectual (fikriyya) and not less than human ideas. Consider why the author makes this strong comparison.
This elevates the spider's behavior from mere instinct to a sign of intelligence, pointing to a wise Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن العنكبوت ، تبني بيوتها على وجه عجيب . وذلك لأنها ما نسجت الشبكة التي هي مصيدتها ، إلا بعد أن تفكرت أنه كيف ينبغي أن يكون وضعها ، حتى تصلح لاصطياد الذباب بها . وهذه الأفعال فكرية ، وليست أقل من الأفكار الإنسانية
The fourth: That the spider builds its houses in a wondrous manner. That is because it only weaves the web which is its trap after it has thought about how its arrangement ought to be, so that it is suitable for catching flies with it. And these actions are intellectual, and not less than human ideas.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the spider's web construction as an argument for intelligence. The spider does not act blindly; it first thinks about the proper arrangement of its web to serve as a trap for flies. The author explicitly states that these actions are intellectual (fikriyya) and not inferior to human thoughts, implying that the spider's intelligence is a sign of divine wisdom.

Key Terms
عنكبوت
شبكة
مصيدة
تفكر
أفكار فكرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spider webs are purely instinctual and show no intelligence.
Correction: The passage argues that the spider's actions are intellectual and deliberate, showing thoughtful design.

Checkpoint
What does the spider do before weaving its web according to the passage?
A) It searches for a suitable location
B) It thinks about how to arrange the web
C) It waits for prey to appear
D) It repairs an old web

Key Takeaway
The spider's thoughtful web construction is a sign of divine wisdom and intelligence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a spider web, do you recognize the intelligence behind it as a sign from your Creator, or do you pass by in heedlessness? How can you train your heart to see Allah's wisdom in every created thing?
?
The spider thoughtfully arranges its web for survival. What have you thoughtfully arranged today to draw closer to Allah? Is your life's 'web' — your daily actions — as purposefully designed as that of a small creature?
?
If a spider's action is considered intellectual and not less than human ideas, then why do you sometimes act with less care and purpose than a spider? How can you elevate your actions to be more thoughtful and worshipful?

Camel and Donkey Navigation: Evidence of Divine Design
The author presents the fifth argument: animal navigation as evidence of divine guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the instinctive navigational ability of camels and donkeys serves as a teleological argument for divine guidance.

Core Idea
Animal instinct points beyond itself to a wise Creator.

In this passage, the author presents a fifth argument for the existence of a guiding Creator: the remarkable ability of camels and donkeys to retrace paths in total darkness without any external teacher. This instinctive navigation, often superior to human reason in such contexts, suggests a source of guidance beyond the animals themselves. Let's examine the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own observation come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text is cut off at the end.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that camels and donkeys can travel a path a second time without any guide or teacher. This is the core observation.
3
Consider the human contrast
The author begins to contrast this with human inability. Reflect on why pointing out human limitation strengthens the argument for divine intervention.
The full contrast is missing, but the intent is clear.
4
Ask: What does this imply?
Think about the source of this ability. If no external guide is needed, what or who provided this instinct? This leads to the idea of a wise Creator.
This connects the passage to the broader theme of divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الجمل والحمار إذا سلكا طريقاً في الليلة الظلماء ففي المرة الثانية يقدر على سلوك ذلك الطريق من غير إرشاد مرشد ، أو تعليم معلم أن الناس إذا ضلوا في ذلك الطريق ، وقدموا الجمل وتبعوه ، يجدون الطريق المستقيم عند متابعته . وأيضاً : فإن الإنسان لا يمكنه الانتقال
The fifth: That the camel and the donkey, if they travel a path on a dark night, then on the second time they are able to travel that path without guidance from a guide or instruction from a teacher. Indeed, when people get lost on that path and put the camel forward and follow it, they find the straight path when following it. Also: that a human cannot travel
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the navigational ability of camels and donkeys as an argument for design. These animals can retrace a path in complete darkness on a second occasion without any external guidance. Moreover, when humans are lost, they can follow the camel and find the correct route. The author begins to contrast this with human inability, but the text is cut off. The implication is that the animals possess a divinely given memory or instinct superior to human reasoning in this context.

Key Terms

طريق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals navigate purely by memory or trial and error without divine involvement.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that no guide or teacher is involved, pointing to an innate divinely placed instinct.

Checkpoint

A) They can find water sources
B) They can retrace a path a second time without guidance
C) They can predict the weather
D) They can communicate with humans

Key Takeaway
Camel and donkey navigation is presented as evidence that a guiding Creator has placed instinct in animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have been lost—mentally, spiritually, or physically—did you ever notice something humble (like an animal or a simple sign) guiding you back? How does that reveal a Guiding Hand beyond your own planning?
?
You rely on your own intelligence and learning daily. But what about the countless instincts in animals that you cannot teach or replicate? How does that confront your pride and point you to the necessity of a wise Creator?
?
If a camel can find its way without a teacher, why do you so often demand clear external signs before trusting the Creator’s guidance in your life? What step can you take today to follow His instinctive signs?

The Three Principles of Human Action
The author defines the three ordered principles of human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ordered stages that constitute human action: knowledge, inclination, and bodily power, and how they causally lead to action.

Core Idea
Human action arises from three necessary stages: knowledge, inclination, and power.

In this passage, the author breaks down the structure of human action into three sequential principles. He shows that every voluntary action begins with a belief about benefit or harm, which then generates an inclination, and finally combines with physical ability to produce the act. This provides a clear causal framework for understanding why we do what we do.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the three stages. Notice how the author orders them from highest/farthest to last/closest.
The passage refers to a previous chapter on motives and deterrents; this is background, not required to understand the three stages.
2
Identify each stage
Identify the three stages: (1) knowledge and perception, (2) strong inclination, (3) power in the limb. Note the causal link between each stage.
The first stage is about belief in benefit or harm; the second is the necessary inclination that follows; the third combines inclination with physical capability.
3
Analyze the causal relationships
See how each stage necessarily leads to the next. Knowledge of benefit/harm causes inclination, and inclination combined with power produces the act.
The author uses 'luzūm' (necessity) to emphasize the deterministic causal chain.
4
Reflect on the definition of free agent
The passage states that because humans act seeking good, they are called free agents (fāʿil mukhtār). Consider how the causal chain does not negate free will but explains its mechanism.
This connects to broader theological debates about free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن الأفعال الإنسانية لها مبادى ثلاثة ، مرتبة بعضها على بعض . فأعلاها وأبعدها عن قوع الفعل : العلم والإدراك . وذلك لأنه ما لم يقع في قلبه : أن الفعل الفلاني راجح [ النفع ، أو خالص النفع ، فإنه لا يفعله . وما لم يقع في قلبه أن الفعل الفلاني راجح الضرر ] أو خالص الضرر أو معادل الضرر ، فإنه لا يتركه . وقد بالغنا في تقرير هذه القاعدة في باب « الدواعي والصوارف ) من هذا الكتاب . ولأجل أن الإنسان لا يفعل فعلا ، إلا لطلب الخير، سمي هذا الفاعل فاعلاً مختاراً . والمرتبة الثانية - وهي المرتبة المتوسطة . أنه إذا حصل اعتقاد النفع الخالص أو الراجح ، ترتب عليه على سبيل اللزوم : حصول ميل قوي متأكد [ في الفعل . وإذا حصل اعتقاد الضرر الخالص أو الراجح ترتب عليه على سبيل اللزوم حصول ميل قوي متأكد في الترك ] . والمرتبة الثالثة - وهي المرتبة الأخيرة القريبة المتصلة بالفعل - أن الأعضاء إذا كانت سليمة قوية على الفعل والترك . فإذا انضم ذلك الميل المتعين إلى تلك القدرة القائمة بالعضو ، صار مجموع ذلك الميل وتلك القدرة : مؤثراً في حصول الفعل وهذا كلام ملخص معلوم قد سبق تقريره في باب الدواعي والصوارف ) .
Know that human actions have three principles, arranged one upon another. The highest and farthest from the occurrence of the act is knowledge and perception. That is because unless it occurs in his heart that such-and-such an act is preponderant in benefit, or purely beneficial, he will not do it. And unless it occurs in his heart that such-and-such an act is preponderant in harm, or purely harmful, or equal in harm, he will not abandon it. We have insisted on establishing this rule in the chapter on 'Motives and Deterrents' of this book. And because a man does not perform an act except seeking good, this agent is called a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). The second stage — the intermediate stage — is that when belief in pure or preponderant benefit occurs, there follows necessarily a strong, confirmed inclination towards the act. And when belief in pure or preponderant harm occurs, there follows necessarily a strong, confirmed inclination towards avoidance. The third stage — the last stage close and connected to the act — is that if the limbs are sound and capable of both act and omission; then when that determined inclination joins with the power residing in the limb, the combination of that inclination and that power becomes effective in producing the act. This is a summarized, well-known statement that has already been established in the chapter on 'Motives and Deterrents'.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage defines the three ordered principles of human action. It begins by stating that human acts have three principles arranged sequentially. The first and most remote is knowledge and perception: without a belief in benefit or harm, no action or avoidance occurs. The author refers to his earlier chapter on motives and deterrents. Because humans only act for good, they are called free agents. The second stage is the necessary generation of inclination from belief in benefit or harm. The third stage is the actual power (qudra) of the limbs combined with the determined inclination, which together produce the act. The passage emphasizes the necessary causal links between these stages.

Key Terms
العلم والإدراك
الميل القوي
القدرة القائمة بالعضو
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think human actions are purely random or uncaused, or that they arise only from raw impulse without prior knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that every voluntary action is preceded by a belief about benefit/harm, which then necessarily generates an inclination, and only then, combined with physical power, produces the act. This highlights a causal structure.

Checkpoint
How many principles (stages) does the author identify for human actions?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Human actions are the result of a causal chain of knowledge, inclination, and power, which defines the human as a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you did something you knew was harmful. According to this lesson, that should be impossible if you truly believed it was purely or preponderantly harmful. Was your belief genuine? Or did you deceive yourself? What does that say about the state of your heart?
?
The passage teaches that your actions are chained to your beliefs and inclinations. If you want to change your actions, you must first change what you truly believe is beneficial. Is there an area of your life where your actions contradict the teachings of Islam? Start tracing back: do you truly believe that obedience is more beneficial than sin? If not, ask Allah to grant you that conviction.
?
You are called a 'free agent' because you act seeking good. But how often do you reflect on whether your actions are truly seeking the good that Allah has defined? Today, before you act, pause and ask: 'What do I believe about the benefit of this action?' Let that knowledge shape your inclination, and let your inclination lead to righteous action.

How Whispers and Inspiration Influence Action
Proof of the first rank for whispers and inspiration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific rank at which demonic whispers and angelic inspiration influence human actions, and why they cannot affect the later ranks.

Core Idea
Whispers and inspiration only remind you of what you already know.

This passage presents a logical argument to determine how external influences like devilish whispers and angelic inspiration affect our actions. The author identifies three possible 'ranks' of causation and systematically eliminates two, leaving only the first rank where these forces operate by reminding us of forgotten considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ranks
Read the passage to find the three ranks mentioned: the last, intermediate, and first. Understand what each rank refers to in the causal chain of action.
The ranks correspond to different stages: the final causing of the act, the formation of intention, and the initial awareness.
2
Understand why the last rank is impossible
The author argues that the combination of ability (qudra) and inclination (mayl) produces the act necessarily. The Necessary by itself cannot be affected by another. Therefore, whispers cannot influence this rank.
'Necessary by itself' may refer to the self or the causal nexus, which is self-sufficient.
3
Understand why the intermediate rank is impossible
The belief that an act contains benefit or harm produces will or aversion necessarily. Necessary things cannot be influenced by another. So whispers cannot affect this rank either.
This eliminates the stage where intention is formed.
4
Conclude the first rank
With the first two ranks invalid, the only remaining possibility is the first rank: the initial awareness. Whispers and inspiration merely remind a person of forgotten pleasures or pains associated with an act.
This aligns with the Qur'anic verse where the devil says he only invited, not compelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: وسوسة الشياطين وإلهام الملك. إما أن يقعا في المرتبة الأخيرة، أو في المرتبة المتوسطة، أو في المرتبة الأولى. لا جائز وقوعها في المرتبة الأخيرة، لأن تأثير مجموع القدرة مع الميل في الفعل: أمر ضروري لازم. والواجب لذاته، يمتنع أن يكون لغيره فيه أثر. ولا جائز أيضاً أن يحصل للإلهام والوسوسة أثر في المرتبة المتوسطة. لأن تأثير اعتقاد أنه مشتمل على النفع الخالص أو الراجح في وجود إرادة الفعل: تأثير ذاتي ضروري [وكذلك تأثير اعتقاد أنه مشتمل على الضرر الخالص أو الراجح في وجود كراهية الفعل تأثير ضروري] والأشياء الضرورية، يمتنع أن يكون لغيرها فيها أثر. ولما بطل هذان القسمان، تعين أن يكون تأثير الوسوسة والإلهام في المرتبة الأولى فقط مثل: أنه كان ناسياً [لما في المعصية الفلانية من اللذة والطيب والراحة، فالشيطان يذكره بهذه الحالة. أو كان ناسياً لما] في الطاعة الفلانية من البهجة والسعادة والآثار التامة الكاملة، فالملك يذكره ذلك. ولما تلخص هذا البحث الشريف العالي، ظهر بهذا صدق ما ورد في الكتاب الإلهي. حيث أخبر عن الشيطان أنه قال: «وما كان لي عليكم من سلطان، إلا أن دعوتكم، فاستجبتم لي، فلا تلوموني ولوموا أنفسكم».
And when this is established, we say: The whispering of the devils and the inspiration of the angel. Either they occur in the last rank, or in the intermediate rank, or in the first rank. It is not permissible for them to occur in the last rank, because the effect of the combination of power (qudra) with inclination (mayl) upon the act is a necessary and necessary matter. And the Necessary by itself (al-wājib li-dhātih) cannot have any effect from another upon it. It is also not permissible for inspiration and whispering to have an effect in the intermediate rank. Because the effect of the belief that it contains pure or preponderant benefit upon the existence of the will of the act is an intrinsic necessary effect [and similarly the effect of the belief that it contains pure or preponderant harm upon the existence of aversion of the act is a necessary effect]. And necessary things cannot have any effect from another upon them. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it is determined that the effect of whispering and inspiration is only in the first rank, such as: he was forgetful [of what is in a certain sin of pleasure, delight, and comfort, so the devil reminds him of this state. Or he was forgetful of what] is in a certain act of obedience of joy, happiness, and perfect complete effects, so the angel reminds him of that. And when this noble and lofty inquiry is summarized, by this the truth of what is mentioned in the Divine Book becomes apparent, where He informed about the devil that he said: 'And I had no authority over you except that I invited you, and you responded to me, so do not blame me but blame yourselves.'
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing the specific rank at which demonic whispers (waswasa) and angelic inspiration (ilhām) exert their influence on human actions. The author begins by stating the thesis: the whispers and inspiration must occur in one of three ranks: the last rank (al-martaba al-akhīra), the intermediate rank (al-mutawassiṭa), or the first rank (al-ūlā). He then argues that the last rank is impossible because the combination of power and inclination necessary for the act is a necessary (ḍarūrī) matter, and the Necessary by itself (God or perhaps the self) cannot be affected by another. Next, he argues that the intermediate rank is also impossible because the belief that an act contains benefit or harm produces will or aversion necessarily, and necessary things cannot be influenced by another. Since both these divisions are invalid, the only remaining possibility is that whispering and inspiration affect the first rank—specifically, by reminding a person of forgotten aspects of an act (e.g., pleasure in sin or joy in obedience). The author concludes that this is confirmed by the Qur'anic verse (Ibrahim 14:22) where the devil says he had no authority but only invitation.

إلهام
مرتبة
تأثير
قدرة
ميل
ضروري
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whispers directly cause a person to sin or inspiration directly causes obedience.
Correction: The passage shows that whispers and inspiration only remind the person of forgotten motives; they do not directly affect the will or power necessary for action.

Checkpoint
According to the author, in which rank do demonic whispers and angelic inspiration affect human action?
A) The last rank
B) The intermediate rank
C) The first rank
D) All three ranks

Key Takeaway
Demonic whispers and angelic inspiration only influence action by reminding a person of forgotten motives, not by directly affecting the will or power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you blame the devil's whisper or your own neglect to remember the consequences? If the whisper only reminds you of what you already forgot, what does that say about your responsibility to guard your heart?
?
If the only power of whispers is to remind you of forgotten pleasures, then what does that reveal about your own inclinations before the whisper? Are you already drawn to temporary comfort over obedience?
?
Think of a moment you felt inspired to do good. Did you credit the angel’s inspiration, or did you recognize that you already knew the value of that good but had forgotten? How can you train yourself to remember before being reminded?

First Branch: The Devil's Need for Assistance
The devil requires assistance from bodily and psychic forces to cast whispers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the devil's whispering depends on exploiting individual human temperaments and psychic inclinations.

Core Idea
The devil cannot whisper alone; he uses your own nature against you.

This lesson explains the first branch of the proof regarding the devil's inability to cast whispering without assistance. The author shows that the devil must work through the bodily temperament and the soul's faculties, because people vary greatly in their likes and dislikes. Understanding this helps clarify how both devilish whisper and angelic inspiration operate through the same psychological mechanisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Take a moment to read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before analyzing.
2
Identify the key terms
Notice how the author introduces two types of faculties: humoral (mizājiyya) and psychic (nafsāniyya). Also note the concepts of inclination (mayl) and aversion (nufra).
These terms are central to understanding the mechanism.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the devil's dependence on these faculties is significant for the broader argument about moral responsibility and divine assistance.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الأول: إن الشيطان لا يقوى على إلقاء الوسوسة، إلا بإعانة من القوى المزاجية، والقوى النفسانية. وذلك لأنا قد بينا: أن الناس مختلفون في الميل إلى بعض الأشياء، والنفرة عن بعض الأشياء. فالذي يريده هذا، يبغضه ذاك، وبالعكس. وأقسام هذه الاختلافات بحسب الرغبة في المال والجاه، وبحسب أقسام المطعومات والمشروبات والمنكوحات. والتفرجات: مختلفة غير مضبوطة. فإذا أراد الشيطان حمله على فعل من الأفعال القبيحة، ذكره ما فيه من اللذات والراحات بما يميل طبعه إليه. وإذا أراد الملك منعه عنه ذكره ما فيه من المكروهات التي ينفر طبعه عنها.
The first branch: Indeed, the devil is not able to cast whispering except with the assistance of the humoral faculties (al-quwā al-mizājiyya) and the psychic faculties (al-quwā al-nafsāniyya). That is because we have explained: people differ in inclination to some things and aversion from some things. So what this one desires, that one hates, and vice versa. And the divisions of these differences are according to desire for wealth and status, and according to the categories of foods, drinks, and spouses. And amusements: they are varied and not restricted. So when the devil wants to incite him to a reprehensible act, he reminds him of what is in it of pleasures and comforts to which his nature inclines. And when the angel wants to prevent him from it, he reminds him of what is in it of offensive things from which his nature averses.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is the first subsidiary branch (al-farʿ al-awwal) derived from the previous proof. The author states that the devil cannot cast whispering effectively without the help of two types of faculties: the humoral (mizājiyya, relating to bodily temperaments) and the psychic (nafsāniyya, relating to the soul). The reason is that people vary greatly in their inclinations (mayl) and aversions (nufra). What one person desires, another despises. These differences manifest in various domains: wealth, status, food, drink, marital relations, and amusements, which are limitless. When the devil wants to persuade someone to commit a bad deed, he reminds them of the pleasures and comforts that align with their particular nature. Conversely, when the angel wants to deter them, he reminds them of the harms or displeasures that their nature dislikes. Thus the effectiveness of both whisper and inspiration depends on the individual's specific temperament and psychic makeup.

Key Terms
قوى مزاجية (quwā mizājiyya)
قوى نفسانية (quwā nafsāniyya)
ميل (mayl)
نفرة (nufra)
طباع (ṭibāʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The devil has independent power to affect humans directly.
Correction: This passage shows his influence is mediated by exploiting innate human tendencies.

Checkpoint

B) Assistance from humoral and psychic faculties
C) Permission from God alone
D) The cooperation of other devils

Key Takeaway
The devil's whispering is effective only through the humoral and psychic faculties, exploiting the natural variability of human inclinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the devil whispers to you, which of your personal desires for wealth, status, food, or comfort does he use? Are you ready to counter that with the angel's reminder of the harm in that sin?
?
Think of a recurring sin you struggle with. Can you identify the specific pleasure or comfort the devil uses to lure you? What is the corresponding harm the angel would remind you of? Will you actively recall that harm next time you are tempted?
?
Your unique nature—your humoral and psychic makeup—is not an excuse but a battlefield. How will you train yourself to recognize when the devil is exploiting your natural inclinations, and immediately turn to the angel's reminder?

Introduction and Division of Motion into Three Types
Sanusi introduces the first proof and states the division of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall structure of the first philosophical proof that the heavens are living beings, and the classification of motion into natural, forced, and voluntary.

Core Idea
Motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary; for the heavens, only voluntary is possible.

This lesson introduces the first philosophical argument for the life of the celestial spheres. The proof begins with a simple but powerful division of all motion into three categories. By understanding this tripartite classification, you will grasp the foundation upon which the entire argument rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first mentions that there are multiple ways to prove the life of the heavens, then presents the first proof which is based on the three categories of motion.
The author uses both demonstrative and persuasive arguments.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage states that every moving object has motion that is either natural (ṭabī‘iyya), forced (qasriyya), or voluntary (irādiyya). Find these terms in the Arabic text.
These terms are key to the entire argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author asserts that the first two categories are false for celestial bodies, so the third (voluntary) is established. This implies the heavens are alive.
The detailed reasons for rejecting natural and forced motion come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على أن الأفلاك والكواكب أحياء عاقلة : وجوه . بعضها برهانية ، وبعضها إقناعية وقد ذكرنا مراراً : أن هذه المطالب العالية الشريفة الرفيعة ، يجب أن يبالغ الإنسان في معرفتها بأي طريق كان ، سواء كان برهانياً أو إقناعياً . الحجة الأولى : وهي الحجة الفلسفية القديمة - قالوا : ثبت إما بالحس وإما بالبرهان : أن الأفلاك والكواكب متحركة بالاستدارة ، وكل متحرك فحركته إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية . والأولان باطلان ، فتعين الثالث . وذلك يقتضي كون الأفلاك أحياء . أما بيان الحصر : فهو أنه قد ثبت في باب خواص الواجب والممكن : أن كل جسم يصدر عنه أثر مخصوص ، لا بالقسر ولا بالعرض، فإنه يجب أن يكون ذلك لقوة موجودة فيه ، تناسب ذلك الأثر . إذ لو لم يكن ذلك ، لكان حال ذلك الجسم بالنسبة إلى ذلك الأثر كحال سائر الأجسام بالنسبة إليه ، وكان ذلك التخصيص رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساويين على الآخر ، لا المرجح . وهو محال . فنقول : ذلك الأثر ، إذا لم يكن بالفسر ، فلا بد وأن يكون لقوة موجودة فيه . وتلك القوة إما أن يكون لها شعور بما صدر عنها ، وهي القوة الحيوانية الإرادية ، أو لا يكون لها شعور بذلك الأثر ، وهي القوة الطبيعية المحضة . وأما إذا صدر عنه ذلك الأثر بالقسر ، فهو الذي يكون بالقسر . فقد ثبت : أن كل جسم يختص بأثر ، فذلك الأثر إما أن يكون طبيعياً أو قسرياً أو إرادياً . وذلك يدل على صحة الحصر المذكور . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون حركة الفلك طبيعية ، لأن الحركة تقتضي إزالة حالة حاصلة . فتلك الحالة ، إما أن تكون طبيعية أو لا تكون ، فإن كانت طبيعية مع أنا فرضنا أن الطبيعية تقتضي إزالتها ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون وجود تلك الحالة طبيعياً وأن يكون زوالها طبيعياً ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين . وهو محال . فثبت : أن الطبيعية لا توجب الحركة ، إلا عند حصول حالة منافرة للطبيعة . فلا جرم أن الطبيعة توجب إزالتها . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلو كانت حركة الفلك طبيعية لكانت تلك الحركة هرباً عن حالة منافرة بالطبع . وذلك محال لوجهين : الأول : إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لكانت تلك الحركة مستقيمة . لأن الخط المستقيم أقصر من المستدير . ومتى أمكن اتصال الجسم إلى الحالة الطبيعية بالطريق الأسهل ، امتنع العدول إلى الطريق الأصعب . فثبت : أن كل حركة طبيعية ، فهي مستقيمة بالطبع. فينعكس انعكاس النقيض : أن ما لا يكون مستقيماً ، لا يكون طبيعياً . الثاني : إنه على التقدير الذي ذكرناه ، تكون الحركة هرباً طبيعياً عن تلك الحالة ، والهرب الطبيعي عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون عين الطلب الطبيعي لذلك الشيء ، لكن الحركة المستديرة عن النقطة المعينة ، عين الطلب لها ، فيمتنع كون الحركة المستديرة طبيعية. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كون الحركة الفلكية قسرية لوجوه : الأول : إنه يمتنع انتهاء كل قسر إلى قسر آخر ، لغير نهاية ، بل يجب الانتهاء في هذه الحركة الدورية إلى ما لا يكون بالقسر . والثاني : إن القسر لا يكون دائماً ، ولا أكثرياً . وهذه الحركة دائمة على نهج واحد . الثالث : إن القسر هو الذي يكون على خلاف الطبيعة ولما ثبت أن حركات الأفلاك يمتنع كونها طبيعية ، ظهر أنه يمتنع أيضاً كونها قسرية . فظهر بما ذكرنا : أن حركات الأفلاك إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية ويمتنع
And that which indicates that the celestial spheres and planets are living, rational beings: [there are] aspects. Some of them are demonstrative, and some of them are persuasive. And we have mentioned repeatedly: that these lofty, noble, elevated matters, a person must strive in knowing them by whatever means possible, whether demonstrative or persuasive. The first proof: and it is the ancient philosophical proof – they said: it is established, either by sense or by demonstration, that the celestial spheres and planets are moving in a circular motion, and every moving thing, its motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And the first two are false, so the third is determined. And that necessitates that the celestial spheres are living. As for the explanation of the exhaustive division: it is that it has been established in the chapter of the properties of the necessary and the possible: that every body from which proceeds a specific effect, neither by force nor accidentally, then it must be that this is due to a power existing in it, corresponding to that effect. For if that were not the case, the state of that body with respect to that effect would be like the state of other bodies with respect to it, and that specification would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the equally possible over the other without a preponderator. And that is impossible. So we say: that effect, if it is not by force, then it must be due to a power existing in it. And that power either has awareness of what proceeds from it – and it is the animal voluntary power – or it has no awareness of that effect – and it is the pure natural power. And as for when that effect proceeds from it by force, then it is that which is by force. So it is established: that every body that is distinguished by an effect, that effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And that indicates the correctness of the aforementioned exhaustive division. And we only said: that it is impossible for the motion of the celestial sphere to be natural, because motion necessitates removing an existing state. So that state is either natural or not. If it is natural while we have supposed that the natural [force] necessitates its removal, then it necessarily follows that the existence of that state is natural and that its removal is natural, and that necessitates combining two opposites. And that is impossible. So it is established: that nature does not necessitate motion except when a state contrary to nature occurs. So necessarily nature necessitates its removal. And if that is so, then if the motion of the celestial sphere were natural, that motion would be a flight from a state contrary to nature. And that is impossible for two reasons: First: if it were so, that motion would be straight. Because the straight line is shorter than the circular. And whenever the body can reach the natural state by the easier path, it is impossible to turn to the more difficult path. So it is established: that every natural motion is straight by nature. Then the contrapositive follows: that whatever is not straight is not natural. Second: upon the estimation we mentioned, the motion would be a natural flight from that state, and natural flight from a thing is impossible to be identical to natural seeking of that thing, but circular motion around a specific point is identical to the seeking of it, so it is impossible for circular motion to be natural. And we only said: that it is impossible for celestial motion to be forced for reasons: First: it is impossible for every force to terminate in another force ad infinitum, rather termination in this circular motion must be to that which is not by force. Second: force is not perpetual, nor for the most part. And this motion is perpetual in one manner. Third: force is that which is contrary to nature, and since it is established that celestial motions are impossible to be natural, it appears that they are also impossible to be forced. So it appears from what we have mentioned: that celestial motions are either natural, forced, or voluntary, and it is impossible [that they be natural or forced, so they must be voluntary].
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical proof (al-ḥujjat al-ūlā) that the celestial spheres (al-afāk) and planets are living, rational beings. The author begins by stating that there are multiple indicators, some demonstrative and some persuasive, and that one should strive to know these lofty matters by any valid means. The proof itself is introduced as the ancient philosophical argument. It proceeds by establishing that the celestial bodies are observed or demonstrated to move in a circular motion (mutaḥarrika bil-istidāra). Every moving object's motion must fall into one of three categories: natural (ṭabī‘iyya), forced (qasriyya), or voluntary (irādiyya). The author then asserts that the first two are false for celestial motion, so the third is necessarily true, which entails that the celestial spheres are alive. The passage then provides a detailed justification for the exhaustive division (al-ḥaṣr) of motion into these three types, drawing on principles from the chapter on properties of the necessary and the possible. It explains that any body producing a specific effect, not by force or accident, must have a power (quwwa) appropriate to that effect. That power either has awareness of its effect (voluntary animal power) or lacks awareness (pure natural power). If the effect is by force, it is forced. Thus every specific effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary, confirming the division. Next, the author argues that celestial motion cannot be natural. The reasoning: motion removes an existing state. If that state is natural, then both its existence and removal would be natural, which is contradictory. Therefore nature only produces motion when a contrary state occurs, making the motion a flight from that state. This would imply the motion is straight (since the straight line is the shortest path), but celestial motion is circular, so it cannot be natural. Additionally, a natural flight from a state cannot be identical to a natural seeking of that state, yet circular motion around a point is a seeking of that point, so it cannot be natural. Then the author gives three reasons why celestial motion cannot be forced: (1) forced motion cannot regress infinitely, so it must terminate in something not forced, but celestial motion is perpetual and circular; (2) forced motion is not perpetual or even frequent, while celestial motion is perpetual and uniform; (3) force is contrary to nature, and since natural motion is already impossible for celestial bodies, forced motion is also impossible because it would require a natural tendency to resist. The passage concludes that celestial motion must be voluntary, and thus the celestial spheres are living.

حركة قسرية

Justification of the Division: Power and Awareness
Sanusi provides a logical justification for the threefold division of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the division of motion into natural, forced, and voluntary is derived from the principle that every specific effect requires a power in the body that either has awareness or not.

Core Idea
Every specific effect must be due to a power, and that power is either aware (voluntary) or unaware (natural); if forced, it is external.

This lesson delves into the philosophical underpinnings of the threefold division of motion. The author justifies the exhaustive nature of this division by appealing to the properties of necessary and possible beings, showing that any motion not forced must originate from a power within the body, which is either conscious or unconscious.

Guided Path
1
Read the justification of the division
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the logical steps the author takes to prove that every specific effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary.
The argument is rooted in kalam principles about the necessary and possible.
2
Understand the principle
The author states that if a body produces a specific effect not by force or accident, it must have a power appropriate to that effect. This is to avoid arbitrary specification.
This principle is key: a specific effect implies a specific internal power.
3
Distinguish types of power
The power either has awareness of its effect (voluntary animal power) or lacks awareness (pure natural power). If the effect is by force, it is forced.
Awareness is what makes motion voluntary.
4
Confirm the division
The conclusion is that every effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary, confirming the exhaustive division used in the proof.

والذي يدل على أن الأفلاك والكواكب أحياء عاقلة : وجوه . بعضها برهانية ، وبعضها إقناعية وقد ذكرنا مراراً : أن هذه المطالب العالية الشريفة الرفيعة ، يجب أن يبالغ الإنسان في معرفتها بأي طريق كان ، سواء كان برهانياً أو إقناعياً . الحجة الأولى : وهي الحجة الفلسفية القديمة - قالوا : ثبت إما بالحس وإما بالبرهان : أن الأفلاك والكواكب متحركة بالاستدارة ، وكل متحرك فحركته إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية . والأولان باطلان ، فتعين الثالث . وذلك يقتضي كون الأفلاك أحياء . أما بيان الحصر : فهو أنه قد ثبت في باب خواص الواجب والممكن : أن كل جسم يصدر عنه أثر مخصوص ، لا بالقسر ولا بالعرض، فإنه يجب أن يكون ذلك لقوة موجودة فيه ، تناسب ذلك الأثر . إذ لو لم يكن ذلك ، لكان حال ذلك الجسم بالنسبة إلى ذلك الأثر كحال سائر الأجسام بالنسبة إليه ، وكان ذلك التخصيص رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساويين على الآخر ، لا المرجح . وهو محال . فنقول : ذلك الأثر ، إذا لم يكن بالفسر ، فلا بد وأن يكون لقوة موجودة فيه . وتلك القوة إما أن يكون لها شعور بما صدر عنها ، وهي القوة الحيوانية الإرادية ، أو لا يكون لها شعور بذلك الأثر ، وهي القوة الطبيعية المحضة . وأما إذا صدر عنه ذلك الأثر بالقسر ، فهو الذي يكون بالقسر . فقد ثبت : أن كل جسم يختص بأثر ، فذلك الأثر إما أن يكون طبيعياً أو قسرياً أو إرادياً . وذلك يدل على صحة الحصر المذكور . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون حركة الفلك طبيعية ، لأن الحركة تقتضي إزالة حالة حاصلة . فتلك الحالة ، إما أن تكون طبيعية أو لا تكون ، فإن كانت طبيعية مع أنا فرضنا أن الطبيعية تقتضي إزالتها ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون وجود تلك الحالة طبيعياً وأن يكون زوالها طبيعياً ، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين . وهو محال . فثبت : أن الطبيعية لا توجب الحركة ، إلا عند حصول حالة منافرة للطبيعة . فلا جرم أن الطبيعة توجب إزالتها . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلو كانت حركة الفلك طبيعية لكانت تلك الحركة هرباً عن حالة منافرة بالطبع . وذلك محال لوجهين : الأول : إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لكانت تلك الحركة مستقيمة . لأن الخط المستقيم أقصر من المستدير . ومتى أمكن اتصال الجسم إلى الحالة الطبيعية بالطريق الأسهل ، امتنع العدول إلى الطريق الأصعب . فثبت : أن كل حركة طبيعية ، فهي مستقيمة بالطبع. فينعكس انعكاس النقيض : أن ما لا يكون مستقيماً ، لا يكون طبيعياً . الثاني : إنه على التقدير الذي ذكرناه ، تكون الحركة هرباً طبيعياً عن تلك الحالة ، والهرب الطبيعي عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون عين الطلب الطبيعي لذلك الشيء ، لكن الحركة المستديرة عن النقطة المعينة ، عين الطلب لها ، فيمتنع كون الحركة المستديرة طبيعية. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كون الحركة الفلكية قسرية لوجوه : الأول : إنه يمتنع انتهاء كل قسر إلى قسر آخر ، لغير نهاية ، بل يجب الانتهاء في هذه الحركة الدورية إلى ما لا يكون بالقسر . والثاني : إن القسر لا يكون دائماً ، ولا أكثرياً . وهذه الحركة دائمة على نهج واحد . الثالث : إن القسر هو الذي يكون على خلاف الطبيعة ولما ثبت أن حركات الأفلاك يمتنع كونها طبيعية ، ظهر أنه يمتنع أيضاً كونها قسرية . فظهر بما ذكرنا : أن حركات الأفلاك إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية ويمتنع
And that which indicates that the celestial spheres and planets are living, rational beings: [there are] aspects. Some of them are demonstrative, and some of them are persuasive. And we have mentioned repeatedly: that these lofty, noble, elevated matters, a person must strive in knowing them by whatever means possible, whether demonstrative or persuasive. The first proof: and it is the ancient philosophical proof – they said: it is established, either by sense or by demonstration, that the celestial spheres and planets are moving in a circular motion, and every moving thing, its motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And the first two are false, so the third is determined. And that necessitates that the celestial spheres are living. As for the explanation of the exhaustive division: it is that it has been established in the chapter of the properties of the necessary and the possible: that every body from which proceeds a specific effect, neither by force nor accidentally, then it must be that this is due to a power existing in it, corresponding to that effect. For if that were not the case, the state of that body with respect to that effect would be like the state of other bodies with respect to it, and that specification would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the equally possible over the other without a preponderator. And that is impossible. So we say: that effect, if it is not by force, then it must be due to a power existing in it. And that power either has awareness of what proceeds from it – and it is the animal voluntary power – or it has no awareness of that effect – and it is the pure natural power. And as for when that effect proceeds from it by force, then it is that which is by force. So it is established: that every body that is distinguished by an effect, that effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And that indicates the correctness of the aforementioned exhaustive division. And we only said: that it is impossible for the motion of the celestial sphere to be natural, because motion necessitates removing an existing state. So that state is either natural or not. If it is natural while we have supposed that the natural [force] necessitates its removal, then it necessarily follows that the existence of that state is natural and that its removal is natural, and that necessitates combining two opposites. And if that is so, then if the motion of the celestial sphere were natural, that motion would be a flight from a state contrary to nature. And whenever the body can reach the natural state by the easier path, it is impossible to turn to the more difficult path. So it is established: that every natural motion is straight by nature. Second: force is not perpetual, nor for the most part. And this motion is perpetual in one manner. Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical proof (al-ḥujjat al-ūlā) that the celestial spheres (al-afāk) and planets are living, rational beings. The author begins by stating that there are multiple indicators, some demonstrative and some persuasive, and that one should strive to know these lofty matters by any valid means. The author then asserts that the first two are false for celestial motion, so the third is necessarily true, which entails that the celestial spheres are alive. The passage then provides a detailed justification for the exhaustive division (al-ḥaṣr) of motion into these three types, drawing on principles from the chapter on properties of the necessary and the possible. Causality and Created Things

Refutation of Natural and Forced Motion for Celestial Bodies
Sanusi refutes natural and forced motion for celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments why celestial motion cannot be natural or forced, leaving only voluntary motion.

Core Idea
Celestial motion cannot be natural because it is circular (not straight) and does not flee a state; it cannot be forced because it is perpetual and uniform.

Having established the threefold division, the author now eliminates natural and forced motion for celestial bodies. This lesson examines the two arguments against natural motion and the three against forced motion, concluding that celestial motion must be voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Read the arguments against natural motion
Read the passage where the author proves that celestial motion cannot be natural. Two arguments are given: one based on straightness, and one based on the distinction between flight and seeking.
The first argument uses the idea that natural motion is always straight.
2
Understand the first argument
If motion were natural, it would be a flight from a contrary state, and the straight line is the easiest path, so the motion would be straight. Since celestial motion is circular, it cannot be natural.
The contrapositive: what is not straight is not natural.
3
Understand the second argument
Natural flight from a state cannot be identical to natural seeking of that state. Circular motion around a point is seeking that point, so it cannot be flight.
This shows circular motion is not natural flight.
4
Read the arguments against forced motion
Three reasons are given: infinite regress, non-perpetual nature of force, and contradiction with nature.
The author uses the fact that celestial motion is perpetual and uniform.
5
Conclude
Since both natural and forced motion are impossible, celestial motion must be voluntary, affirming the heavens are living.
The passage ends abruptly but the conclusion is clear.

المستديرة عن النقطة المعينة ، عين الطلب لها ، فيمتنع كون الحركة المستديرة طبيعية. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كون الحركة الفلكية قسرية لوجوه : الأول : إنه يمتنع انتهاء كل قسر إلى قسر آخر ، لغير نهاية ، بل يجب الانتهاء في هذه الحركة الدورية إلى ما لا يكون بالقسر . والثاني : إن القسر لا يكون دائماً ، ولا أكثرياً . وهذه الحركة دائمة على نهج واحد . الثالث : إن القسر هو الذي يكون على خلاف الطبيعة ولما ثبت أن حركات الأفلاك يمتنع كونها طبيعية ، ظهر أنه يمتنع أيضاً كونها قسرية . فظهر بما ذكرنا : أن حركات الأفلاك إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية ويمتنع
And that which indicates that the celestial spheres and planets are living, rational beings: [there are] aspects. Some of them are demonstrative, and some of them are persuasive. And we have mentioned repeatedly: that these lofty, noble, elevated matters, a person must strive in knowing them by whatever means possible, whether demonstrative or persuasive. The first proof: and it is the ancient philosophical proof – they said: it is established, either by sense or by demonstration, that the celestial spheres and planets are moving in a circular motion, and every moving thing, its motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And the first two are false, so the third is determined. And that necessitates that the celestial spheres are living. As for the explanation of the exhaustive division: it is that it has been established in the chapter of the properties of the necessary and the possible: that every body from which proceeds a specific effect, neither by force nor accidentally, then it must be that this is due to a power existing in it, corresponding to that effect. For if that were not the case, the state of that body with respect to that effect would be like the state of other bodies with respect to it, and that specification would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the equally possible over the other without a preponderator. And that is impossible. So we say: that effect, if it is not by force, then it must be due to a power existing in it. And that power either has awareness of what proceeds from it – and it is the animal voluntary power – or it has no awareness of that effect – and it is the pure natural power. And as for when that effect proceeds from it by force, then it is that which is by force. So it is established: that every body that is distinguished by an effect, that effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And that indicates the correctness of the aforementioned exhaustive division. And we only said: that it is impossible for the motion of the celestial sphere to be natural, because motion necessitates removing an existing state. So that state is either natural or not. If it is natural while we have supposed that the natural [force] necessitates its removal, then it necessarily follows that the existence of that state is natural and that its removal is natural, and that necessitates combining two opposites. And that is impossible. So it is established: that nature does not necessitate motion except when a state contrary to nature occurs. So necessarily nature necessitates its removal. And if that is so, then if the motion of the celestial sphere were natural, that motion would be a flight from a state contrary to nature. And that is impossible for two reasons: First: if it were so, that motion would be straight. Because the straight line is shorter than the circular. And whenever the body can reach the natural state by the easier path, it is impossible to turn to the more difficult path. So it is established: that every natural motion is straight by nature. Then the contrapositive follows: that whatever is not straight is not natural. Second: upon the estimation we mentioned, the motion would be a natural flight from that state, and natural flight from a thing is impossible to be identical to natural seeking of that thing, but circular motion around a specific point is identical to the seeking of it, so it is impossible for circular motion to be natural. And we only said: that it is impossible for celestial motion to be forced for reasons: First: it is impossible for every force to terminate in another force ad infinitum, rather termination in this circular motion must be to that which is not by force. Second: force is not perpetual, nor for the most part. And this motion is perpetual in one manner. Third: force is that which is contrary to nature, and since it is established that celestial motions are impossible to be natural, it appears that they are also impossible to be forced. So it appears from what we have mentioned: that celestial motions are either natural, forced, or voluntary, and it is impossible [that they be natural or forced, so they must be voluntary].
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical proof (al-ḥujjat al-ūlā) that the celestial spheres (al-afāk) and planets are living, rational beings. The author begins by stating that there are multiple indicators, some demonstrative and some persuasive, and that one should strive to know these lofty matters by any valid means. The proof itself is introduced as the ancient philosophical argument. It proceeds by establishing that the celestial bodies are observed or demonstrated to move in a circular motion (mutaḥarrika bil-istidāra). Every moving object's motion must fall into one of three categories: natural (ṭabī‘iyya), forced (qasriyya), or voluntary (irādiyya). The author then asserts that the first two are false for celestial motion, so the third is necessarily true, which entails that the celestial spheres are alive. The passage then provides a detailed justification for the exhaustive division (al-ḥaṣr) of motion into these three types, drawing on principles from the chapter on properties of the necessary and the possible. It explains that any body producing a specific effect, not by force or accident, must have a power (quwwa) appropriate to that effect. That power either has awareness of its effect (voluntary animal power) or lacks awareness (pure natural power). If the effect is by force, it is forced. Thus every specific effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary, confirming the division. Next, the author argues that celestial motion cannot be natural. The reasoning: motion removes an existing state. If that state is natural, then both its existence and removal would be natural, which is contradictory. Therefore nature only produces motion when a contrary state occurs, making the motion a flight from that state. This would imply the motion is straight (since the straight line is the shortest path), but celestial motion is circular, so it cannot be natural. Additionally, a natural flight from a state cannot be identical to a natural seeking of that state, yet circular motion around a point is a seeking of that point, so it cannot be natural. Then the author gives three reasons why celestial motion cannot be forced: (1) forced motion cannot regress infinitely, so it must terminate in something not forced, but celestial motion is perpetual and circular; (2) forced motion is not perpetual or even frequent, while celestial motion is perpetual and uniform; (3) force is contrary to nature, and since natural motion is already impossible for celestial bodies, forced motion is also impossible because it would require a natural tendency to resist. The passage concludes that celestial motion must be voluntary, and thus the celestial spheres are living.

Correction: The author argues that natural motion is necessarily straight, so circular motion cannot be natural.

By eliminating natural and forced motion, the only remaining possibility is voluntary motion, implying the heavens are living beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the heavens are living beings acting voluntarily, how does that change the way you look at the sky and the universe? What responsibility does that place on you as a conscious observer?
?
The argument shows that every motion must have a cause — natural, forced, or voluntary. In your own life, are your actions more often driven by natural impulses, external pressures, or genuine voluntary choice? Which do you think is most pleasing to your Creator?
?
Sanusi deduces that the celestial bodies are not mere objects but beings with will. If the stars and planets obey God willingly, what does that say about your own obedience? Are you compelled by fear or habit, or do you willingly submit to the One who set the heavens in motion?
