The Self-Evident Superiority of Life and Divine Wisdom
Razi presents the self-evident judgment that life is better and applies it to the celestial case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that denying life to celestial bodies would contradict divine wisdom and mercy.

Core Idea
Life is inherently better than lifelessness, and the greatest creation must be the best.

Having established the vastness and nobility of the celestial spheres, Razi now invokes a self-evident truth: life is superior to inertness. He then draws a devastating conclusion: if the celestial bodies were lifeless, then most of creation would be defective, which is incompatible with God's mercy and wisdom.

Guided Path
1

This is a foundational premise.
2
Connect to the celestial case
Consider what would happen if the celestial bodies lacked life. Razi says most of creation would be deficient.
The celestial spheres constitute the majority of the universe.
3
Understand the contradiction
Reflect on why this would contradict divine mercy and wisdom. God's mercy and wisdom demand that the greater portion of creation be good and noble.
This is the core of the proof.

The Comparison of Perfection and the Principle of Proportion
Razi compares the perfection of celestial and terrestrial bodies and their attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument from proportionality: that the noblest bodies deserve the noblest attributes, and denying this contradicts divine wisdom.

Core Idea
The noblest bodies must have the noblest attributes; otherwise, divine wisdom is overturned.

Razi strengthens his argument with a comparison: celestial bodies far surpass terrestrial ones in duration, purity, and clarity. Similarly, life and intellect surpass inertness and death. Therefore, to give life to base bodies while denying it to noble bodies would contradict divine mercy and wisdom. This reinforces the conclusion that celestial bodies must be alive.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison of bodies
Read where Razi compares celestial and terrestrial bodies in terms of duration, purity, and clarity.
The celestial bodies are superior in every way.
2
Read the comparison of attributes

The contrast is stark.
3
Understand the principle of proportion
Razi argues that it would be absurd to assign noble attributes to base bodies and withhold them from noble bodies. This would contradict mercy and wisdom.
This is the final step of the proof.

The Sixth Proof: Celestial Bodies Possess Life and Knowledge
Razi applies the causality principle to celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof that celestial bodies must possess life and knowledge because they cause perfections in lower bodies.

Core Idea
Cause of a perfection must possess that perfection more fully.

Razi presents the sixth proof for the attributes of celestial bodies. He argues that since the heavens regulate the perfections of the lower world, they must themselves possess those perfections. This lesson walks through the causal principle and the supporting evidence from astrology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument: first the causal principle, then its application, then an empirical illustration.
Pay attention to the phrase 'صريح العقل وأصل الفطرة' – it signals the conclusion.
2
Identify the causal principle
Find the sentence: 'كل كمال حصل في معلول عن علة، فتلك العلة بذلك الكمال أولى من ذلك المعلول'. This is the key premise. Understand it fully.
3
Examine the astrological evidence
Read the part about astrological judgments. Razi uses this as empirical support. Consider why he includes it and how it strengthens the argument.
This is not an endorsement of all astrology; it's an observation about causal influence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنا قد بينا في كتاب ( القدم والحدوث، في صفات السموات : أن بحركاتها تنتظم مصالح هذا العالم . وهي المبادىء لحدوث الصور والأعراض في هذا العالم . ثم قد ثبت في أول العقل : أن كل كمال حصل في معلول عن علة ، فتلك العلة بذلك الكمال أولى من ذلك المعلول . ولا شك أن الحياة والإدراك والعلوم كمالات ، فوجب أن تكون الأجرام الفلكية أولى بهذه الكمالات من هذه الأجسام الرخوة الخسيسة . والذي يزيل الشبهة عن هذا الباب : أن من مارس علم الأحكام، ووقف على أسرارها : علم أنه متى وقع كوكب من الكواكب المناسبة لحالة من الأحوال ، في موضع قوي مناسب من طالع مولد إنسان . فإن ذلك الإنسان يصير وحيد عصره ، وفريد دهره في تلك الحالة. فإذا كان هذا التعلق البعيد لتلك الكواكب بهذه الأجسام الرخوة ، يوجب الكمال. فالقول بأن تلك الذوات المقدمة المنيرة الطاهرة الشريفة ، أولى بهذه الكمالات مما ينادى [ بصحته ] صريح العقل ، وأصل الفطرة .
The sixth proof: We have already explained in the book 'Precedence and Origination' concerning the attributes of the heavens that through their movements the interests of this world are regulated, and they are the principles for the occurrence of forms and accidents in this world. Then it has been established at the beginning of reason that every perfection that occurs in an effect from a cause, that cause is more deserving of that perfection than the effect. And there is no doubt that life, perception, and sciences are perfections. So it is necessary that the celestial bodies are more deserving of these perfections than these soft, base bodies. And what removes doubt about this matter is that whoever practices the science of astrological judgments and understands its secrets knows that whenever a planet that is appropriate for a certain condition falls in a strong, suitable position from the ascendant of a human being's birth, then that human becomes unique in his era and peerless in his time in that condition. So if this remote connection of those planets to these soft bodies causes perfection, then the statement that those advanced, luminous, pure, noble beings are more deserving of these perfections is proclaimed by the explicit reason and the original disposition (fitra).
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the life and knowledge of celestial bodies. It first asserts that the movements of the heavens are responsible for the order and generation of forms and accidents in the lower world. Then it invokes a causal principle: the cause of a perfection must possess that perfection to a higher degree. Since life, perception, and knowledge are perfections generated in lower bodies through celestial influences, the celestial bodies themselves must possess these perfections more fully. To remove doubt, Razi appeals to astrology: astrologers observe that planetary positions at birth can cause extraordinary abilities in humans, showing that even a distant influence produces perfection; how much more must the celestial bodies themselves be perfect. Therefore, reason and innate disposition affirm that celestial bodies are more deserving of these perfections.

Key Terms
كمال
معلول
علة
أجرام فلكية
حياة
إدراك
علوم
فطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi fully endorses all astrological practices as valid science.
Correction: Razi uses astrological observations only as empirical support for the causal principle, not as a blanket endorsement of astrology. The argument remains rational: if a remote influence can cause perfection, the source must be even more perfect.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, why must celestial bodies possess life and knowledge?
A) Because they are made of a special substance
B) Because they cause perfections in lower bodies and the cause must be more perfect
C) Because the Prophet said so
D) Because they are large

Key Takeaway
The heavens must possess life and knowledge because they cause these perfections in the lower world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that celestial bodies are more deserving of life and perception than you are. Do you truly believe that the universe is teeming with beings superior to you in knowledge and awareness? Or does your ego resist this? This week, whenever you look at the night sky, pause and consciously acknowledge their superiority as part of God's creation.
?
Razi uses astrology as evidence that even a distant planetary influence can cause perfection in a human. When you consider the stars, do you feel a sense of awe and humility before their power, or do you reduce them to mere objects? Commit to one act of humility today: recall that you are an effect of a grand cosmos, not its master.
?
The principle states that the cause of a perfection must possess it more fully. If you cause goodness in others, does your heart assume that you are more deserving of that goodness? Are you humble enough to recognize that all perfection comes from God, yet you are merely a conduit? This lesson challenges you to see yourself as an effect, not the cause. Can you accept that and thank the true Cause?

Sixth Argument: Heavens are Living by Causal Perfection
An argument that heavens are living based on cause-effect perfection and astrological evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument for the heavens being living, which uses the principle of causal perfection and astrological evidence to show that celestial bodies possess life, perception, and knowledge more fully than earthly beings.

Core Idea
Every perfection in an effect is more fully present in its cause.

This passage presents the sixth argument for the living nature of the heavens. It begins by noting that the heavens organize worldly interests and are principles for forms. Then it invokes a crucial causal principle: any perfection in an effect must be more fully in its cause. Since life and knowledge are perfections found in lowly bodies, the heavenly bodies—being their principles—must possess them even more. The argument is reinforced by astrological observations showing planetary influence on human excellence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a sense of the overall argument and its flow.
Notice the structure: premise, principle, application, evidence, conclusion.
2
Identify the causal perfection principle
The passage states: 'every perfection that occurs in an effect from a cause, that cause is more deserving of that perfection than the effect.' This is the core logical move. Understand it well.
This principle is not limited to God; it applies to all cause-effect relationships.
3
Apply the principle to life and knowledge
The passage argues that life, perception, and knowledge are perfections. Since the heavens are causes of these perfections in the world, they must possess them in a superior degree.
The 'lowly base bodies' are the effects, so the heavens as causes are more deserving.
4
Examine the astrological evidence
The passage cites astrological experience: planetary positions at birth can make a person uniquely excellent. This shows the remote influence of planets, implying they have perfection themselves.
This evidence strengthens the logical argument by showing observable effects.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The argument concludes that reason and innate disposition affirm that the celestial bodies are more deserving of perfections like life and knowledge.
Reflect on how the two parts—causal principle and empirical evidence—support each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السموات : أن بحركاتها تنتظم مصالح هذا العالم . وهي المبادىء لحدوث الصور والأعراض في هذا العالم . ثم قد ثبت في أول العقل : أن كل كمال حصل في معلول عن علة ، فتلك العلة بذلك الكمال أولى من ذلك المعلول . ولا شك أن الحياة والإدراك والعلوم كمالات ، فوجب أن تكون الأجرام الفلكية أولى بهذه الكمالات من هذه الأجسام الرخوة الخسيسة . والذي يزيل الشبهة عن هذا الباب : أن من مارس علم الأحكام، ووقف على أسرارها : علم أنه متى وقع كوكب من الكواكب المناسبة لحالة من الأحوال ، في موضع قوي مناسب من طالع مولد إنسان . فإن ذلك الإنسان يصير وحيد عصره ، وفريد دهره في تلك الحالة. فإذا كان هذا التعلق البعيد لتلك الكواكب بهذه الأجرام الرخوة ، يوجب الكمال. فالقول بأن تلك الذوات المقدمة المنيرة الطاهرة الشريفة ، أولى بهذه الكمالات مما ينادى بصحته صريح العقل ، وأصل الفطرة .
The heavens: that by their movements the interests of this world are organized. And they are the principles for the occurrence of forms and accidents in this world. Then it has been established in the first of reason: that every perfection that occurs in an effect from a cause, that cause is more deserving of that perfection than the effect. And there is no doubt that life, perception, and knowledge are perfections, so it is necessary that the celestial bodies are more deserving of these perfections than these lowly base bodies. And what removes the doubt about this chapter: that whoever practices the science of [astrological] judgments and becomes acquainted with its secrets, knows that when a planet from the planets suitable for a certain condition occurs in a strong suitable position from the ascendant of a person's birth, then that person becomes unique of his time and peerless of his age in that condition. So if this remote connection of those planets to these lowly bodies necessitates perfection, then the statement that those elevated, luminous, pure, noble entities are more deserving of these perfections is that which calls for its correctness, clear reason and original disposition.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the sixth argument) for the heavens being living and rational. It begins by stating that the heavens organize the world's interests through their motions and are principles for forms and accidents. Then it invokes a principle of causal perfection: any perfection found in an effect must exist more fully in its cause. Since life, perception, and knowledge are perfections found in worldly things, the heavens as causes must possess them more. The argument then strengthens this with astrological evidence: the alignment of planets at birth can cause a person to become uniquely excellent. This remote influence on lowly bodies shows that the planets themselves have a kind of perfection, and the celestial bodies—being luminous and pure—are even more deserving of life and knowledge. The conclusion is that reason and innate disposition affirm this.

Key Terms
الأجرام الفلكية
كمالات
علة ومعلول
علم الأحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The causal perfection principle only applies to God, not to creatures.
Correction: The principle is general: any cause possesses perfections of its effect more fully. It applies to all cause-effect relationships, including celestial bodies as causes.

Checkpoint
What is the main principle used in the sixth argument to prove that the heavens are living?
A) The heavens are luminous and pure.
B) Every perfection in an effect must be more fully present in its cause.
C) Astrology proves planetary influence.
D) The heavens move uniformly.

Key Takeaway
The heavens must be living because they are causes of perfections in the world, and a cause possesses perfections more fully than its effect, with astrological evidence reinforcing this conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky, do you ever imagine that those brilliant points of light are alive—more alive than you? What would it mean for you to exist as a being dependent on such living causes?
?
The principle says every perfection in an effect is more fully present in its cause. Consider the best qualities you have—intelligence, creativity, love. If you are the effect, what cause do you acknowledge behind them? How does that cause deserve your gratitude and submission?
?
The author calls earthly bodies 'lowly and fragile' (أجسام رخوة خسيسة). Do you live as if your body is a humble vessel receiving perfection from above, or do you act as if you are the source of your own worth? What would change if you truly believed your perfections are borrowed from a higher source?

The Two Objections to the Heavens' Life
Presentation of two objections from deniers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments used by those who deny that the heavens are living and rational.

Core Idea
Deniers argue from uniformity and extreme heat.

Before we can examine the answers, we must first understand the objections. The deniers present two distinct arguments: one based on the unchanging pattern of celestial motion, and another based on the sun's intense heat. These objections challenge the idea that the heavens possess life and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the Arabic passage containing both objections. Notice how each objection is introduced and the reasoning behind it.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection claims that the heavens move uniformly without change, which contradicts voluntary motion. Identify the key terms: 'نهج واحد' (uniform manner) and 'المتحرك بالإرادة' (mover by will).
Uniformity is seen as incompatible with free choice.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection focuses on the sun's extreme heat, arguing that such heat prevents life. Note the analogy: fire's heat prevents life, so the sun's greater heat is even more preventive.
This is an argument from analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المانعون من كون الأفلاك أحياء عاقلة بوجهين :

الأول : إن هذه الأفلاك والكواكب تتحرك على نهج واحد ، لا يتغير البتة . والمتحرك بالإرادة لا يكون كذلك .

الثاني : إن جرم الشمس في غاية السخونة ، ولما كانت السخونة النارية مانعة من الحياة ، فالسخونة العظيمة التي في جرم الشمس أولى أن تكون مانعة من الحياة .

والجواب عن الحجة الأولى من وجوه :

الأول : إنه حق أن كل ما كان طبيعياً ، فإنه يبقى على نهج واحد ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن كل ما بقي على نهج واحد ، فإنه يكون طبيعياً . لما ثبت : أن الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كنفها .

والثاني : إن محرك هذه الأفلاك هو الله تعالى عند الكل ، فهي حركات صادرة عن الفاعل المختار ، مع أنها باقية على نهج واحد ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، مع أنها صادرة عن إرادة ذلك الفاعل ؟

والثالث : إن المريد إذا فعل فعلاً مخصوصاً ، فإنما فعل ذلك الفعل لأجل حصول تلك الإرادة فلو قدرنا بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها ، لوجب بقاء
And the deniers of the heavens being living and rational argued with two points:

First: These heavens and planets move in a uniform manner, never changing. And that which moves by will is not like that.

Second: The body of the sun is extremely hot, and since heat from fire prevents life, the great heat in the sun's body is more likely to prevent life.

And the answer to the first argument is from several aspects:

First: It is true that everything that is natural remains in a uniform manner, but it does not necessarily follow that everything that remains in a uniform manner is natural. Because it has been established that a universal affirmative proposition does not convert as it is.

Second: The mover of these heavens is God Almighty according to everyone. So they are movements emanating from the free agent, while they remain in a uniform manner. So why is it not possible that the matter is thus, while they emanate from the will of that agent?

Third: The one who wills, when he performs a specific action, performs that action only because of the occurrence of that will. So if we suppose the continuation of that will in its state, the continuation of
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents objections from those who deny that the heavens are living and rational, along with the author's response to the first objection. The deniers give two arguments: (1) the heavens move uniformly and never change, unlike voluntary motion which is variable; (2) the sun's intense heat prevents life. The answer focuses on the first objection with three responses. The first response uses logic: not everything uniform is natural, because a universal affirmative statement ('all natural things are uniform') does not convert to 'all uniform things are natural'. The second response argues that since God is the mover of the heavens according to all, His voluntary action can produce uniform motion—there is no contradiction. The third response (incomplete) suggests that if the will persists, the action persists, implying uniform motion can continue as long as God wills it. The second objection is not addressed here.

Key Terms
المانعون
نهج واحد
المتحرك بالإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uniformity automatically means motion is not voluntary.
Correction: Uniformity can also result from consistent will, as the answers will show.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first objection?
A) The heavens change their motion frequently.
B) The heavens move uniformly and never change, unlike voluntary motion.
C) The sun's heat prevents life.
D) The planets move randomly.

Key Takeaway
The deniers give two reasons for denying life to the heavens: uniform motion and extreme heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something's uniformity or predictability means it lacks will or purpose? Where in your life do you treat the consistent as lifeless? What would change if you saw the consistent as a sign of deliberate, constant will?
?
The deniers use the sun's heat to deny life. What 'heat' in your own life — a difficulty, a harshness, a challenge — have you used as an excuse to deny that something has a soul or purpose? How might you be wrong?
?
If you were to defend the heavens' life, how would you answer the second objection? Have you ever been too quick to accept a surface-level argument without seeking a deeper reply? What would it take for you to dig deeper next time?

First Answer: Logical Refutation
First logical answer to uniformity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that not everything uniform is natural, so voluntary motion can be uniform.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative propositions do not convert simply.

The first answer tackles the assumption that uniform motion implies natural necessity. The author uses a logical principle: while all natural things are uniform, the reverse is not necessarily true. This opens the door for voluntary uniform motion, as in the case of God's consistent will.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Read the Arabic passage containing the first answer. Note the logical terminology: 'الموجبة الكلية' (universal affirmative) and its conversion.
2
Understand the logical principle
The answer states that 'everything natural is uniform' does not imply 'everything uniform is natural'. This is because a universal affirmative proposition (All A are B) does not convert to (All B are A).
This is a basic principle of Aristotelian logic.
3
Apply to the objection
Thus, the deniers cannot conclude that uniform motion must be natural; it could be voluntary and still uniform.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second and Third Answers: Divine Agency and Persisting Will
Two theological answers: divine voluntary causation and persistence of will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments: that God's voluntary action can produce uniform motion, and that a persisting will leads to persisting action.

Core Idea
God's constant will explains the heavens' uniform motion.

The author offers two more answers to the uniformity objection. The second invokes God as the mover: since God is a free agent, His will can produce consistent motion. The third (incomplete) argues that if the will remains, the action continues, so uniform motion can result from a steady will.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the second answer which appeals to divine agency. Note that God is accepted as the mover by all parties.
2
Understand the third answer
Read the third answer (as much as is present). It argues that if will persists, the action persists, explaining uniform motion.
The passage is cut off at the end.
3
Reflect on the combined force
Both answers show that uniformity is compatible with voluntary action when the agent's will is constant.

The Objection from Uniformity and the Logical Answer
Objection from uniformity and first answer using logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that uniform motion implies nature is fallacious, based on the logical principle that a universal affirmative does not convert simply.

Core Idea
Uniformity does not entail natural necessity, because 'all natural is uniform' does not mean 'all uniform is natural'.

The opponents of the view that the celestial spheres are living and rational present an objection: the spheres move uniformly, while voluntary motion is not uniform. This suggests the spheres move by nature. In response, the first answer uses a logical principle: while everything natural is uniform, the reverse is not necessarily true. This lesson unpacks the objection and the first answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text of the objection carefully. Note the claim: the spheres move uniformly, voluntary motion is not like that, so the spheres are not voluntary.
The opponents are called 'al-māni'ūn'.
2
Examine the first answer
Read the first answer. It uses the logical contrapositive or rather the failure of conversion. Understand the principle: 'All A are B' does not imply 'All B are A'.
The term 'موجبة كلية' is a universal affirmative.
3
Apply the principle
Apply it: All natural motions are uniform, but uniform motions are not necessarily natural. So the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المانعون من كون الأفلاك أحياء عاقلة بوجهين : الأول : إن هذه الأفلاك والكواكب تتحرك على نهج واحد ، لا يتغير البتة . والمتحرك بالإرادة لا يكون كذلك . والجواب عن الحجة الأولى من وجوه : الأول : إنه حق أن كل ما كان طبيعياً ، فإنه يبقى على نهج واحد ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن كل ما بقي على نهج واحد ، فإنه يكون طبيعياً . لما ثبت : أن الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كنفها . والثاني : إن محرك هذه الأفلاك هو الله تعالى عند الكل ، فهي حركات صادرة عن الفاعل المختار ، مع أنها باقية على نهج واحد ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، مع أنها صادرة عن إرادة ذلك الفاعل ؟ والثالث : إن المريد إذا فعل فعلاً مخصوصاً ، فإنما فعل ذلك الفعل لأجل حصول تلك الإرادة فلو قدرنا بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها لوجب بقاء ذلك الفعل بحاله . ضرورة أنه لم يلزم من دوام المؤثر دوام ذلك الأثر لكن بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها أمر ممكن . وإلا لانتهت تلك الإرادة إلى وقت يمتنع بقاؤها ، فيلزم انتقالها من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو محال . فثبت أن بقاء تلك الإرادة ممكن . وثبت أنه يلزم من بقائها بقاء ذلك الأثر ، والموقوف على الممكن ممكن . فيلزم القطع بأن بقاء الفعل الاختياري على حالة واحدة ممكن من غير تغير وتبدل . وهو المطلوب . والوجه الرابع في الجواب : ما ذكره بطليموس في كتاب الثمرة فقال : إن المختار إذا طلب الأفضل لم يبق بينه وبين الطبيعة فرق . وتفسيره : إن الأفضل في كل شيء لا يكون إلا واحداً . فإذا كان الفاعل المختار حكيماً وطلب الأفضل لم يجد إلا ذلك الواحد فلا جرم وجب بقاؤه على ذلك الفعل . وذلك يمنع من وقع التغير في فعل الفاعل الحكيم .
And the opponents of the view that the celestial spheres are living and rational adduced two arguments: The first: These spheres and planets move in a single uniform manner, never changing at all. That which moves by will is not like that. The answer to the first argument has several aspects: The first: It is true that whatever is natural remains in a single uniform manner, but it does not follow from that that everything which remains in a single uniform manner is natural. Because it is established that a universal affirmative proposition does not convert simply. The second: The mover of these spheres is God Most High according to everyone, so these motions are acts proceeding from the free agent, despite being uniform; so why is it not permissible that the matter be so, even though they proceed from the will of that Agent? The third: When a willing agent performs a specific act, he only performs that act because of the occurrence of that will. So if we suppose that will to remain as it is, then the persistence of that act in its state becomes necessary. It is not necessary that persistence of the cause entail persistence of the effect, but the persistence of that will as it is is possible. Otherwise that will would end at a time after which its persistence is impossible, leading to its transition from intrinsic possibility to intrinsic impossibility, which is absurd. So it is established that the persistence of that will is possible. And it is established that its persistence necessitates the persistence of that effect, and what is dependent upon the possible is possible. Thus it is necessarily concluded that the persistence of a voluntary act in a single state is possible without change or alteration. That is what was intended. The fourth aspect of the answer: What Ptolemy mentioned in the book 'al-Thamara' (The Fruit), where he said: When the free agent seeks the best, there remains no difference between him and nature. Its explanation: The best in everything is only one. So when the free agent is wise and seeks the best, he finds only that one; hence he necessarily remains upon that act. And that prevents the occurrence of change in the act of the wise agent.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from those who deny that the celestial spheres are living and rational (المانعون), followed by four detailed answers. The objection is: The spheres and planets move uniformly and without change, but voluntary motion (المتحرك بالإرادة) is not like that — i.e., voluntary agents produce varied motions, not uniform ones. The answer begins with four aspects (وجوه). The first answer uses logic: it is true that everything natural is uniform, but the converse (that everything uniform is natural) does not follow, because a universal affirmative proposition ('All A are B') does not simply convert to 'All B are A'. So uniformity alone does not prove natural necessity. The second answer points out that according to everyone (عند الكل), the mover of the spheres is God, who is a free agent (الفاعل المختار). So even though the motions are uniform, they proceed from His will; there is no contradiction. The third answer delves into the nature of will: When a willing agent performs an act, it is because of that will. If that will persists unchanged, the act must persist. The persistence of the will is possible; otherwise it would become impossible at some point, which is absurd. Therefore the persistence of the voluntary act in one state is possible. The fourth answer, attributed to Ptolemy, argues that a wise free agent always chooses the best (الأفضل), and the best in each case is only one. So he necessarily remains upon that single act, and change would not occur. Thus uniformity does not negate volition.

Key Terms
المانعون
المتحرك بالإرادة
الموجبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something moves uniformly, it must be caused by nature, not by will.
Correction: Uniformity does not prove natural causation; it could also result from a constant will. The logical form 'All A are B' does not imply 'All B are A'.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does the first answer use to refute the objection?
A) The contrapositive of a universal affirmative.
B) The universal affirmative does not convert simply.
C) The uniformity of nature.
D) The necessity of the best.

Key Takeaway
Uniformity of motion does not imply natural causation; logical conversion is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone acting consistently, do you assume they are merely following a routine without free will? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the possibility that their will might be constant and deliberate? What small step can you take today to recognize the agency behind consistency?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because your own reasoning seemed airtight, yet you later discovered a logical flaw like the one in this passage? What personal belief might you be clinging to because of an invalid inference? Are you willing to reexamine it today?
?
If even the celestial spheres could be moved by will, then your own actions—no matter how patterned—are not determined by nature alone. Do you live as if your choices truly matter, or do you hide behind 'that's just how I am'? What one area of your life requires you to claim your voluntary motion before God?

The Free Agent and Persistence of Will
Second and third answers: God as free agent and the possibility of persistent will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fact that God is the free mover of the spheres, combined with the possibility of a persistent will, explains the uniformity of celestial motion.

Core Idea
Uniform motion can proceed from a constant will; persistence of will implies persistence of action.

The second and third answers to the objection build on the nature of the agent. The second answer notes that according to all, God moves the spheres, and He is a free agent, so uniformity does not negate will. The third answer elaborates: if a will persists unchanged, the act must persist. Since persistent will is possible, uniform voluntary action is possible. This lesson explores these two answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the Arabic for the second answer. Note that it says the mover is God, and He is a free agent, so there is no contradiction.
The argument is that even if the motion is uniform, it can still be voluntary if the agent's will is constant.
2
Read the third answer
Read the third answer carefully. It is the longest. Understand the logic: the act follows the will; if will persists, act persists. The will can persist because otherwise it would become impossible, which is absurd.
Key terms: 'إرادة' (will), 'إمكان ذاتي' (intrinsic possibility), 'امتناع ذاتي' (intrinsic impossibility).
3
Synthesize
Combine both: the uniformity of celestial motion is not a problem because it can be explained by a constant divine will, which is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المانعون من كون الأفلاك أحياء عاقلة بوجهين : الأول : إن هذه الأفلاك والكواكب تتحرك على نهج واحد ، لا يتغير البتة . والمتحرك بالإرادة لا يكون كذلك . والجواب عن الحجة الأولى من وجوه : الأول : إنه حق أن كل ما كان طبيعياً ، فإنه يبقى على نهج واحد ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن كل ما بقي على نهج واحد ، فإنه يكون طبيعياً . لما ثبت : أن الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كنفها . والثاني : إن محرك هذه الأفلاك هو الله تعالى عند الكل ، فهي حركات صادرة عن الفاعل المختار ، مع أنها باقية على نهج واحد ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، مع أنها صادرة عن إرادة ذلك الفاعل ؟ والثالث : إن المريد إذا فعل فعلاً مخصوصاً ، فإنما فعل ذلك الفعل لأجل حصول تلك الإرادة فلو قدرنا بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها لوجب بقاء ذلك الفعل بحاله . ضرورة أنه لم يلزم من دوام المؤثر دوام ذلك الأثر لكن بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها أمر ممكن . وإلا لانتهت تلك الإرادة إلى وقت يمتنع بقاؤها ، فيلزم انتقالها من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو محال . فثبت أن بقاء تلك الإرادة ممكن . وثبت أنه يلزم من بقائها بقاء ذلك الأثر ، والموقوف على الممكن ممكن . فيلزم القطع بأن بقاء الفعل الاختياري على حالة واحدة ممكن من غير تغير وتبدل . وهو المطلوب . والوجه الرابع في الجواب : ما ذكره بطليموس في كتاب الثمرة فقال : إن المختار إذا طلب الأفضل لم يبق بينه وبين الطبيعة فرق . وتفسيره : إن الأفضل في كل شيء لا يكون إلا واحداً . فإذا كان الفاعل المختار حكيماً وطلب الأفضل لم يجد إلا ذلك الواحد فلا جرم وجب بقاؤه على ذلك الفعل . وذلك يمنع من وقع التغير في فعل الفاعل الحكيم .

Ptolemy's Argument: The Wise Agent Always Chooses the Best
Fourth answer: Ptolemy's argument that a wise agent always chooses the best, which is one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ptolemy's principle—that a wise agent always chooses the best, and the best is one—explains the uniformity of celestial motion without denying free will.

Core Idea
A wise free agent always chooses the best, and the best is singular, so action remains uniform.

The fourth answer to the objection is attributed to Ptolemy. He argues that a wise free agent always seeks the best. Since the best in any given situation is only one, the agent will consistently choose that one act. Therefore, the uniformity of celestial motion is consistent with voluntary choice—it is the result of always choosing the best. This lesson explores Ptolemy's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth answer
Read the Arabic text. Note that it says the free agent, when seeking the best, finds only one option, so he remains upon it.
The term 'الأفضل' means 'the best'.
2
Understand the reasoning
Explain: If the agent is wise and always chooses the best, and the best is unique, then his action will be constant. This does not contradict volition; it is the perfection of will.
This argument turns the objection on its head: uniformity is actually a sign of wisdom.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answers the objection: the opponents thought uniformity implies nature, but Ptolemy shows it can imply perfect will.

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Second Objection and Two Answers on Solar Heat and Life
Second objection: sun's heat precludes life; two answers denying heat attribution or its incompatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two answers given to the objection that the sun's heat precludes life, and the analogies used to support them.

Core Idea
The sun can cause heat without being hot, and heat does not necessarily prevent life.

This passage addresses a second objection from opponents who claim that the sun's intense heat makes life impossible. The author offers two distinct answers: first, denying that celestial bodies are inherently hot or cold, and second, even if they were, heat does not preclude life. We will explore the logical and empirical arguments used.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two answers
Notice the structure: first answer uses analogies (motion, anger), second answer concedes and uses counterexamples (salamander, ostrich).
The two answers are introduced by 'والوجه الثاني في الجواب'.
3
Reflect on the dialectical style
Consider why the author adds a skeptical note about the examples, showing the level of argumentation.
This reveals the reasoned debate characteristic of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : قد بينا في الطبيعيات : أن أجرام الأفلاك والكواكب غير موصوفة بالحرارة والبرودة . ولا يلزم من خلوها في ذاتها عن الحرارة والبرودة أن لا تكون في التسخين والتبريد مؤثرة . ألا ترى أن الحركة توجب السخونة مع أن اتصافها بالسخونة محال . والغضب الشديد يوجب السخونة مع أن اتصاف الصفة المسماة بالغضب بالسخونة محال . والوجه الثاني في الجواب : أن نقول هب أنها موصوفة بالسخونة الشديدة . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك يمنع قبول الحياة ؟ فإنا نشاهد أن السمندر يتكون في النار ، والنعامة تبتلع الحديدة المحماة . وأمثال هذه الدلائل لولا أنه ذكرها بعض الناس وإلا لكان يجب على العاقل أن لا يلتفت إليها .
And the answer to the second argument: We say: We have explained in the natural sciences that the bodies of the spheres and planets are not described as hot or cold. And their being devoid in themselves of heat and cold does not entail that they are not effective in heating and cooling. Do you not see that motion produces heat, even though being described as heat is impossible for motion? And intense anger produces heat, even though being described as heat is impossible for the attribute called anger. The second aspect of the answer: We say: Suppose they are described as intensely hot. Why do you say that prevents the reception of life? For we observe that the salamander is generated in fire, and the ostrich swallows heated iron. And were it not that some people have mentioned such examples, it would be incumbent upon a rational person not to pay attention to them.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the opponents: the sun's body is extremely hot, and intense heat prevents life. The answer is given in two aspects. The first answer denies that the celestial bodies are inherently hot or cold; they are not described with such qualities in themselves. However, this does not mean they are not effective in heating or cooling. Analogies: motion causes heat but motion itself is not hot; anger causes heat but anger is not hot. So the sun can cause heat without being hot itself. The second answer concedes for argument's sake that the sun is intensely hot. It then asks why that precludes receiving life, citing empirical examples: the salamander is generated in fire, and the ostrich swallows hot iron. The author adds a skeptical note: were it not that some people mention these examples, a rational person would not pay attention to them — implying these examples are weak but still used to counter the objection.

Key Terms
أجرام الأفلاك والكواكب
الحرارة
البرودة
التسخين
التبريد
الغضب
السمندر
النعامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sun is cold because it is not described as hot.
Correction: The text says celestial bodies are not described as hot or cold, but they can still cause heat. The argument is about causal efficacy, not temperature.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the first answer use to show that something can cause heat without being hot?
A) The sun and the moon.
B) Motion and anger.
C) Fire and water.
D) The salamander and the ostrich.

Key Takeaway
Two answers to the heat objection: celestial bodies are not inherently hot but can cause heat, and even if hot, heat does not automatically preclude life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily reliance on causes and effects, how often do you assume that the cause must itself possess the quality of the effect? How might this assumption limit your understanding of God's power, who creates effects directly without need of intermediaries?
?
The author uses the salamander and ostrich as counterexamples, but adds a skeptical note. When you defend a belief, do you rely on weak evidence because it supports your position, or do you honestly evaluate it? What belief do you need to reconsider today?
?
If heat does not necessarily prevent life, what other 'impossibilities' have you accepted in your spiritual journey that God's power can overturn? Will you ask Him to expand your vision of what is possible?

Proof that a Voluntary Act Can Remain Unchanged
Proof that permanence of voluntary act is possible without change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that a voluntary act can persist in the same state without change, using essential possibility and the persistence of the will.

Core Idea
A voluntary act can persist unchanged because its cause (the will) can persist without contradiction.

This lesson unpacks a subtle argument from Islamic philosophical theology. The author, al-Matālib al-Āliyah, addresses a common doubt: if a cause remains, must its effect change? He argues that the effect can also remain, provided the cause can remain. By analyzing the nature of possibility and impossibility, he establishes that a voluntarily-produced state can persist indefinitely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, conditional, contradiction, conclusion.
This is a dense philosophical proof, so read slowly.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that the permanence of the cause does not force the permanence of the effect. But the will remaining as it is, is possible.
The phrase 'بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها أمر ممكن' is central.
3
Follow the proof by contradiction
The author argues: if the will could not remain, it would have to end at a time becoming impossible, which would be a transition from essential possibility to impossibility — an absurdity.
This shows that the will's remaining is not only possible but necessary to avoid contradiction.
4
Trace the conclusion
Since the will can persist, and the act depends on the will, the act can also persist. Therefore, a voluntary act can stay the same without change.
This refutes the idea that voluntary acts must constantly change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الفعل بحاله . ضرورة أنه لم يلزم من دوام المؤثر ، دوام ذلك الأثر ، لكن بقاء تلك الإرادة بحالها ، أمر ممكن. وإلا لانتهت تلك الإرادة إلى وقت ، يمتنع بقاؤها . فيلزم انتقالها من الإمكان الذاتي، إلى الامتناع الذاتي . وهو محال . فثبت : أن بقاء تلك الإرادة ممكن. وثبت : أنه يلزم من بقائها بقاء ذلك الأثر ، والموقوف على الممكن . ممكن . فيلزم القطع بأن بقاء الفعل الاختياري على حالة واحدة : ممكن من غير تغير وتبدل . وهو المطلوب
That action in its state. Necessarily, from the permanence of the efficient cause, the permanence of that effect does not follow, but the remaining of that will in its state is a possible matter. Otherwise, that will would end at a time such that its remaining is impossible, leading to its transfer from essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is absurd. So it is established that the remaining of that will is possible. And it is established that from its remaining follows the remaining of that effect, and that which is dependent upon the possible is possible. Hence it necessarily follows that the remaining of a voluntary act in one state is possible without change or alteration. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a voluntary act can remain in the same state without change. The argument proceeds: The permanence of the cause does not necessitate the permanence of the effect. However, the will remaining as it is, is possible. If it were not possible, the will would have to cease at some point, which would mean it transitions from possible to impossible — an absurdity. Therefore, the will can persist. And if the will persists, the effect (the act) also persists, since the act depends on the will. So the act can remain unchanged. Thus the conclusion is that a voluntary act can stay the same without alteration.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المؤثر
الأثر
الإمكان الذاتي
الامتناع الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a cause remains, the effect must change constantly.
Correction: The author shows that the effect can also remain if the cause remains unchanged, because the effect depends on the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would happen if the will could not remain in its state?
A) It would become necessary.
B) It would end at a time when its remaining is impossible.
C) It would change into a different will.
D) It would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The permanence of a voluntary act is rationally possible without change, as demonstrated by the possibility of the will persisting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that a will can remain constant without change. Examine one area of your life where your resolve has weakened. Are you excusing yourself by believing change is inevitable? What would it mean to you to know that your intention can persist unchanged? What step will you take today to make one righteous intention permanent?
?
If the permanence of a voluntary act is possible, why do you find it so hard to maintain consistency in your worship or character? Is it because you doubt the power of your own will, or because you have not truly settled it? How can this proof strengthen your resolve to be steadfast?
?
The proof refutes the idea that a stable cause must produce a changing effect. Are there areas in your life where you seek change for its own sake, ignoring the value of constancy? What good habit have you abandoned because you thought it couldn't last? What would it look like to revive it and keep it unchanged?

Ptolemy's Reply: The Best is Unique
Ptolemy's argument that a wise agent seeking the best cannot change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ptolemy's logical argument that a wise agent who always seeks the best must act uniformly and cannot change.

Core Idea
If the best is unique, a wise agent always chooses the same.

Ptolemy offers a fourth reply in the discussion about divine action and change. He argues that a free agent who seeks the best is indistinguishable from a natural agent because the best is only one. This concise passage lays out a logical chain that challenges common intuitions about freedom and divine choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analysis. Let the argument's flow become familiar before you interpret it.
Notice the structure: a statement followed by an interpretation.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify Ptolemy's main claim: when the free agent seeks the best, there is no difference between him and a natural agent.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Trace the logical steps: the best is unique → a wise agent finds only that best → he remains upon that act. Understand why change is impossible.
The uniqueness of the best is the key premise.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider what this argument means for divine freedom. If God always chooses the best, does that limit His ability to act differently?
This connects to broader theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الرابع في الجواب : ما ذكره ( بطليموس ، في كتاب ( الثمرة ) فقال : « إن المختار إذا طلب الأفضل ، لم يبق بينه وبين الطبيعة فرق ، وتفسيره : إن الأفضل [ في كل شيء لا يكون إلا واحداً . فإذا كان الفاعل المختار حكيماً وطلب الأفضل (۱) ] لم يجد إلا ذلك الواحد فلا جرم وجب بقاؤه على ذلك الفعل . وذلك يمنع من وقع التغير في فعل الفاعل الحكيم .
And the fourth face of the answer: what Ptolemy mentioned in the book 'al-Thamara'. He said: 'Indeed, when the free agent seeks the best, there remains no difference between him and nature. Its interpretation: The best in everything is only one. So if the agent is wise and seeks the best, he finds only that one. Hence necessarily he remains upon that act. And that prevents the occurrence of change in the act of the wise agent.'
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents Ptolemy's argument as a fourth reply. Ptolemy argues that if a free agent (mukhtār) seeks the best (al-afḍal), then he is no different from a natural agent, because the best is unique. A wise agent will always choose the same best act, so his act remains constant. Thus, change is prevented in the act of a wise agent.

Key Terms
المختار
الأفضل
الفاعل الحكيم
طبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A free agent can always choose differently regardless of what is best.
Correction: Ptolemy argues that if the agent is wise and seeks the best, and the best is unique, then the agent must choose that best and cannot change.

Checkpoint

A) Because he is forced by nature.
B) Because the best is only one and he always chooses it.
C) Because he is not free.
D) Because he has no will.

Key Takeaway
Ptolemy argues that a wise agent who seeks the best must act uniformly because the best is singular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ptolemy argues that a wise agent, when seeking the best, becomes as determined as nature itself. Does this challenge your belief that you are free to choose differently? How does your inconsistency reveal a lack of wisdom?
?

Answer to the Argument: Celestial Bodies and Heat
The author replies that celestial bodies are not inherently hot yet cause heat, citing examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that celestial bodies cannot be living due to heat, using two distinct arguments: one about causality and one about counterexamples.

Core Idea
Heat does not preclude life; even non-hot causes can produce heat.

This passage continues the discussion from the previous arguments against life in celestial bodies. The author now directly answers the second argument that celestial bodies cannot be alive because they are hot. He offers two clever rebuttals: first, that being a cause of heat does not require being hot, and second, that extreme heat does not necessarily prevent life, as seen in nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two answers
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author explicitly marks 'the second face of the answer', indicating a two-part reply.
The first answer uses analogies from motion and anger.
2
Understand the first answer: causality without inherent quality
The author argues that celestial bodies can cause heating and cooling without being hot or cold themselves. Reflect on the examples of motion and anger causing heat without being hot.
This is a key point in causation: effects do not require the cause to possess the same attribute.
3
Understand the second answer: counterexamples from nature
The author hypothetically accepts extreme heat and then asks why that would prevent life. He provides examples of animals that thrive in hot conditions: the salamander in fire and the ostrich swallowing hot iron.
These examples show that life can exist in extreme heat, so heat is not a disqualifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : قد بينا في الطبيعيات : أن أجرام الأفلاك والكواكب غير موصوفة بالحرارة والبرودة . ولا يلزم من خلوها في ذاتها عن الحرارة والبرودة . أن لا تكون في التسخين والتبريد مؤثرة . ألا ترى أن الحركة توجب السخونة ، مع أن اتصافها بالسخونة محال . والغضب الشديد يوجب السخونة ، مع أن اتصاف الصفة المسماة [ بالغضب (۳) ] بالسخونة محال . والوجه الثاني في الجواب : [ أن نقول (۳)] هب أنها موصوفة بالسخونة الشديدة . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك يمنع قبول الحياة ؟ فإنا نشاهد أن السمندر يتكون في النار ، والنعامة تبتلع الحديدة المحماة. وأمثال هذه الدلائل ، لولا أنه ذكرها بعض الناس ، وإلا لكان يجب على العاقل أن لا يلتفت إليها .
And the answer to the second argument: we say: we have already shown in physics that the bodies of the spheres and stars are not described with heat and cold. And it does not follow from their being devoid in their essence of heat and cold that they are not effective in heating and cooling. Do you not see that motion causes heat, even though being described with heat is impossible for it? And intense anger causes heat, even though being described with heat is impossible for the attribute called anger. And the second face of the answer: let us assume they are described with intense heat. Then why do you say that that prevents the acceptance of life? For we observe that the salamander is generated in fire, and the ostrich swallows a red-hot iron. And such proofs, if some people had not mentioned them, a rational person would have to pay no attention to them.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second argument, presumably that celestial bodies cannot be living because they are hot or cold. The author gives two replies. First: The celestial spheres and stars are not inherently hot or cold, yet they cause heating and cooling. Just as motion causes heat without being hot itself, and anger causes heat without being hot, so the celestial bodies can cause effects without being hot. Second: Even if we suppose they are intensely hot, why would that prevent life? Examples from nature: the salamander lives in fire, and the ostrich swallows hot iron. These examples show that extreme heat does not necessarily preclude life. The author notes that these examples are so well-known that a rational person should not ignore them.

Correction: The author shows that extreme heat does not necessarily preclude life, citing the salamander and ostrich as counterexamples.

Ibn Sina's Three Aspects of the Celestial Soul
Ibn Sina enumerates three aspects of the celestial soul and supports with analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ibn Sina ascribes three distinct aspects to each celestial sphere: an animal soul, a rational soul, and an abstract intellect.

Core Idea
Each celestial sphere has three levels: animal soul, rational soul, and abstract intellect.

Ibn Sina, known as the Chief Master, presents a sophisticated theory of celestial souls. He draws analogies between the human soul and the celestial soul to argue that each sphere possesses three distinct faculties. This lesson introduces this classification, setting the stage for deeper arguments about their nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first part of the passage where Ibn Sina identifies the celestial soul as a corporeal power pervading the sphere. Notice the analogy to the human animal soul.
2
Identify the three aspects
Read where Ibn Sina says each sphere has three things: animal soul, rational soul, and abstract intellect. Understand each term.
3
Reflect on the analogies
Consider why Ibn Sina uses analogies to the human soul. What does this imply about the celestial soul's nature?
The analogies suggest a similarity in function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كلام الشيخ الرئيس في أكثر كتبه، يدل على أن النفس الفلكية المباشرة لتحريك جسم السماء: قوة جسمانية سارية في ذلك الجسم. فإنه قال في (النجاة) وفي (الشفاء): (نسبة النفس الفلكية إلى جسم الفلك، كنسبة النفس الحيوانية التي لنا إلينا). وذكر في (الإشارات): (إن له مع ذلك نفساً ناطقة. نسبتها إلى جسم الفلك، كنسبة النفس الناطقة التي لنا إلينا). وأيضاً: أثبت أن لكل فلك عقلاً على حدة، فعلى هذا الطريق وجب أن يحصل لكل فلك أمور ثلاثة: النفس الحيوانية، والنفس الناطقة، والعقل المجرد. أما النفس الحيوانية، فقد احتج على إثباتها بأن قال: المبدأ القريب لهذه الحركة: إرادة جزئية، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يحصل لذلك المبدأ: علوم جزئية، وإذا كان كذلك، وجب أن تكون تلك القوة جسمانية. فهذه مقدمات ثلاث: المقدمة الأولى: في إثبات قوله: (المبدأ القريب لهذه الحركة: إرادة جزئية، فالدليل عليه وجهان: الأول: إن الماهية الكلية، مشترك فيها بين الجزئيات. والقصد إلى...
From the speech of the Chief Master (al-shaykh al-ra'is) in most of his books, it indicates that the celestial soul (al-nafs al-falakiyya) that directly moves the body of the heaven is a corporeal power (quwwa jismāniyya) pervading that body. For he said in (al-Najāt) and in (al-Shifā'): 'The relation of the celestial soul to the body of the sphere is like the relation of the animal soul (al-nafs al-ḥayawāniyya) that we have to us.' And he mentioned in (al-Ishārāt): 'It has along with that a rational soul (nafs nāṭiqa). Its relation to the body of the sphere is like the relation of the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) that we have to us.' And also: he affirmed that each sphere has a separate intellect ('aql). So according to this path, it is necessary that each sphere obtains three things: the animal soul, the rational soul, and the abstract intellect (al-'aql al-mujarrad). As for the animal soul, he argued for its existence by saying: 'The immediate principle (al-mabda' al-qarīb) of this motion is a particular will (irāda juz'iyya). And if that is so, it is necessary that there accrue to that principle particular sciences ('ulūm juz'iyya). And if that is so, it is necessary that that power be corporeal (jismāniyya).' These are three premises: The first premise: concerning the proof of his statement: 'The immediate principle of this motion is a particular will.' The proof for it has two aspects: The first: that the universal essence (al-māhiyya al-kulliyya) is shared among particulars. And the intention (al-qaṣd) towards...
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's (the Chief Master) view on the celestial soul. It begins by stating a key doctrine: the celestial soul that directly moves the heavenly body is a corporeal power pervading that body. Ibn Sina supports this by citing his own works: in al-Najāt and al-Shifā', he compares the celestial soul's relation to its sphere to that of our animal soul to our bodies. In al-Ishārāt, he adds that it also has a rational soul, again analogizing to our rational soul. Furthermore, he affirms that each sphere has a separate intellect. From these, he concludes that each sphere must possess three distinct entities: an animal soul (responsible for motion), a rational soul (for higher cognition), and an abstract intellect (pure intelligence). The passage then focuses on proving the animal soul component. Ibn Sina argues: the immediate cause of celestial motion is a particular will. Since a particular will requires particular knowledge (knowledge of particulars), the power that has this will and knowledge must be corporeal (because corporeal powers are capable of particular perception). This forms the first premise: the immediate principle of motion is a particular will. He then states that there are two proofs for this premise, but only the first is partially given: that the universal essence is common to particulars, and that the intention (to move a particular) requires particular knowledge. The text cuts off mid-explanation. The passage thus sets up a philosophical argument for the nature of celestial souls, distinguishing three levels and beginning the demonstration of the first.

Key Terms
النفس الفلكية
النفس الحيوانية
النفس الناطقة
العقل المجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That celestial souls are purely rational and have no animal aspect.
Correction: Ibn Sina explicitly affirms that each sphere has an animal soul, which is a corporeal power that directly moves the sphere, in addition to a rational soul and an abstract intellect.

Checkpoint

A) Two: an animal soul and a rational soul
B) Three: an animal soul, a rational soul, and an abstract intellect
C) Four: an animal soul, a rational soul, an abstract intellect, and a corporeal power
D) One: a single simple soul

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina classifies each celestial sphere into three aspects: animal soul, rational soul, and abstract intellect.

?
The celestial sphere has three levels, yet many people live only on the animal level. If you died today, would your rational soul have led a life worthy of the abstract intellect's gaze? What one habit will you change this week to raise your soul's focus?

The First Premise: Celestial Motion Requires Particular Will
Ibn Sina presents the first premise of his proof for the animal soul and gives the first of two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's first premise for proving the animal soul: that the immediate principle of celestial motion is a particular will, and the first proof using the universal essence.

Core Idea
Celestial motion needs a particular will, which requires a corporeal power.

Having established that each sphere has an animal soul, Ibn Sina now argues for its existence. He builds a syllogism starting with the claim that the immediate mover is a particular will. This lesson focuses on that first premise and its first proof, which involves the shared nature of universal essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read from 'أما النفس الحيوانية' to the end of the passage. Notice how Ibn Sina structures his proof.

3
Understand the first proof
The first proof states that the universal essence is shared among particulars. Think about what this implies for the need for particular knowledge.
4
Connect to corporeality
Understand why a particular will and knowledge require a corporeal power.

From the speech of the Chief Master (al-shaykh al-ra'is) in most of his books, it indicates that the celestial soul (al-nafs al-falakiyya) that directly moves the body of the heaven is a corporeal power (quwwa jismāniyya) pervading that body. For he said in (al-Najāt) and in (al-Shifā'): 'The relation of the celestial soul to the body of the sphere is like the relation of the animal soul (al-nafs al-ḥayawāniyya) that we have to us.' And he mentioned in (al-Ishārāt): 'It has along with that a rational soul (nafs nāṭiqa). Its relation to the body of the sphere is like the relation of the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) that we have to us.' And also: he affirmed that each sphere has a separate intellect ('aql). So according to this path, it is necessary that each sphere obtains three things: the animal soul, the rational soul, and the abstract intellect (al-'aql al-mujarrad). As for the animal soul, he argued for its existence by saying: 'The immediate principle (al-mabda' al-qarīb) of this motion is a particular will (irāda juz'iyya). And if that is so, it is necessary that there accrue to that principle particular sciences ('ulūm juz'iyya). And if that is so, it is necessary that that power be corporeal (jismāniyya).' These are three premises: The first premise: concerning the proof of his statement: 'The immediate principle of this motion is a particular will.' The proof for it has two aspects: The first: that the universal essence (al-māhiyya al-kulliyya) is shared among particulars. And the intention (al-qaṣd) towards...
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's (the Chief Master) view on the celestial soul. It begins by stating a key doctrine: the celestial soul that directly moves the heavenly body is a corporeal power pervading that body. Ibn Sina supports this by citing his own works: in al-Najāt and al-Shifā', he compares the celestial soul's relation to its sphere to that of our animal soul to our bodies. In al-Ishārāt, he adds that it also has a rational soul, again analogizing to our rational soul. Furthermore, he affirms that each sphere has a separate intellect. From these, he concludes that each sphere must possess three distinct entities: an animal soul (responsible for motion), a rational soul (for higher cognition), and an abstract intellect (pure intelligence). The passage then focuses on proving the animal soul component. Ibn Sina argues: the immediate cause of celestial motion is a particular will. Since a particular will requires particular knowledge (knowledge of particulars), the power that has this will and knowledge must be corporeal (because corporeal powers are capable of particular perception). This forms the first premise: the immediate principle of motion is a particular will. He then states that there are two proofs for this premise, but only the first is partially given: that the universal essence is common to particulars, and that the intention (to move a particular) requires particular knowledge. The text cuts off mid-explanation. The passage thus sets up a philosophical argument for the nature of celestial souls, distinguishing three levels and beginning the demonstration of the first.

الإرادة الجزئية
الماهية الكلية
العلوم الجزئية

Misconception: That celestial motion is caused by an intellect alone without any will or particular intention.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that the immediate principle of motion must be a particular will, which shows a corporeal aspect, not just intellectual.

Checkpoint

B) A particular will

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's first premise for the animal soul is that the immediate mover of celestial motion is a particular will, proven by the shared nature of universal essences.

?
Ibn Sina's proof shows that a universal essence alone cannot cause particular motion. Consider an intention you had today: did it arise from a general principle or from a specific situation? What does that teach you about the limits of abstract reasoning in your life?

First Proof: Universal Essence Cannot Specify Particulars
First proof that immediate principle is a particular will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a universal will cannot cause a particular motion without a specificator, and why a particular will is necessary.

Core Idea
Intending the common does not pick out the particular.

The first proof starts with the nature of universal essences. A universal essence is shared among many particulars. Intending the common aspect does not entail intending any specific particular. Therefore, if a particular motion were caused by a universal will, it would occur without a preponderant and specificator, which is impossible. This forces the conclusion that the immediate cause must be a particular will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof and its English translation once to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'أما قولنا ... وأما أن ...'
2
Understand the key concepts
Identify the concepts of 'universal essence' (الماهية الكلية), 'common part' (القدر المشترك), and 'specificator' (المخصص).
These are central to the proof.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Notice how the author argues: universal essence is shared -> intention of common part does not intend a particular -> particular occurrence without specificator is impossible -> therefore a particular will is needed.
This is a direct argument from impossibility of the contrary.
4
Contemplate the key premise
Reflect on why intending the common part does not entail intending any particular: because the aspect of participation is different from the aspect of distinction.
This is a crucial metaphysical insight.
5
Apply the conclusion
The proof establishes that for any particular voluntary motion, the immediate cause must be a particular will. Consider why this applies to celestial motion as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى : في إثبات قوله : ( المبدأ القريب لهذه الحركة : إرادة جزئية ) . فالدليل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن الماهية الكلية مشترك فيها بين الجزئيات . والقصد إلى القدر المشترك بين الجزئيات لا يفيد القصد إلى واحد من تلك الجزئيات . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع وقوع شيء منها . أما قولنا : ( الماهية الكلية مشترك فيها بين الجزئيات ) فالأمر فيه ظاهر ، لأن الحركة مثلا ماهية مشترك فيها بين الحركة من هذه النقطة إلى تلك ، وبين سائر الحركات . وأما أن القصد إلى القدر المشترك فيه لا يكون قصداً إلى شيء من تلك الجزئيات : فلأن ما به المشاركة مغاير لما به الممايزة ، وغير مستلزم له . فالقصد إلى ما به المشاركة ، لا يكون قصداً إلى ما به الممايزة . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلو وقع جزء واحد من جزئيات ذلك الكلي لكان ذلك وقوعاً من غير المرجح والمخصص . وهو محال . فثبت : أن الحركة الجزئية المعينة إذا كانت إرادية ، فإن المؤثر في وقوعها : إرادية جزئية معينة . والثاني : إن الإرادة الكلية باقية . وبقاء العلة يوجب بقاء المعلول ، فلو كان المقتضي لهذه الحركة المعينة إرادة باقية ، لزم من بقاء تلك الإرادة ، بقاء الجزء المعين من الحركة . وبقاء الجزء المعين من الحركة : محال . إذ لو بقي ذلك الجزء ، لانقطعت الحركة وصارت سكوناً . ولا يمكن أن يقال : المؤثر في حصول ذلك الجزء المعين من الحركة ، هو تلك الإرادة الباقية ، لكن بشرط زوال الجزء السابق من الحركة . لأن على هذا التقدير ، يلزم جعل عدم الحادث السابق ، جزءاً من علة وجود الحادث اللاحق . وذلك محال . ولما بطل هذا ، ثبت : أن العلة الحصول هذه الحركات المتعاقبة : إرادات جزئية متعاقبة . وهو المطلوب .
The first premise: in proving his statement: 'The immediate principle of this motion is a particular will.' The proof for it has two aspects: The first: The universal essence is shared among particulars. And intending the common part among the particulars does not yield intending one of those particulars. And if that is so, it is impossible for any of them to occur. As for our statement: 'The universal essence is an essence shared among particulars,' the matter is clear, because motion, for example, is an essence shared between motion from this point to that, and between other motions. And as for that intending the common part in it is not intending something from those particulars: because that by which there is participation is different from that by which there is distinction, and does not entail it. So intending that by which there is participation is not intending that by which there is distinction. And if that is so, if one part of the particulars of that universal occurred, that would be an occurrence without a preponderant and a specificator. That is impossible. So it is established: that the particular determinate motion, if it is voluntary, then the effecter in its occurrence is a particular determinate volition. The second: The universal will is enduring. And the endurance of the cause necessitates the endurance of the effect. So if the necessitator of this determinate motion were an enduring will, then from the endurance of that will would follow the endurance of the determinate part of motion. And the endurance of the determinate part of motion is impossible. For if that part endured, the motion would cease and become rest. And it cannot be said: 'The effecter in the occurrence of that determinate part of motion is that enduring will, but on condition of the passing away of the previous part of motion.' Because on this assumption, it would necessitate making the non-existence of the previous event a part of the cause of the existence of the subsequent event. That is impossible. And when this is invalidated, it is established: that the cause for the occurrence of these successive motions is successive particular wills. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage provides two rational proofs for the first premise in Ibn Sina's argument for the celestial animal soul. The premise states that the immediate principle of celestial motion is a particular will (إرادة جزئية). The author explains that there are two proofs (دليلان) for this. The first proof argues from the nature of universal essences: a universal essence is shared among particulars; intending the common aspect does not specify any particular. If a particular motion occurred from a universal will, it would happen without a specificator, which is impossible. Hence the cause must be a particular will. The second proof examines the temporal nature of motion: a universal will is enduring, but an enduring cause would produce an enduring effect, which would stop motion. Since motion is successive, the cause must be a series of particular wills that come and go. The conclusion is that the cause of the successive celestial motions is successive particular wills.

إرادة جزئية
إرادة كلية
ماهية كلية
قدر مشترك
مخصص
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A universal will can directly produce a particular motion.
Correction: Intending the common aspect does not specify any particular; a specificator is needed, so the immediate cause must be a particular will.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why can a universal will (إرادة كلية) not produce a particular motion?
A) Because a universal will is weaker than a particular will.
B) Because intending the common part does not specify any particular, so an occurrence would lack a specificator.
C) Because a universal will is eternal and cannot change.
D) Because particular motions require direct contact with the mover.

Key Takeaway
A universal will cannot cause a particular motion; the immediate cause must be a particular will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you set a general intention to do good, do you find that you actually follow through? What specific step have you left out, and how does this lesson show that your vague intention is not enough?
?
Your life is filled with particular actions—praying, speaking, helping. Are you relying on a general 'desire to be good' to produce those actions, or have you concretely specified each one? This lesson says the former is impossible. What will you specify today?
?
If you claim to intend good for all Muslims, but you have not acted for any one of them, are you excused by your universal intention? The proof says no. Name one particular person you will help today, and commit to it now.

Second Proof: Enduring Will Cannot Produce Successive Motions
Second proof that immediate principle is a particular will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an enduring universal will cannot produce successive motions and why a series of particular wills is necessary.

Core Idea
An enduring cause produces an enduring effect, but motion requires change.

The second proof shifts focus to the temporal nature of celestial motion. If the cause were an enduring universal will, its effect would also be enduring. But an enduring part of motion would actually be a state of rest, contradicting the successive nature of motion. Thus, the cause cannot be a single enduring will; it must be a succession of particular wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof and its translation.
Note the conditional: 'فلو كان المقتضي...'
2
Understand the causal principle
Grasp the principle: 'بقاء العلة يوجب بقاء المعلول' – the endurance of the cause necessitates the endurance of the effect.
This is a key causal axiom.
3
Apply to motion
If the will were enduring, the determined part of motion would endure. But an enduring motion part would mean cessation of motion (since motion requires change).
This is a contradiction.
4
Consider the objection
The author anticipates the objection: the enduring will could cause a particular part on condition of the previous part's non-existence. He refutes it because it would make non-existence a cause.
This is a subtle but important refutation.
5
Conclude
Since the objection fails, the only coherent explanation is that successive motions are caused by successive particular wills.
Thus the immediate principle is a particular will for each part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is not like yours in that it does not change or need to be renewed, how does that confront your tendency to imagine God's actions as similar to your own? Will you consciously affirm today that every good thing you experience is from a single, unchanging divine will, even when your own desires fluctuate?
?
This proof shows that the non-existence of a previous event cannot be a cause for the next. In your spiritual life, do you ever treat your past failures (non-existence of good) as necessary causes for your present growth? Where might you be excusing yourself by saying 'I had to sin to learn humility'? What would it mean to instead attribute each present moment directly to God's particular will?
?
The argument forces you to see that an enduring cause produces an enduring effect—so if God's will were unchanging, the world would be static. But you live in a world of constant change and renewal. Does this vast difference between the Creator and creation humble you? Can you let go of the demand that God's dealings with you be predictable and instead trust His wisdom in creating a universe of successive moments?
