First Opinion: Circular Motion from Void and Heavy Body
Author introduces two groups and presents the first opinion with its three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first opinion on the cause of the celestial sphere's circular motion, its reliance on three premises, and why the author presents it as flawed.

Core Idea
A heavy body in infinite void must rotate; that is said to cause the sphere's motion.

This lesson examines the first of several opinions on why the celestial sphere moves in a circle. Supporters of natural motion argue that an infinite void outside the world and a heavy descending body necessarily rotate. The author presents this view but then lists three premises that reveal its weakness, preparing you to see why it is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice how the author first divides people into two groups (natural vs. intentional motion) and then focuses on the first group's first opinion.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two groups
The author says there are two groups regarding the cause of circular motion: those who say it is natural, and those who say it is by intention. This lesson only covers the natural group's first opinion.
3
Understand the first opinion's reasoning
The opinion claims: there is an infinite void outside the world. A heavy body with no support descends, and during descent it must rotate upon itself. This rotation is what causes the sphere's circular motion.
Note the logic: void → descent → rotation → sphere's motion.
4
Examine the three premises
The author says this doctrine rests on three premises: (1) proving an infinite void outside the world, (2) that spheres are heavy, (3) that homogeneous void has no distinguishing features, so no direction is preferred, making eternal descent impossible.
The third premise actually undermines the first opinion.
5
Recognize the author's stance
The author mentions that the second premise has already been shown false, and the third premise makes the opinion incoherent. So he is presenting the opinion to refute it, not endorse it.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

الفصل الثالث في تعديد مذاهب الناس في السبب الموجب، لكون الفلك متحركا بالاستمارة
الناس فريقان : منهم من يقول : إنه يتحرك حركة مستديرة على سبيل الطبيعية . ومنهم من يقول : إنه يتحرك حركة مستديرة بالقصد والإرادة أما الذاهبون إلى القول الأول . فقد ذكروا فيه وجوهاً كثيرة . فالقول الأول قول من يقول : ثبت بالدليل وجود خلاء خارج العالم ، لا نهاية له . والجسم الثقيل الذي لا علاقة فوقه ، ولا دعامة تحته ينزل . والجسم النازل بهذه الصفة ، لا بد وأن يستدير على نفسه حال نزوله . فسبب كون الفلك متحركاً بالاستدارة ، هذا المعنى
واعلم : أن بناء هذا المذهب على مقدمات :
إحداها : إثبات الخلاء. وأنه لا نهاية له خارج العالم . وقد عرفت الكلام فيه .
والثانية : إن أجرام الأفلاك ثقيلة وقد دللنا فيها تقدم على فساد هذا الرأي
والثالثة : إنا قد بينا : أن الخلاء المتشابه الذي لا نهاية له ، لا يتميز فيه جانب من جانب بشيء من الخواص، فلم يكن كون بعضها فوقاً ، وبعضها تحتاً ، أولى من العكس. وإذا تساوت الجهات في جميع الصفات ، امتنع أن يقال : إن الفلك ينزل من جانب إلى جانب آخر نزولاً أبدياً
Chapter Three: On enumerating the opinions of people regarding the cause necessitating that the sphere is moving in a circular manner. People are two groups: some say that it moves in a circular motion in a natural way. And some say that it moves in a circular motion by intention and will. As for those who go to the first opinion, they have mentioned many aspects concerning it. The first opinion is the saying of one who says: It is established by proof the existence of a void outside the world, having no end. And a heavy body that has no connection above it and no support beneath it descends. And a body descending in this manner must rotate upon itself during its descent. So the cause of the sphere being moving in a circular manner is this meaning. And know: that the construction of this doctrine is upon premises: The first: proving the void, and that it has no end outside the world. And you have known the discussion about it. The second: that the bodies of the spheres are heavy, and we have indicated previously the corruption of this view. The third: that we have shown that the homogeneous void that has no end cannot have one side distinguished from another by any property, so it is not more likely that some are above and some are below than the opposite. And when directions are equal in all attributes, it is impossible to say that the sphere descends from one side to another side eternally.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the first opinion on the cause of the celestial sphere's circular motion. The author begins by stating the chapter's topic: enumerating the opinions of people regarding the cause that necessitates the sphere moving in a circular manner. He then divides people into two groups: those who say the sphere moves naturally, and those who say it moves by intention and will. He proceeds to detail the first group, mentioning that they have many aspects. The first opinion within this group is attributed to those who claim that proof establishes the existence of an infinite void outside the world, and that a heavy body with nothing above it and no support beneath it descends. Such a descending body necessarily rotates upon itself during descent, and this is the cause of the sphere's circular motion. The author then notes that this doctrine is built on three premises. The first premise is proving the void and its infinity outside the world, a topic previously discussed. The second premise is that the bodies of the spheres are heavy, which the author claims to have already shown to be corrupt. The third premise is that the homogeneous infinite void has no distinguishing features, so no direction (above or below) is more likely; since all directions are equal, it is impossible to say the sphere descends eternally from one side to another. This third premise undermines the first opinion's explanation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author affirms the first opinion as true.
Correction: The author states that the second premise has been shown false and the third premise makes the opinion incoherent, so he is presenting it for refutation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first opinion's claim that the sphere rotates due to a heavy body descending in an infinite void is undermined by its own premise that the void is homogeneous and directionless.

Second Opinion: Necessary Eternal Motion Without External Cause
Author presents the second opinion that the sphere's motion has no beginning and cannot cease, making it necessary in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that the sphere's motion is necessary in itself and therefore requires no external cause, based on the premises of beginninglessness and impossibility of cessation.

Core Idea
The sphere's motion is self-necessitating and eternal — no external cause needed.

After examining the view that the sphere's motion is caused by an external mover, the author now presents a second opinion: that the motion is necessary in itself. This argument relies on the established premises that the spheres' movements have no beginning, cannot cease, and cannot reverse direction. Let us follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: premises, argument, conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'واجب الوجود لذاته' - it refers to the motion, not to God.
2
Identify the key premises
The author first states three established facts: (1) movements have no beginning, (2) their cessation is impossible, (3) their reversal is impossible. List them.
These premises are taken as given from previous discussions.
3
Follow the causal argument
Trace how each part of motion is necessitated by the preceding part. The sphere's reaching a specific limit from a previous part compels motion to the next limit. Understand that this creates a chain.
This chain implies that the motion is self-perpetuating, requiring no external cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the motion is 'necessary of existence in itself' (واجب الوجود لذاته). Therefore, it cannot be explained by a separate cause. Reflect on why this follows.
This is a key distinction: the motion itself is necessary, not just its existence.
5
Review the implication
Since the motion is necessary in itself, positing an external cause would be contradictory. The sphere moves eternally by its own nature.
This opinion contrasts with the first opinion which required an external mover.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : إنه ثبت : أنه لا بداية الحركات الأفلاك ، وثبت أيضاً : أن انقطاع تلك الحركات محال ، وأن رجوعها عن مجاريها إلى جانب آخر محال ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا جزء من أجزاء الحركة الفلكية ، إلا وقد حصل قبله جزء آخر من الحركة. وانتهاء الفلك بذلك الجزء السابق من الحركة ، إلى ذلك الحد المعين : أوجب حركته من ذلك الحد ، إلى حد آخر بعده . وإذا كان كذلك ، فكل جزء من أجزاء الفلك ، إنما حصل لأن الجزء المتقدم عليه ، أوجب وقوعه على ذلك الوجه [ وعلى هذا التقدير فيكون وقوع هذه الحركة على هذا الوجه (۱) ] أمراً واجب الوجود لذاته (۲) والواجب لذاته لا يجوز تعليله بعلة منفصلة ، فامتنع تعليل كون الفلك متحركاً بعلة منفصلة
The second opinion: that it is established that the movements of the spheres have no beginning, and it is also established that the cessation of those movements is impossible, and their return from their courses to another side is impossible. And when this is established, we say: there is no part of the parts of the sphere's motion except that before it there occurred another part of motion. And the sphere's reaching that previous part of motion to that specific limit necessitated its motion from that limit to another limit after it. And if this is the case, then every part of the sphere's parts only occurred because the preceding part necessitated its occurrence in that manner [and on this assumption, the occurrence of this motion in this manner (1) is a thing necessary of existence in itself (2). And that which is necessary in itself cannot be caused by a separate cause, so it is impossible to explain the sphere's being moving by a separate cause.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion on the cause of the sphere's circular motion. The author states that it is established that the movements of the spheres have no beginning, that their cessation is impossible, and that their reversal from their courses is also impossible. Given these premises, the argument proceeds: every part of the sphere's motion is preceded by another part. The sphere's arrival at a specific limit from the previous part of motion necessitates motion from that limit to the next. Consequently, each part of the sphere's motion exists only because the preceding part necessitated it in that specific manner. The author adds (with an editorial note) that on this assumption, the occurrence of this motion in this way is something necessary of existence in itself. What is necessary in itself cannot be explained by a separate cause. Therefore, it is impossible to attribute the sphere's motion to any external cause. This argument concludes that the motion is self-subsisting and eternal, requiring no external mover.

Correction: Here it refers to the motion itself being necessary, not to God. The motion is self-necessitating and eternal.

The Third Opinion: Eternal Atoms and Circular Motion from Mixture
Author presents the third opinion and its first sub-opinion on celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion on celestial motion: that the sphere's matter consists of eternal atoms and its circular motion arises from the mixture of hot and cold atoms.

Core Idea
Celestial motion may arise from physical mixture of hot and cold atoms, like molten gold.

The author presents a third opinion that the sphere's matter is made of eternal, indivisible atoms, and its form is created. Within this view, he examines two explanations for circular motion. Here, he elaborates on the first: that atoms of different temperatures mix so thoroughly that separation becomes impossible, resulting in a rotational motion. He compares this to molten gold rotating in a crucible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Identify the key components: the third opinion, its two sayings, and the first saying with the analogy.
Notice the author's use of 'قديم المادة' and 'محدث الصورة'.
2
Understand the analogy of molten gold
Trace how the heat difference and fusion prevent separation, leading to rotation. Relate this to the sphere's atoms.
The analogy is central to the argument.
3
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author says it is 'not impossible' — what kind of argument is he making? Consider the strength of this physical explanation.
4
Distinguish from other atomistic theories
Compare this explanation to Ash'ari atomism: here it's about material mixture, not occasionalism.
See the misconception section for clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثالث : إن الفلك قديم المادة ، محدث الصورة . ومادته إنما هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . وتلك الذرات والهباءات. وعلى هذا القول ، ففي سبب حركته بالاستدارة قولان :
الأول : إن تلك الأجزاء . كان بعضها حاراً ، وبعضها بارداً . اختلطت وامتزجت اختلاطاً محكماً ، بحيث تعذر انفصال بعضها عن البعض ، صار ذلك سبباً لحصول الحركة الدورية . ومثاله : أن الذهب إذا ذوب في البوتقة . فإنه بعد الذوب يستدير على نفسه . والسبب فيه : أن الجزء الذي بلاصق البوتقة تقوى سخونته جداً . ولأجل قوة السخونة يصعد ، والجزء المرتفع تضعف سخونته ، فيميل إلى أسفل . وأجزاء الذهب متلاحمة متصلة ، التحاماً واتصالا ، يمنع انفكاك بعضها عن البعض . ولما طلب بعض تلك الأجزاء الصعود ، وبعضها الهبوط ، وتعذر انفصال البعض عن البعض ، صار ذلك سبباً لحصول الحركة الدورية في جرم الذهب الذائب . فلا يمتنع أن يكون سبب استدارة الفلك هذه الحالة ، أو ما يشبهها ويناسبها
And the third opinion: that the sphere is eternal in matter, created in form. And its matter consists of parts that are indivisible, and those are atoms and motes. And on this opinion, there are two sayings concerning the cause of its circular motion: The first: that those parts, some were hot and some were cold. They mixed and intermingled thoroughly, such that separation of some from others became impossible, and that became a cause for the occurrence of the circular motion. And its example: that gold, when it is melted in the crucible, then after melting it rotates upon itself. And the cause in that is that the part adjacent to the crucible becomes very hot, and due to the intensity of heat it rises, and the elevated part weakens in heat, so it inclines downward. And the parts of gold are fused and connected, with a fusion and connection that prevents the disengagement of some from others. And because some of those parts sought ascent and some descent, and the separation of some from others was impossible, that became a cause for the occurrence of circular motion in the body of molten gold. So it is not impossible that the cause of the sphere's rotation is this state, or something similar and appropriate to it.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion on the cause of the sphere's circular motion. It states that the sphere is eternal in its matter (material) but created in its form. Its matter consists of indivisible parts, namely atoms and motes. On this opinion, there are two sub-opinions, the first of which is presented here. According to this first sub-opinion, some of these atoms were hot and some cold. They mixed and intermingled thoroughly, making separation impossible, and this mixing became the cause of circular motion. The author provides an analogy: when gold is melted in a crucible, it rotates upon itself after melting. The cause: the part next to the crucible becomes very hot and rises, while the elevated part cools and inclines downward. The gold parts are fused and connected, preventing separation. Because some parts rise and some fall, but separation is impossible, the result is a circular motion in the molten gold. Similarly, it is not impossible that the sphere's rotation is caused by such a state or something analogous. This sub-opinion explains celestial motion through physical properties of atoms (heat and cold) and their mixture.

محدث الصورة
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
ذرات
هباءات
حركة دورية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This atomistic theory is identical to the Ash'ari atomism.
Correction: This specifically addresses celestial motion based on material mixture, not the general atomistic framework of occasionalism.

The Second Proof: Celestial Motion is Self-Necessitating
Author presents the second argument that celestial motion is necessary and uncaused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the serial necessity of celestial motion leads to the conclusion that such motion is necessary in itself and cannot have an external cause.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is self-necessitating, ruling out an external cause.

This lesson presents a carefully structured proof that the eternal, irreversible motion of the celestial spheres is self-explanatory. The author builds on three established premises and then shows that each part of the motion necessitates the next, making the whole necessary in itself—and therefore uncaused by any separate agent. Let's walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated 'ثبت' which signals established premises.
2
Identify the three premises
The passage opens with three points: the movements have no beginning, their cessation is impossible, and their reversal is impossible. Mark these as the foundations of the argument.
3
Trace the core reasoning
Follow the logic: because every part of motion is preceded by another part, each part is necessitated by the previous one. This makes the entire motion necessary in itself.
The phrase 'واجب الوجود لذاته' is key.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that since the motion is necessary in itself, it cannot be caused by an external cause. Ask: What does this imply about the relationship between the sphere and a separate mover?
The author argues for self-sufficiency of celestial motion, not the denial of a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : إنه ثبت : أنه لا بداية الحركات الأفلاك ، وثبت أيضاً : أن انقطاع تلك الحركات محال ، وأن رجوعها عن مجاريها إلى جانب آخر محال ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا جزء من أجزاء الحركة الفلكية ، إلا وقد حصل قبله جزء آخر من الحركة. وانتهاء الفلك بذلك الجزء السابق من الحركة ، إلى ذلك الحد المعين : أوجب حركته من ذلك الحد ، إلى حد آخر بعده . وإذا كان كذلك ، فكل جزء من أجزاء الفلك ، إنما حصل لأن الجزء المتقدم عليه ، أوجب وقوعه على ذلك الوجه وعلى هذا التقدير فيكون وقوع هذه الحركة على هذا الوجه أمراً واجب الوجود لذاته والواجب لذاته لا يجوز تعليله بعلة منفصلة ، فامتنع تعليل كون الفلك متحركاً بعلة منفصلة
The second statement: It is established that the movements of the celestial spheres have no beginning, and it is also established that the cessation of those movements is impossible, and that their return from their courses to another side is impossible. When this is established, we say: There is no part of the celestial movement except that another part of movement occurred before it. And the sphere's ending at that previous part of movement, to that specific limit, necessitated its movement from that limit to another limit after it. If this is so, then every part of the parts of the sphere only occurred because the preceding part necessitated its occurrence in that manner. And on this estimation, the occurrence of this movement in this manner is a thing necessary in itself. And that which is necessary in itself is not permissible to be caused by a separate cause. Therefore, it is impossible to cause the sphere's being in motion by a separate cause.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the motion of the celestial sphere is necessary in itself and cannot have an external cause. The author begins by establishing three premises: (1) the movements have no beginning (they are eternal), (2) their cessation is impossible, (3) their reversal to another direction is impossible. From these, he argues that every part of the movement is preceded by another part. The sphere's reaching a certain limit from a previous part necessarily causes it to move to the next limit. Therefore, each part of the sphere's motion is necessitated by the previous part, making the entire motion a necessary existent in itself. Since a necessary being cannot have a separate cause, the motion cannot be caused by something external. The argument aims to show that the natural motion of the sphere is self-necessitating, ruling out an external mover. Note the use of 'necessary in itself' (واجب الوجود لذاته), a key term in kalam.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
حركات الأفلاك
تعليل بعلة منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies the existence of a Creator or shows that God is not needed for celestial motion.
Correction: The proof only argues that celestial motion is self-necessitating and uncaused by a separate cause; it does not deny a Creator. The author's aim is to show that the sphere's motion is inherent, not that there is no God.

Checkpoint

D) Because the sphere is not a living being.

Key Takeaway
The serial necessity of celestial motion entails that the motion is necessary in itself and therefore cannot have an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The proof concludes that the sphere's motion is necessary in itself and uncaused. The Third View: Pre-eternal Matter with Originated Form and the Mixing Cause for Rotation
Third view: sphere pre-eternal in matter, originated in form; rotation caused by mixing of hot and cold particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Kalam view on celestial motion, which posits pre-eternal matter and originated form, and the first explanation for its rotation based on the mixing of hot and cold particles.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere rotates because hot and cold atoms mix and conflict, creating circular motion.

This passage presents a physical explanation for the rotation of the celestial sphere without invoking an external mover. Instead, it relies on the inherent properties of matter—specifically, the mixing of hot and cold particles. The author uses the analogy of molten gold to illustrate how such mixing can produce circular motion. Understanding this view is important because it represents a key Kalam attempt to reconcile pre-eternal matter with observed motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author gives the third view and then a first sub-view on cause.
2
Identify the core claim of the third view
The third view states that the celestial sphere is pre-eternal in matter (made of indivisible atoms) but originated in form. Locate this statement.
3
Understand the first cause of rotation
The first sub-view: some atoms are hot, some cold. They mix thoroughly, causing a conflict between rising hot and sinking cold, but cohesion prevents separation, leading to circular motion.
This is a physical mechanism, not a divine act.
4
Examine the gold analogy
The author compares the sphere's rotation to molten gold in a crucible: the part near the crucible gets hot and rises, cooler parts sink, but the gold is cohesive, so it rotates. Read this analogy carefully.
The analogy is meant to show that similar physical causes are plausible.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this explanation avoids needing an external mover and instead attributes motion to the inherent properties of matter. This is a naturalistic explanation within a Kalam framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثالث : إن الفلك قديم المادة ، محدث الصورة . ومادته إنما هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . وتلك الذرات والهباءات. وعلى هذا القول ، ففي سبب حركته بالاستدارة قولان : الأول : إن تلك الأجزاء . كان بعضها حاراً ، وبعضها بارداً . اختلطت وامتزجت اختلاطاً محكماً ، بحيث تعذر انفصال بعضها عن البعض ، صار ذلك سبباً لحصول الحركة الدورية . ومثاله : أن الذهب إذا ذوب في البوتقة . فإنه بعد الذوب يستدير على نفسه . والسبب فيه : أن الجزء الذي بلاصق البوتقة تقوى سخونته جداً . ولأجل قوة السخونة يصعد ، والجزء المرتفع تضعف سخونته ، فيميل إلى أسفل . وأجزاء الذهب متلاحمة متصلة ، التحاماً واتصالا ، يمنع انفكاك بعضها عن البعض . ولما طلب بعض تلك الأجزاء الصعود ، وبعضها الهبوط ، وتعذر انفصال البعض عن البعض ، صار ذلك سبباً لحصول الحركة الدورية في جرم الذهب الذائب . فلا يمتنع أن يكون سبب استدارة الفلك هذه الحالة ، أو ما يشبهها ويناسبها
The third statement: The celestial sphere is pre-eternal in matter, originated in form. Its matter consists of indivisible parts, namely atoms and particles. According to this, regarding the cause of its rotational motion, there are two statements. The first: Those parts, some were hot and some were cold. They intermixed and blended thoroughly, so that separation of some from others became impossible. This became a cause for the occurrence of circular motion. Its example is that gold, when melted in a crucible, after melting, it rotates upon itself. The reason for that is that the part adjacent to the crucible becomes very heated. Due to the intensity of heat, it rises, and the risen part decreases in heat, so it tends downwards. The parts of gold are coherent and connected with a cohesion and connection that prevents the separation of some from others. When some of those parts sought ascent and others descent, and separation of some from others became impossible, that became a cause for the occurrence of circular motion in the body of molten gold. So it is not inconceivable that the cause of the sphere's rotation is this state or something similar and appropriate to it.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the third view on celestial motion, which holds that the sphere's matter is pre-eternal but its form is originated. The matter consists of indivisible atoms. The first of two explanations for its rotation is given: some atoms are hot, some cold. They mix thoroughly, creating a conflict of tendencies: hot atoms rise, cold atoms sink, but due to strong cohesion, they cannot separate. This produces a circular motion. An analogy is made with molten gold: the part closest to the crucible gets very hot and rises, while cooler parts sink, and because the gold is coherent, it rotates. The author concludes that the sphere's rotation could be caused by a similar mechanism. This is a physical explanation for the observed rotation, avoiding the need for an external mover.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This view denies a Creator by attributing motion solely to matter.
Correction: The view does not deny a Creator; it attempts to explain natural motion physically, without necessarily excluding divine involvement.

Checkpoint
What is the analogy used to explain the rotation of the celestial sphere in the first sub-view of the third argument?
A) The rotation of water in a whirlpool.
B) The rotation of molten gold in a crucible.
C) The motion of a spinning top.
D) The orbit of planets around the sun.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere's rotation can be explained by the mixing of hot and cold particles, as analogized by molten gold in a crucible.

?

Or do you fear that any physical explanation diminishes God's role? Reflect honestly: Do you prefer to keep God as a 'God of the gaps', or can you see His hand in the very laws and processes He created?

Democritus: Atomic Collisions and Celestial Motion
Democritus explains atomic collision leading to sphere formation and rotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Democritus' atomistic account of how random collisions in the void generated the sphere and its perpetual rotation.

Core Idea
Random atomic collisions can produce eternal celestial motion without purpose.

Democritus offers a purely mechanical explanation for the motion of the heavens. In his view, the universe is made of atoms moving randomly in infinite void. This passage shows how he believes the sphere formed and began rotating, and why it continues forever.

Guided Path
1
Understand the atomic setting
Recognize that Democritus posits an infinite void with atoms in perpetual random motion. This sets the stage for all that follows.
This is the foundation of his naturalism.
2
Trace the collision and sphere formation
The passage describes that some atoms happen to collide in a specific way, giving rise to the celestial sphere. Notice the role of chance.
There is no guiding intellect; it is mechanical.
3
See the imbalance leading to rotation
Some atomic parts have stronger motion in one direction, overpowering others. This causes the sphere to rotate in a particular manner.
The imbalance is the key to motion.
4
Understand the eternal persistence
Once the rotation is established, the weaker parts cannot overcome the stronger ones. Therefore the sphere continues its motion forever.
This is a mechanical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثاني : قول : ديمقراطيس ، وذلك لأن مذهبه أن هذه الهباءات والذرات كانت متحركة ، حركة دائمة في الخلاء الذي لا نهاية له . ثم اتفق في بعضها أن تصادمت على وجه خاص ، وتعارضت في حركاتها وتمانعت . فتولد الفلك لهذا السبب . ثم اتفق لبعض تلك الأجزاء أن كانت قوة حركاتها إلى بعض الجوانب ، أقوى من قوة الأجزاء التي تمانعها وتعارضها ، فلا جرم الفلك إلى ذلك الجانب ، فاستدار على ذلك الوجه الخاص . ثم إن المغلوب لا يعود غالباً ، والمقهور لا يصير قاهراً . فلا جرم بقي الفلك على تحرك جسم حركة خاصة إلى آخر الأبد . فهذه الوجوه الأربعة مفرعة على قول من يقول : حركات الأفلاك طبيعية
The second statement: the statement of Democritus, because his school is that these particles and atoms were moving with perpetual motion in the infinite void. Then it happened among some of them that they collided in a specific manner, opposed each other in their motions and resisted each other. Thus the sphere was generated for this reason. Then it happened that for some of those parts, the force of their motion toward some directions was stronger than the force of the parts that resisted and opposed them, so inevitably the sphere went toward that side and rotated in that specific manner. Then the overcome does not become the overcomer, and the vanquished does not become the conqueror. So inevitably the sphere remained upon the motion of a body with a particular motion forever. These four aspects are derived from the statement that the movements of the spheres are natural.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage explains Democritus' atomistic account of celestial motion. He posits an infinite void in which atoms are in perpetual random motion. Some atoms happen to collide in a specific way, forming the sphere. Among the parts, some have stronger motion in one direction, overcoming the opposition, causing rotation. Once this state is established, the imbalance persists forever because the weaker parts cannot reverse the situation. Thus the sphere continues its specific motion eternally. The author then notes that these four views (the second proof, the third view with its two sub-views, and this one) all derive from the claim that celestial motions are natural (tabi'iyya). This sets the stage for the next section on voluntary motion. The passage demonstrates a philosophical naturalism where no purpose or intellect is involved, only mechanical necessity.

The First Team Denies a Specific Purpose in Spheres' Motion
The author introduces the voluntary motion view and presents the first team's argument that the spheres have no specific purpose in their motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first group's argument that voluntary motion does not require a specific purpose, and why they hold that all orbits are equally possible.

Core Idea
Identical points on a sphere make all motions equally possible, so choice is free.

In this passage, the author begins discussing the voluntary motion view and immediately splits its proponents into two camps. The first camp denies that the spheres have any specific purpose in their motion. Their argument is built on the idea that celestial bodies are simple and all points on them are identical. If that is true, then no orbit is naturally more likely than any other. This sets the stage for a volitional agent to choose arbitrarily.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the example is incomplete.
2
Identify the two groups
Notice that the author divides supporters of voluntary motion into two groups: those who deny a specific purpose and those who affirm one. This lesson focuses on the first group.
3
Understand the premise of similarity
The argument's foundation: celestial bodies are simple, and all assumed points on them are identical in reality and essence. Grasp why this is key.
If points are identical, any difference in possibility would imply contradiction.
4
Follow the logical deduction
From similarity, the author deduces that all orbits must be equally possible. If some were possible and others not, identical things would have different properties, which is impossible.
This leads to the conclusion that no movement has a natural advantage.
5
Consider the incomplete example
The passage ends with an example of a hungry person choosing food. Think about how this illustrates the idea of a volitional agent choosing arbitrarily among identical options.
The example is cut off, but the point is that choice without a natural cause is plausible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: إن حركاتها إرادية . فالقائلون بهذا القول فريقان : منهم من قال : إنه ليس للأفلاك غرض معين في اختيار هذه الحركات ومنهم من أثبت لها غرضاً معيناً . أما الفريق الأول : فتقرير قولهم : إن الأجرام الفلكية بسائط وجميع النقط المفترضة فيها متشابهة في تمام الحقيقة، ومتساوية في تمام الماهية . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون وقوع تلك الحركات على جميع المدارات المختلفة التي لا نهاية لها : ممكناً . إذ لو أمكن البعض ، وامتنع البعض ، لـزم أن تكون الأشياء المتماثلة في تمام الماهية مختلفة في اللوازم والآثار ، وذلك محال . فثبت : أن الكل ممكن وأنه ليس للبعض رجحان على البعض لا في أصل الإمكان ولا في السهولة ، ولا في أثر من الآثار البتة . ومتى تشابهت الأفعال ، وتساوت على الوجه الذي ذكرناه لم يمتنع أن يختار الفاعل المختار أحد تلك الأقسام دون الأخرى لا لعلة . ومثاله : أن الجائع إذا خير بين أكل
As for the second division: it is to say that their movements are voluntary. Those who hold this opinion are two groups: among them are those who say that the spheres have no specific purpose in choosing these movements, and among them are those who affirm a specific purpose. As for the first group: the exposition of their statement: The celestial bodies are simple, and all the assumed points in them are similar in complete reality and equal in complete essence. When it is so, it is necessary that the occurrence of those movements upon all the different infinite orbits is possible. For if some were possible and some impossible, it would necessitate that things identical in complete essence differ in their entailments and effects, and that is impossible. So it is established that all are possible, and that some have no preponderance over others in either the principle of possibility or in ease, nor in any effect at all. When actions are similar and equal in the manner we have described, it is not impossible that the volitional agent choose one of those divisions over another without a cause. An example: when a hungry person is given the choice between eating...
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage opens the second major division of opinions: that the motions of the spheres are voluntary rather than natural. Within this division, there are two sub-groups. The first group denies that the spheres have a specific purpose in their motions. Their argument is based on the nature of celestial bodies: they are simple (بسائط) and all points on them are identical in reality and essence. If that is the case, then motion along any of the infinite possible orbits is equally possible. If some orbits were impossible and others possible, that would imply that identical things have different properties, which is impossible. Therefore, all orbits are equally possible with no inherent advantage. When all actions are similar and equal, a volitional agent can choose one without a cause. The author gives an example (though incomplete) of a hungry person choosing between foods. This argument aims to show that the observed specific motion of the sphere cannot be explained by nature alone but requires a volitional agent that arbitrarily chooses one direction.

Democritus' Atomic Theory of Celestial Motion
Presents Democritus' atomic theory of celestial sphere formation and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Democritus' mechanistic explanation of the formation and perpetual motion of the celestial sphere from random atomic collisions.

Core Idea
Random atomic collisions, not design, explain the heavens according to Democritus.

This passage presents the second of several opinions on the cause of celestial motion, that of the ancient atomist Democritus. He argued that the celestial sphere and its motion emerged from chance collisions of atoms in infinite void, without any purpose or intelligence. We will examine his theory as a representative of the naturalistic, materialistic view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's presentation of Democritus' theory come through.
Notice the sequential logic: initial state, collision, generation, rotation, perpetuation.
2
Identify the key stages
List the steps in Democritus' explanation: perpetual motion in void, chance collision, generation of sphere, asymmetry of forces causing rotation, and eternal continuation.
Each step follows from the previous in a material cause-effect chain.
3
Note the absence of teleology
Observe that there is no mention of purpose, design, or external cause. Everything is due to material interactions and chance.
This contrasts with other views that invoke intelligence or nature.
4
Consider the author's purpose
Think why the author includes this theory. He is presenting it as one of several opinions to be classified and eventually refuted.
Do not mistake this as the author's own view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثاني : قول : ديمقراطيس ، وذلك لأن مذهبه أن هذه الهباءات والذرات كانت متحركة ، حركة دائمة في الخلاء الذي لا نهاية له . ثم اتفق في بعضها أن تصادمت على وجه خاص ، وتعارضت في حركاتها وتمانعت . فتولد الفلك لهذا السبب . ثم اتفق لبعض تلك الأجزاء أن كانت قوة حركاتها إلى بعض الجوانب ، أقوى من قوة الأجزاء التي تمانعها وتعارضها ، فلا جرم الفلك إلى ذلك الجانب ، فاستدار على ذلك الوجه الخاص . ثم إن المغلوب لا يعود غالباً ، والمقهور لا يصير قاهراً . فلا جرم بقي الفلك على جرم تحرك جسم الحركة الخاصة إلى آخر الأبد
The second opinion: the opinion of Democritus. This is because his doctrine is that these particles and atoms were moving with a perpetual motion in infinite void. Then it happened that some of them collided in a specific manner, and their motions opposed and hindered each other. Thus the celestial sphere was generated for this reason. Then it happened that for some of those parts, the force of their motion toward certain directions was stronger than the force of the parts that hindered and opposed them; inevitably the sphere [moved] toward that direction, and it rotated in that specific manner. Then the overcome does not become the overcomer, and the vanquished does not become the victor. Therefore the sphere necessarily remained upon [its] body, moving with that specific motion forever.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents Democritus' materialistic explanation for the motion of the celestial sphere. It is a specific ancient theory (one of the 'four aspects' mentioned later) that belongs to the view that celestial motions are natural (tabi'iyya). The author describes Democritus' doctrine: originally, atoms (dharrat) and particles (hubā'āt) moved randomly in infinite void (khalā'). Due to chance collisions, some atoms clashed in a particular way, causing mutual opposition and hindrance, which generated the celestial sphere. Then, because certain parts had stronger motion in some directions than their opposing parts, the sphere inevitably rotated in that direction. Once this order was established, the overcome never becomes overcomer, so the sphere continues in its specific motion eternally. This is a mechanistic, atomistic explanation without intelligent design.

Voluntary Celestial Motion Without Purpose
Divides celestial motions into voluntary; presents the view that they choose without purpose, with argument and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that celestial motions are voluntary and can be chosen without a determinate purpose, based on the simplicity and similarity of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
Celestial bodies, being simple and uniform, allow choice without a cause.

In this passage, the author turns from natural motions to voluntary celestial motions. He identifies two subgroups within those who hold this view: one that denies a determinate purpose and one that affirms it. Here we examine the first group's reasoning, which hinges on the nature of celestial bodies as simple and uniform, leading to the conclusion that any orbit is equally possible and that a free agent can choose without an external determinant. An everyday example illustrates the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the structure: a classification, an argument, and an example.
2
Understand the classification
The author divides the voluntary motion view into two groups. Identify what distinguishes the first group: they deny a determinate purpose.
3
Analyze the argument's premises
The argument begins with the nature of celestial bodies: they are simple (basā'iṭ) and all points are similar. Why does this matter? It leads to the conclusion that all orbits are equally possible.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
If all motions are equally possible, then a free agent (the celestial soul) can choose any without a cause. The text provides an example: a hungry person choosing between two identical loaves.
Distinguish this from randomness—the choice is willful but without a preponderant factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إن حركاتها إرادية . فالقائلون بهذا القول فريقان : منهم من قال : إنه ليس للأفلاك غرض معين في اختيار هذه الحركات ومنهم من أثبت لها غرضاً معيناً . أما الفريق الأول : فتقرير قولهم : إن الأجرام الفلكية بسائط وجميع النقط المفترضة فيها متشابهة في تمام الحقيقة، ومتساوية في تمام الماهية . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون وقوع تلك الحركات على جميع المدارات المختلفة التي لا نهاية لها : ممكناً . إذ لو أمكن البعض ، وامتنع البعض ، لـزم أن تكون الأشياء المتماثلة في تمام الماهية مختلفة في اللوازم والآثار ، وذلك محال . فثبت : أن الكل ممكن وأنه ليس للبعض رجحان على البعض لا في أصل الإمكان ولا في السهولة ، ولا في أثر من الآثار البتة . ومتى تشابهت الأفعال ، وتساوت على الوجه الذي ذكرناه لم يمتنع أن يختار الفاعل المختار أحد تلك الأقسام دون الأخرى لا لعلة . ومثاله : أن الجائع إذا خير بين أكل رغيفين ، والعطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الآخر لا لمرجح . وكذلك النفس التي للفلك الأعظم ، ولسائر الأفلاك ، اختار كل واحد منها نوعاً معيناً من الحركة ، لا لغرض ولا لمرجح البتة
As for the second division: that it is said that their motions are voluntary. Those who hold this opinion are two groups: some of them said that the heavens have no determinate purpose in choosing these motions, and some affirmed a determinate purpose for them. As for the first group: the exposition of their statement: the celestial bodies are simple (basā'iṭ) and all hypothetical points in them are similar in complete reality and equal in complete quiddity. When this is the case, it must be that the occurrence of those motions on all the different infinite orbits is possible. For if some were possible and others impossible, then things similar in complete quiddity would differ in their concomitants and effects, which is impossible. So it is established that everything is possible, and that some have no preponderance over others, neither in the principle of possibility nor in ease, nor in any effect at all. When actions are similar and equal in the manner we mentioned, it is not impossible that the free agent chooses one of those divisions over another without a cause. An example: a hungry person when given the choice between eating two loaves, and a thirsty person when given the choice between drinking two cups, he chooses one of them without a preponderant factor. Likewise, the soul of the supreme heaven and of the other heavens each chose a specific type of motion without any purpose or preponderant factor at all.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage classifies the views on celestial motions. After discussing natural motions, the author turns to the second division (al-qism al-thānī): voluntary motions (ḥarakāt irādiyya). Those who hold this view split into two groups. The first group denies that the heavens have any determinate purpose (gharaḍ mu'ayyan) in choosing their motions. Their argument rests on the nature of celestial bodies: they are simple (basā'iṭ) and all points in them are similar in reality and equal in quiddity. Hence, any motion on any of the infinitely many possible orbits is equally possible. If some were possible and others not, then identical things would differ in their properties, which is absurd. Therefore, all possible motions are equally possible. Since the agent (the celestial soul) is a free chooser (fā'il mukhtār), it can select any one of these equally possible motions without any cause, just as a hungry person may choose between two identical loaves without a preponderant reason. This argument denies the need for an external determinant for celestial motion, relying on the homogeneity of the celestial substance.

Key Terms
إرادية
غرض معين
بسائط
ماهية
فاعل مختار
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example suggests celestial motion is random or arbitrary.
Correction: The text emphasizes that the choice is made by a free agent (the celestial soul) and is willful, not random. The 'without a cause' refers to no external determinant, not absence of will.

Checkpoint
According to the first group, why are all possible celestial orbits equally possible?
A) Because celestial bodies are composite and have different parts
B) Because celestial bodies are simple and all points are similar
C) Because God predetermines all motions
D) Because the heavens have a specific purpose

Key Takeaway
The first group argues that because celestial bodies are simple and uniform, all motions are equally possible, allowing a free agent to choose without a determinate purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The hungry person chooses one loaf over an identical one without any reason. Think of a time you made a decision without a clear reason. Did you doubt or second-guess yourself? How does this argument from celestial motion challenge your need to always have a ‘why’?
?
If the celestial souls choose their motions without purpose, are you comfortable with the idea that some of your own choices might also be free acts of will with no deeper purpose? Or does that unsettle you? Why?
?
The text suggests that choice without a cause is not impossible. How does this affect your view of God's actions? Do you ever question God's wisdom when you cannot see a purpose? How would this lesson help you trust that His will is enough?

The Three Subgroups of the Determinate Purpose View
Presents the three views of those who affirm a determinate purpose for celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct subgroups within the second opinion on celestial motion's purpose, including their specific claims and the illustrative analogy for the combined view.

Core Idea
Celestial motions are purposeful — but what kind of purpose? Three answers: self-perfection, world-order, or both.

In the previous discussion, we encountered the first opinion that denies any purpose in celestial motions. Now we explore the second opinion, which affirms a determinate purpose. However, this opinion is not monolithic; it branches into three subgroups. This passage lays out these three views, each offering a different account of what the heavens aim to achieve. The third subgroup even provides an analogy to clarify its position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three subgroups
Read the passage and note the three groups mentioned. What are their names or descriptions?
The groups are defined by what they claim as the purpose.
2
Compare the first two subgroups
The first focuses on acquiring perfections from higher principles. The second focuses on cultivating this world and its order. How do they differ?
One is self-directed, the other world-directed.
3
Understand the combined view
The third subgroup merges both: the motions originate for perfections, but their specific directions and speeds serve worldly interests.
4
Analyze the analogy
The example of the generous man illustrates how the combined view works: a good being naturally chooses the path that benefits the weak.
Avoid anthropomorphism — this is an analogy for a celestial soul.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author calls these 'three well-regulated opinions.' Why might they be considered well-regulated?
They cover different aspects of purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الثاني : قول من يقول : إن للفلك في حركته غرضاً معيناً معتبراً . وهؤلاء فرق ثلاث : منهم من يقول : ذلك الغرض : كمالات عالية ، يستفيدها من المبادىء العالية لنفسه. ومنهم من يقول : ذلك الغرض عمارة هذا العالم ، وحصول نظامه . ومنهم من جمع بين الطرفين فقال : أصل حركاتها لأجل استفادة الكمالات من المبادىء العالية . وأما جهات حركاتها ومقادير بطئها وسرعتها ، فلأجل مصالح هذا العالم العنصري . وذكروا لهذا مثالاً . وهو : أن رجلاً كريم النفس ، كثير الخير . إذا أراد الذهاب إلى موضع المهم له فيه . وكان إلى ذلك الموضع طريقان متساويان بالنسبة إليه من كل الوجوه. إلا أن ذهابه في أحد الطريقين ، يوجب حصول المنافع الكثيرة لطائفة من الضعفاء ، والعاجزين ، واندفاع الشرور الكثيرة عنهم . وذهابه في الطريق الثاني يوجب بطلان تلك الخيرات والمصالح ، فإن كونه في ذاته خيراً رحيماً ، يقتضي أن يختار الطريق الأنفع للضعفاء ، ويترك الطريق العاري عن النفع . فهذه أقوال ثلاثة مضبوطة
The second opinion: the opinion of those who say that the heaven has in its motion a determinate and considered purpose. These are three groups: some say that purpose is high perfections which it acquires from the high principles for itself. Some say that purpose is the cultivation of this world and the attainment of its order. Some combined the two and said: the origin of its motions is for the sake of acquiring perfections from the high principles. As for the directions of its motions and the measures of its slowness and speed, that is for the sake of the interests of this elemental world. They gave an example for this: a man of generous spirit, abundant in goodness. If he wants to go to a place important to him, and there are two paths equally good in all respects from his perspective, except that going on one path brings many benefits to a group of the weak and helpless and repels many evils from them, while going on the other path would nullify those goods and interests; then his being good and merciful by essence requires that he choose the more beneficial path for the weak, and leave the path devoid of benefit. These are three well-formulated opinions.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of voluntary celestial motions, now presenting the second group—those who affirm a determinate purpose (gharaḍ mu'ayyan mu'tabar) for the heavens. This group is subdivided into three opinions (firaq thalāth). The first says the purpose is to acquire high perfections (kamālāt 'āliya) from the high principles (al-mabādi' al-'āliya) for itself. The second says the purpose is the cultivation ('imāra) of this world and its order (niẓām). The third combines both: the basic motions are for acquiring perfections, but the directions, speed, and slowness are for the welfare of the elemental world (al-'ālam al-'unṣurī). To illustrate the combined view, they give an example of a generous man who, when traveling to an important place via two equally good paths, would choose the one that benefits the weak because he is inherently good and merciful. Similarly, the celestial soul, being good, chooses the path that benefits the lower world. The author concludes that these are three well-regulated opinions (aqwāl thalātha maḍbūṭa).

Key Terms
غرض معين
كمالات عالية
مبادىء عالية
عمارة هذا العالم
نظام
عالم عنصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example of the generous man implies that the celestial soul has human-like emotions and chooses arbitrarily.
Correction: The analogy is meant to illustrate that a good being naturally inclines toward beneficence, not to ascribe human psychology to celestial souls. It is a rational argument about what a perfect being's actions would entail.

Checkpoint
According to the combined view (the third subgroup), what is the purpose of the actual directions and speeds of celestial motions?
A) Acquiring perfections from high principles
B) The welfare of the elemental world
C) Random variation
D) The self-sufficiency of the heavens

Key Takeaway
The three subgroups of the second opinion systematically present the possible purposes of celestial motions: self-perfection, world-order, or a combination of both, with the combined view supported by an analogy of generous action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The generous man in the analogy chooses the path that benefits the weak, even though both paths are equal for him. Where in your daily life do you have a choice between two equally convenient options—one that helps others and one that does not? Which do you usually pick, and what does that reveal about your own soul's orientation?
?
The combined view holds that the aim of celestial motions is self-perfection plus service to the lower world. If your own actions were analyzed, would they show a balance between seeking your own perfection and contributing to the order and good of those around you? Where is the imbalance, and what one change could you make today to align with that higher pattern?
?
If the celestial sphere, which is noble and perfect, still moves for the benefit of weak creatures in this material world, what does that say about your own attitude toward people you consider beneath you? Whom do you ignore or dismiss, and how can you adopt the 'generous spirit' described in the analogy starting this week?

Three Positions on the Purpose of Celestial Motions
Razi enumerates three scholarly positions on the purpose of celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on the purpose of celestial motions, including the subgroups of those who affirm purpose.

Core Idea
Celestial motions are either purposeless or purposeful, with three nuanced views on that purpose.

This passage from Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a key debate in Islamic cosmology: do the celestial spheres move with a purpose? Razi enumerates the main positions, from complete denial to elaborate theories of cosmic benefit. We'll explore each position and the underlying analogies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that celestial motions were a central topic in Islamic cosmology, raising questions about causality and purpose. This passage directly addresses those debates.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the first position (no purpose) is illustrated by analogies, then the second position with three subgroups is expounded, including an example.
The passage begins mid-sentence, which is a continuation from the previous page.
3
Identify the two broad positions
The first position denies any purpose in celestial motions. The second affirms a purpose, and is divided into three groups. Note that the 'three positions' in the title refer to the subgroups of the second position.
4
Examine the subgroups of the second position
List the three groups: (1) purpose is high perfections from above, (2) purpose is the cultivation and order of this world, (3) combined view: origin for perfections, directions/speeds for worldly interests.
5
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the example of the generous man choosing the road that benefits the weak. How does this illustrate the combined view? Can you relate it to the celestial spheres' motions?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رغيفين ، والعطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الآخر لا لمرجح . وكذلك النفس التي للفلك الأعظم ، ولسائر الأفلاك ، اختار كل واحد منها نوعاً معيناً من الحركة ، لا لغرض ولا لمرجح البتة . والقول الثاني : قول من يقول : إن للفلك في حركته غرضاً معيناً معتبراً . وهؤلاء فرق ثلاث : منهم من يقول : ذلك الغرض : كمالات عالية ، يستفيدها من المبادىء العالية لنفسه. ومنهم من يقول : ذلك الغرض عمارة هذا العالم ، وحصول نظامه . ومنهم من جمع بين الطرفين فقال : أصل حركاتها لأجل استفادة الكمالات من المبادىء العالية . وأما جهات حركاتها ومقادير بطئها وسرعتها ، فلأجل مصالح هذا العالم العنصري . وذكروا لهذا مثالاً . وهو : أن رجلاً كريم النفس ، كثير الخير . إذا أراد الذهاب إلى موضع المهم له فيه . وكان إلى ذلك الموضع طريقان متساويان بالنسبة إليه من كل الوجوه. إلا أن ذهابه في أحد الطريقين ، يوجب حصول المنافع الكثيرة لطائفة من الضعفاء ، والعاجزين ، واندفاع الشرور الكثيرة عنهم . وذهابه في الطريق الثاني يوجب بطلان تلك الخيرات والمصالح ، فإن كونه في ذاته خيراً رحيماً ، يقتضي أن يختار الطريق الأنفع للضعفاء ، ويترك الطريق العاري عن النفع . فهذه أقوال ثلاثة مضبوطة
... two loaves, and the thirsty person, if he is given a choice between two cups of drink, he chooses one over the other without any preference. Likewise, the soul of the Supreme Sphere and of the other spheres, each of them chose a specific kind of motion, with no purpose or preference at all. The second position: the position of those who say that the sphere has in its motion a specific, considered purpose. These are three groups: some of them say: that purpose is high perfections, which it derives from the high principles for itself. Some of them say: that purpose is the cultivation of this world and the attainment of its order. Some of them combined both sides and said: the origin of its motions is for deriving perfections from the high principles; but the directions of its motions and the measures of its slowness and speed are for the interests of this elemental world. They gave an example of this: a man of noble character, abundant goodness, if he wants to go to a place important to him, and there are two roads equal to him in every respect, except that his going on one road causes many benefits for a group of weak and incapable people and the repulsion of many evils from them, and his going on the second road causes the nullification of those goods and interests, then his being good and merciful in essence requires that he choose the more beneficial road for the weak and abandon the road devoid of benefit. These are three precise positions.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage enumerates the different positions regarding the purpose of celestial motions. It begins by illustrating the first position (the denial of purpose) through examples: a hungry person choosing between two loaves or a thirsty person choosing between two cups without any preference, analogously, the celestial souls choose a specific motion without any purpose or preference. This first position is not explicitly numbered but is implied. Then the author states the second position, which affirms that the sphere has a specific and considered purpose. This second position is then subdivided into three groups: 1) Those who say the purpose is high perfections derived from the high principles. 2) Those who say the purpose is the cultivation and order of this world. 3) Those who combine both: the origin of motions is for perfections from above, but the directions and speeds are for the interests of the elemental world. An example is given of a generous man choosing between two equal roads, one of which benefits the weak, analogizing the choice of the sphere. The passage concludes by stating that these are three precise positions, referring to the three groups within the second position.

غرض (purpose)
كمالات (perfections)

نفس الفلك (soul of the sphere)

Misconception: The first position (denial of purpose) might be confused as being part of the three enumerated positions.
Correction: The three enumerated positions are actually the three subgroups within the second position (affirmation of purpose), not including the first position of denial.

Razi's Critique of Physical Perfections as the Goal of Celestial Motions
Razi explains and rejects the physical perfections view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the view that celestial motions aim at physical perfections, and why he finds it far-fetched.

Core Idea
Razi rejects the idea that the spheres move just to attain physical perfections.

This passage presents Razi's engagement with the Aristotelian tradition regarding the purpose of celestial motions. Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina argued that the spheres move to actualize their remaining potential positions. Razi strongly dismisses this view as unworthy of a wise person, setting the stage for his own alternative.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification
Notice how Razi divides the perfections into physical and psychic. This sets up the focus on the first type.
The translation clarifies that 'physical' here means bodily perfections.
2
Examine the philosophers' argument
Read how al-Farabi and Ibn Sina argue that only the sphere's positions remain in potentiality, and motion extracts them.
Their reasoning uses the concept of potentiality and actuality.
3
See Razi's strong rejection
Note Razi's decisive language: 'very far-fetched' and 'not befitting a wise, virtuous man'. This is a clear dismissal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القول الأول : وهو أن الغرض للفلك من هذه الحركات استفادة الكمالات وتحصيل السعادات فأقول : التقسيم الصحيح في هذا المقام . أن يقال : تلك الكمالات إما أن تكون جسمانية أو نفسانية . أما القسم الأول فهو المختار عند الحكماء المتأخرين . كـ ( أبي نصر الفارابي ) و ( أبي علي بن سينا ) . وذلك لأنهم قالوا : إن جوهر الفلك كامل في ذاته وفي جميع صفاته. ولم يبق فيه شيء بالقوة ، إلا وقد خرج إلى الفعل إلا في إيوانه وأوضاعه. فإن خروج كلها من القوة إلى الفعل محال . وإلا لزم حصول الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة ، في أمكنة كثيرة ، وذلك محال . فلهذا السبب بقيت الأيون والأوضاع فيه بالقوة ، ولم تخرج بكليتها إلى الفعل [ فهو يتحرك لأجل استخراجها من القوة إلى الفعل ] هذا هو القول المختار عند هذين الرجلين الفاضلين ، ونقلاء عن الحكيم ( أرسطاطاليس ، وهو عندي بعيد جداً ، ولا يليق بالرجل العاقل الفاضل أن يميل عقله إليه
As for the first position: that the purpose of the sphere from these motions is deriving perfections and attaining felicities. I say: the correct division in this place is that these perfections are either physical or psychic. As for the first division, it is the chosen view of the later philosophers such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali ibn Sina. That is because they said: the substance of the sphere is perfect in itself and in all its attributes, and nothing remains in it in potentiality except that it has come into actuality, except in its positions and postures. For all of them to come from potentiality into actuality is impossible, otherwise it would necessitate the existence of one body at one time in many places, and that is impossible. For this reason, the positions and postures remained in it in potentiality and did not come out entirely into actuality. So it moves in order to extract them from potentiality into actuality. This is the chosen view of these two virtuous men, transmitted from the philosopher Aristotle, but it is in my view very far-fetched and not befitting a wise, virtuous man to incline his mind towards it.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the first position among the three previously enumerated: that the sphere's motion aims at deriving perfections and attaining felicities. The author (Razi) begins by stating the correct division: these perfections are either physical (jismaniyya) or psychic (nafsaniyya). The first division (physical perfections) is the view of the later philosophers, notably al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Their argument is that the sphere's substance is already perfect itself and in all attributes, with nothing remaining in potentiality except its positions and postures (positions and orientations). It is impossible for all positions to become actual simultaneously because that would require the same body to be in many places at once. Therefore, the positions remain partially in potentiality, and the sphere moves in order to actualize them sequentially. Razi then states that this is the view transmitted from Aristotle, but he strongly rejects it, calling it 'very far-fetched' (ba'id jiddan) and unworthy of a wise person. This critique is decisive and sets up the alternative psychic perfections view discussed in the next section.

Key Terms
كمالات جسمانية (physical perfections)
جوهر الفلك (substance of the sphere)
قوة (potentiality)
فعل (actuality)
أيون وأوضاع (positions and postures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Razi rejects any purpose for celestial motions.
Correction: Razi's rejection is specifically of the physical perfections view; he accepts psychic perfections as the purpose.

Checkpoint
According to al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, what remains in potentiality in the celestial sphere?
A) Its essence and attributes
B) Its positions and postures
C) Its life and intellect
D) Its motion and rest

Key Takeaway
Razi rejects the physical perfections view, deeming it far-fetched and unworthy of a wise person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' argument sounds plausible and is endorsed by great minds like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Yet Razi calls it 'very far-fetched.' Are there beliefs about Allah's creation or your own life that you hold simply because they are popular or come from respected figures? Identify one such belief. Will you examine it honestly today?
?
Razi says the view is 'not befitting a wise, virtuous man to incline his mind towards it.' This is a strong personal judgment. When you encounter an argument that feels intellectual but contradicts your intuition about God's power or generosity, do you suppress that intuition? Ask yourself: Is there any area of your faith where you have adopted a 'sophisticated' explanation that deep down feels far-fetched? What would it take to return to a simpler, more certain truth?
?
The sphere is said to move to acquire perfections that it already possesses in essence but not in position. Do you ever feel you are chasing after things you already have—like God's love, forgiveness, or purpose—through complicated theories, while neglecting the direct path of obedience and sincerity? Where in your life have you replaced worship with intellectual posturing? Commit today to one concrete action that reconnects you to the heart of faith.

Psychic Perfections and the Argument for Life
Razi enumerates psychic perfections and argues for life as a purpose of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the celestial sphere's motion is aimed at preserving life, based on induction and the concomitant relationship between life and motion.

Core Idea
Life and motion are necessarily linked; the sphere's perfect motion reflects its perfect life.

Now we move from physical perfections to psychic ones. Razi identifies three kinds of psychic perfection: life, perception, and intellect. He then focuses on the first—life—and builds an inductive case that the sphere's constant circular motion is a direct expression of its life. This passage uses a careful philosophical argument to connect observable regularities (like the heart's motion) to celestial phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the full Arabic text once without translating. Notice the structure: it opens the second division, lists three possibilities, then elaborates the first possibility with an inductive proof and an analogy.
Pay attention to the terms 'اللطافة', 'الشفافية', 'النورانية'—they are qualities associated with life.
2
Identify the three psychic perfections
The passage says psychic perfections are one of three: life, perception, or intellect. Each is possible here, but only the first is detailed. Make a note of the three.
The author focuses on life, but remember the other two are left for later discussion.
3
Understand the inductive argument for life and motion
Razi claims induction shows that subtlety, transparency, luminosity, motion, life-power, and intellect are always or mostly together. Conversely, density, stillness, death, and ignorance go together. Work through this chain and see how he uses it.
This is a key philosophical move: establishing a natural concomitance (تلازم) between life and motion.
4
Analyze the analogy of the heart and the sphere
He uses the heart as an analogy: the heart is the fountain of life and is always moving. Apply this to the celestial sphere: it is the fountain of life, so its motion is constant. Then he contrasts the Earth, which is dense and still.
The analogy reinforces the inductive claim with a familiar example.
5
Grasp the conclusion about celestial motion
Since the sphere's life is more perfect and enduring, its motion is the noblest: circular, uniform, and ordered. This concludes the argument that the sphere's aim is to preserve life.
The conclusion ties back to the initial statement about the aim of motion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين في التقسيم : أن يقال : إن مقصود الفلك من تلك الحركات [ استفادة ] كمالات نفسانية واعلم : أن الكمالات النفسانية أحد أمور ثلاثة : إما الحياة ، وإما الإدراك ، وإما العقل، وكل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة محتمل ههنا . أما الاحتمال الأول : وهو أن يقال : المقصود من هذه الحركة إبقاء قوة الحياة . فتقريره : أن يقال : الاستقراء دل على أن اللطافة والشفافية والنورانية والحركة وقوة الحياة والعقل : أمور متلازمة . إما دائماً وإما في الأكثر . وأما الكلمة والسكون [ والموت ] والجهل . فهي أمور متلازمة . إما دائماً ، وإما في الأكثر . ألا ترى أن القلب لما كان ينبوع الحياة ، لا جرم كان دائم الحركة . فكذا ههنا الفلك لما كان ينبوع الحياة ، لا جرم كان دائم الحركة ، ولما كان حياة الفلك أكمل وأشرف من هذه الأجسام ، لا جرم كانت حركاته أشرف الحركات . وهي المستديرة الخالية عن التفاوت والاختلاف ، الموصوفة بكل ترتيب ونظام . وأما جرم الأرض . فلما كان معدن الظلمة والكثافة ، لا جرم كان معدن السكون والجمود . فالموت والسكون متلازمان ، والحياة والحركة متلازمتان. ولما كانت الحياة في عالم الأفلاك ، أبقى وأشرف ، كانت الحركات هناك أكمل وأشرف .
The second division of the two mentioned divisions: that it is said: the aim of the sphere from those motions is the acquisition of psychic perfections. Know that psychic perfections are one of three things: either life, or perception, or intellect, and each of these three divisions is possible here. As for the first possibility: that it is said: the aim of this motion is to preserve the power of life. Its exposition: that it is said: induction has shown that subtlety, transparency, luminosity, motion, the power of life, and intellect are concomitant things, either always or for the most part. As for density, stillness, death, and ignorance, they are concomitant things, either always or for the most part. Do you not see that since the heart is the fountain of life, it is necessarily always in motion? Likewise here, since the sphere is the fountain of life, it is necessarily always in motion. And since the life of the sphere is more perfect and nobler than these bodies, its motions are necessarily the noblest of motions, which are circular, free from disparity and difference, described with every order and system. As for the body of the Earth, since it is the mine of darkness and density, it is necessarily the mine of stillness and immobility. So death and stillness are concomitant, and life and motion are concomitant. And since life in the world of the spheres is more enduring and nobler, the motions there are more perfect and nobler.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage continues the division of perfections into physical and psychic, now focusing on the psychic. The author states that psychic perfections are three: life, perception, and intellect. He then elaborates on the first possibility: that the aim of motion is to preserve the power of life (quwwat al-haya). The proof is based on induction (istiqra') which shows that subtlety, transparency, luminosity, motion, life-power, and intellect are always or mostly concomitant; conversely, density, stillness, death, and ignorance are concomitant. An analogy is drawn with the heart: as the fountain of life, it is always in motion. Similarly, the celestial sphere (falak) as the fountain of life is always in motion. Since the sphere's life is more perfect and noble, its motions are the noblest—circular, uniform, and well-ordered. The Earth, being dense and dark, is the mine of stillness. Thus, motion and life are necessarily linked, and stillness and death are linked. The conclusion is that the eternal life of the celestial spheres necessitates their perfect circular motions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this passage is trying to prove the sphere's life, but the sphere's life is already assumed; the argument is about the necessary connection between life and motion.
Correction: The author is not proving that the sphere lives, but rather that if it lives (which it does), its motion must be as described—constant, circular, and perfect.

Checkpoint

A) They are sometimes concomitant
B) They are mostly or always concomitant
C) They are never concomitant
D) They are independent

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the celestial sphere's perpetual circular motion is a necessary expression of its perfect life, supported by induction and analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says life and motion are concomitant. Your heart moves constantly, but does your spiritual life move you toward Allah with the same constancy? Where in your life does spiritual stillness—neglect of prayer, heedlessness—indicate a lack of true life? What one action will you take today to revive that motion?
?
The sphere's motion is perfectly ordered, free from disparity. Look at your own actions today: where is there disorder, inconsistency, or neglect? Does your worship reflect a life that is 'noble' and 'enduring'? Commit to correcting one area of inconsistency between now and your next prayer.
?
Razi uses induction: light, subtlety, and motion go together. Examine your daily habits: are they 'luminous'—guided by knowledge and sincerity—or 'dense' with heedlessness and routine? What would it take for you to infuse your ordinary tasks with the light of intention and awareness of Allah?

The Causal Relationship Between Motion and Life is Unknown
Declares the unknowability of the causal link between motion and life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the causal relationship between motion and life is rationally unknown, and why this epistemological limit matters for theological arguments about celestial motions.

Core Idea
The connection between motion and life is beyond rational determination.

In this passage, the author presents a crucial limitation of human reason: we cannot determine whether motion causes life, life causes motion, or both arise from a separate cause. This serves as an important caveat before discussing the purposes of celestial motions, reminding us that some causal relations lie beyond our knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three options listed. Consider why the author mentions these specific alternatives.
The Arabic uses 'أو' to link the three possibilities.
2
Understand the conclusion of unknowability
The author states 'that is not known' (فذلك غير معلوم). Recognize that this is a definitive claim about the limits of human knowledge. Ask: why would the author assert this?
This is a statement of epistemological humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البحث عن أن الحركة الموجبة للحياة، أو الحياة هي الموجبة للحركة، أو هما معلولا علة منفصلة فذلك غير معلوم
As for investigating whether motion is the cause of life, or life is the cause of motion, or both are effects of a separate cause, that is not known.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage states that certain causal relationships are unknowable. It lists three possibilities regarding motion and life: motion causes life, life causes motion, or both are effects of a separate cause. The author asserts that which of these is the case is unknown. This serves as a caveat before discussing the purposes of celestial motions.
