Key Takeaway
The causal link between motion and life is not known to human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the exact causal link between motion and life is not known to human reason. Think of a moment when you assumed you knew the exact cause of something in your life—your health, your success, a relationship. How does this lesson's humility about uncertainty challenge your natural tendency to claim certainty? Will you let that uncertainty drive you to trust the One who knows all causes, or will you cling to your assumptions?
?
The lesson presents three possibilities for the relationship between motion and life. Which of those possibilities feels most true to your experience? And are you willing to admit that your preference might be nothing more than a personal inclination, not a certainty? If you had to live without knowing which is true, would that disturb your faith? Examine yourself honestly.
?
This passage declares that something as fundamental as the link between movement and life is beyond our knowledge. How does that affect your understanding of your own life? Are there areas where you insist on knowing 'why' something happened—a trial, a blessing, a delay—when this lesson suggests you cannot know the hidden causal connections? What would it cost you to simply say 'I do not know' and leave it to Allah?

Celestial Motions for Acquiring Knowledge
Explains the second possible purpose for celestial motions: acquisition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second possible purpose for celestial motions according to Ibn al-Khatib: the acquisition of endless knowledge and happiness.

Core Idea
The spheres move endlessly to acquire endless knowledge.

Having considered one possible purpose for celestial motions, the author now presents another: that the spheres move to acquire and perfect knowledge. This explanation draws on an analogy with human thought processes, and links the infinite nature of knowledge to the perpetual motion of the heavens.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Try to grasp the overall argument before breaking it down.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice how the author draws an analogy between human knowledge acquisition and that of celestial souls. Humans use thought and cerebral movements; celestial souls use their own motions to attain knowledge.
The key point is that both involve specific movements to gain knowledge.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that this explanation is plausible and more reasonable than astrological interpretations. Reflect on why the infinite nature of knowledge necessitates endless motion.
The endlessness of knowledge is linked to the endlessness of celestial motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثاني: وهو أن يقال: المقصود للأفلاك من تلك الحركات: اكتساب العلوم والمعارف، والمبالغة في تكميلها . فهذا أيضاً محتمل. وتقريره: إنا نعلم أن الإنسان حال ما يحاول تحصيل المعارف الكسبية ، فإنه يستعين بالفكر والتأمل ، وذلك لا يمكن إلا بتحريك الأرواح الدماغية على وجوه مخصوصة . وإذا كان ذلك كذلك ، لم يمتنع أيضاً أن يقال: إن مراتب العلوم غير متناهية . واللذة الحاصلة من تحصيل العلوم والمعارف : أقوى اللذات وأكمل السعادات . فهذه النفوس الفلكية مستغرقة بتحصيل هذا النوع من السعادات ، وأنه لا يمكنها تحصيل هذا المطلوب إلا بواسطة هذه الحركات المخصوصة ، فكانت مواظبة الأفلاك على هذه الحركات ، لأجل أن يتوصل بها إلى استفادة المعارف والعلوم. ولما كان لا نهاية للعلوم والمعارف ، فلا جرم لا نهاية الحركاتها ، لأجل أنه تحصل الوصلة بها ، إلى تحصيل تلك المطالب. فهذا احتمال ممكن ، وهو أقرب إلى العقول من استخراج الأيون والأوضاع .
As for the second possibility: that it is said: the purpose for the spheres of those movements is the acquisition of sciences and knowledges, and exaggeration in perfecting them. This too is probable. Its explanation: We know that when a human attempts to acquire acquired knowledges, he uses thought and reflection, and that is only possible by moving the cerebral spirits in specific ways. If that is so, it is not impossible to also say that the ranks of sciences are endless. And the pleasure obtained from acquiring sciences and knowledges is the strongest of pleasures and the most perfect of happiness. So these celestial souls are absorbed in obtaining this type of happiness, and they cannot obtain this desired goal except through these specific movements. Thus the spheres' adherence to these movements is in order to attain knowledge and sciences through them. And since there is no end to sciences and knowledges, inevitably there is no end to their movements, because the connection is obtained through them to attain those goals. This is a possible probability, and it is closer to intellects than extracting [al-ayun?] and positions.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the second possible purpose for the motions of the celestial spheres: the acquisition and perfection of knowledge. The author first states the possibility, then explains it by analogy with human cognition. Humans rely on thought and the movement of cerebral spirits to gain knowledge. Similarly, the celestial souls, which have greater capacities, derive immense pleasure from endless knowledge. They require specific motions to attain that knowledge. Since knowledge is infinite, their motions must be perpetual. The author concludes that this is a plausible explanation, more reasonable than certain astrological interpretations (extracting al-ayun and positions).

The Astrological Argument for Celestial Motions
First sub-argument: astrological evidence for seeking perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how astrological concepts of planetary exaltations, debilities, friendships, and enmities are used to argue that celestial motions seek perfection in intellectual power.

Core Idea
The planets' specific properties and relationships reveal a pursuit of increased power and ability.

This lesson presents the first sub-argument for the third probability—that celestial motions aim at intellectual perfection. According to the astrologers, the detailed classifications of planets and their interactions suggest that their movements are driven by a desire to acquire greater perfection in power and capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage on the astrological argument once, then read the translation. Notice the various astrological terms used.
Terms like sharaf (exaltation) and hubut (debility) are technical.
2
List the astrological categories
Identify the different categories mentioned: exaltation, debility, house, triplicity, terms, faces, etc. Understand that each planet has specific associations.
These are standard concepts in medieval astrology.
3
Understand the relationships
Note that astrologers claim friendship and enmity between planets, and each planet assists or opposes others in their actions.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The passage concludes that planetary actions are for seeking increases in power and ability. Ask yourself how the mentioned properties support this.
The argument is that specificities require a purpose, and that purpose is perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثالث: وهو أن تكون حركاتها لطلب الكمال في القوة العقلية فهذا الاحتمال ممكن . وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : وهو قول أصحاب الأحكام. وهو أنه ثبت أن للكواكب في هذه المدارات خصوصيات مختلفة. فبعض تلك البروج لها شرف ، وبعضها هبوط . وكذا القول في البيت والمثلثة والحدود والوجوه والفرج وسائر الأقسام المذكورة في علم الأحكام. وايضاً : فإن الأحكاميون ذكروا أن بين تلك الكواكب صداقة وعداوة ، وأن كل واحد منها يحاول إعانة غيره على فعله تارة ، وينازعه في فعله أخرى . وعلى هذا التقدير فلا يمتنع أن تكون أفعالها ، لأجل طلب تلك الزوائد في القوة والقدرة . الوجه الثاني : قالوا : لا شك أن واجب الوجود لذاته : المبدأ المفيض لجميع أقسام الخير والرحمة ، على جميع أجزاء العالم الروحاني والجسماني . ولا شك أن الحركات الفلكية : أسبابا لنظام هذا العالم . وعند هذا قالوا : « كمال حال الممكنات التشبه بالإله بقدر الطاقة البشرية ، ولهذا المعنى قال صاحب الشريعة : « تخلقوا بأخلاق الله تعالى ، ونقل عن أوائل الفلاسفة أنهم قالوا : الفلسفة عبارة عن التشبه بالإله بقدر الطاقة البشرية ، ولا شك أن هذا التشبه حالة عالية شريفة . ولما كانت الأفلاك أكمل حالاً من البشر ، في علومها ومعارفها ، وأنواع فضائلها ، كانت أولى بتحصيل هذا التشبه ، بقدر ما يليق بها من الطاقة . فهي تتحرك حركات مشتملة على تحصيل مناظم هذا العالم الأسفل . إلا أن مقصودها الأصلي ليس هو العناية بهذه السافلات ، بل مقصودها الأصلي : هو التشبه بالإله تعالى في كونه مبدأ الحصول النظام والخير والرحمة . الوجه الثالث : إنا بينا : أن الفلك حيوان . وهو مطيع لمعبوده ولخالقه . والعبادة عبارة عن مجموع أمور ثلاثة : الاعتقاد بالقلب . والذكر باللسان. والخدمة بالبدن . فالنفس الكلية لما كانت مواظبة على عبادة خالقها ومعبودها - وبدنها هو الفلك - كانت هذه الحركات جارية مجرى الركوع والسجود الله سبحانه وتعالى . فهذا هو القول في تعديد الوجوه الممكنة في أن الغرض للفلك من الحركة : استفادة الكمالات والسعادات من الأسباب العالية
As for the third possibility: that their movements are for seeking perfection in intellectual power, this possibility is possible. And its explanation is from aspects: First: it is the saying of the astrologers. It is established that the planets in these orbits have different specificities. Some of those signs have exaltation, some have debility. Likewise regarding house, triplicity, terms, faces, and [al-faraj?] and the rest of the divisions mentioned in the science of astrology. Also, the astrologers mentioned that between those planets there is friendship and enmity, and each one attempts to help another in its action sometimes, and contends with it in its action at other times. On this supposition, it is not impossible that their actions are for seeking those increases in power and ability. Second aspect: They said: There is no doubt that the Necessary Existent in itself is the principle that bestows all kinds of good and mercy upon all parts of the spiritual and physical world. And there is no doubt that the celestial movements are causes for the order of this world. And on this they said: 'The perfection of the state of contingents is resemblance to God according to the capacity of human power.' And for this meaning the possessor of the Law said: 'Assume the character traits of God Almighty,' and it is transmitted from the early philosophers that they said: 'Philosophy is an expression of resemblance to God according to the capacity of human power.' And there is no doubt that this resemblance is a high and noble state. And since the spheres are more perfect in state than humans in their sciences and knowledges and types of virtues, they are more entitled to obtain this resemblance, according to what befits their capacity. So they move with movements that include the ordering of this lower world. However, their primary intention is not care for these lowly things, but their primary intention is resemblance to God Almighty in that He is the principle of obtaining order, good, and mercy. Third aspect: We have explained that the sphere is an animate being. And it is obedient to its worshipped and its Creator. And worship is an expression of the sum of three things: belief in the heart, remembrance with the tongue, and service with the body. So when the universal soul is persistent in worshipping its Creator and Worshipped, and its body is the sphere, these movements are like bowing and prostrating to God, May He be exalted and glorified. This is the saying in enumerating the possible aspects that the purpose of the sphere from movement is: the acquisition of perfections and happiness from the high causes.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the third possible purpose for celestial motions: seeking perfection in intellectual power. It gives three sub-arguments. The first is from the astrologers: planets have specific exaltations, debilities, friendships, and enmities, and their motions seek increases in power. The second is from the concept of tashabbuh bi-l-ilāh (resemblance to God). The Necessary Existent is the source of all good, and the spheres imitate Him by being causes of order in the lower world, though their primary aim is not the lower world but resemblance. The third is from worship: the sphere is an animate body that obeys God; its motions are like the bodily acts of worship (bowing and prostrating). The conclusion summarizes that these are possible aspects for the sphere's purpose: acquiring perfections and happiness from high causes.

The Argument from Resemblance to God (Tashabbuh)
Second sub-argument: celestial motions as imitation of God's role as bestower of order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that celestial motions aim at resembling God (tashabbuh bi-l-ilāh) by being causes of order and goodness, rather than solely for the benefit of the lower world.

Core Idea
The spheres imitate God by bestowing order and mercy, seeking resemblance as their primary goal.

This lesson presents the second sub-argument for the third probability. Drawing on the principle that the perfection of contingent beings lies in resembling God, the author argues that the celestial spheres—being more perfect than humans—are even more entitled to seek such resemblance. Their motions order the lower world, but their primary intention is to emulate God's role as the source of all good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on resemblance
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the premises about the Necessary Existent and the spheres' superiority.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the statement: 'the perfection of contingents is resemblance to God according to capacity.' Note the supporting quotes from the Prophet and philosophers.
This is a central idea in Islamic philosophy.
3
Understand the application to spheres
The spheres are more perfect than humans, so they are more entitled to this resemblance. Their motions bring order to the lower world, but that is not their primary goal.
4
Distinguish primary and secondary intention
The passage emphasizes that the spheres' primary intention is resemblance to God, not care for the lower world. This distinction is crucial.
The lower world benefits as a consequence.

وأما الاحتمال الثالث: وهو أن تكون حركاتها لطلب الكمال في القوة العقلية فهذا الاحتمال ممكن . وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : وهو قول أصحاب الأحكام. وهو أنه ثبت أن للكواكب في هذه المدارات خصوصيات مختلفة. فبعض تلك البروج لها شرف ، وبعضها هبوط . وكذا القول في البيت والمثلثة والحدود والوجوه والفرج وسائر الأقسام المذكورة في علم الأحكام. وايضاً : فإن الأحكاميون ذكروا أن بين تلك الكواكب صداقة وعداوة ، وأن كل واحد منها يحاول إعانة غيره على فعله تارة ، وينازعه في فعله أخرى . وعلى هذا التقدير فلا يمتنع أن تكون أفعالها ، لأجل طلب تلك الزوائد في القوة والقدرة . الوجه الثاني : قالوا : لا شك أن واجب الوجود لذاته : المبدأ المفيض لجميع أقسام الخير والرحمة ، على جميع أجزاء العالم الروحاني والجسماني . ولا شك أن الحركات الفلكية : أسبابا لنظام هذا العالم . وعند هذا قالوا : « كمال حال الممكنات التشبه بالإله بقدر الطاقة البشرية ، ولهذا المعنى قال صاحب الشريعة : « تخلقوا بأخلاق الله تعالى ، ونقل عن أوائل الفلاسفة أنهم قالوا : الفلسفة عبارة عن التشبه بالإله بقدر الطاقة البشرية ، ولا شك أن هذا التشبه حالة عالية شريفة . ولما كانت الأفلاك أكمل حالاً من البشر ، في علومها ومعارفها ، وأنواع فضائلها ، كانت أولى بتحصيل هذا التشبه ، بقدر ما يليق بها من الطاقة . فهي تتحرك حركات مشتملة على تحصيل مناظم هذا العالم الأسفل . إلا أن مقصودها الأصلي ليس هو العناية بهذه السافلات ، بل مقصودها الأصلي : هو التشبه بالإله تعالى في كونه مبدأ الحصول النظام والخير والرحمة . الوجه الثالث : إنا بينا : أن الفلك حيوان . وهو مطيع لمعبوده ولخالقه . والعبادة عبارة عن مجموع أمور ثلاثة : الاعتقاد بالقلب . والذكر باللسان. والخدمة بالبدن . فالنفس الكلية لما كانت مواظبة على عبادة خالقها ومعبودها - وبدنها هو الفلك - كانت هذه الحركات جارية مجرى الركوع والسجود الله سبحانه وتعالى . فهذا هو القول في تعديد الوجوه الممكنة في أن الغرض للفلك من الحركة : استفادة الكمالات والسعادات من الأسباب العالية
As for the third possibility: that their movements are for seeking perfection in intellectual power, this possibility is possible. And its explanation is from aspects: First: it is the saying of the astrologers. It is established that the planets in these orbits have different specificities. Some of those signs have exaltation, some have debility. Likewise regarding house, triplicity, terms, faces, and [al-faraj?] and the rest of the divisions mentioned in the science of astrology. Also, the astrologers mentioned that between those planets there is friendship and enmity, and each one attempts to help another in its action sometimes, and contends with it in its action at other times. On this supposition, it is not impossible that their actions are for seeking those increases in power and ability. Second aspect: They said: There is no doubt that the Necessary Existent in itself is the principle that bestows all kinds of good and mercy upon all parts of the spiritual and physical world. And there is no doubt that the celestial movements are causes for the order of this world. And on this they said: 'The perfection of the state of contingents is resemblance to God according to the capacity of human power.' And for this meaning the possessor of the Law said: 'Assume the character traits of God Almighty,' and it is transmitted from the early philosophers that they said: 'Philosophy is an expression of resemblance to God according to the capacity of human power.' And there is no doubt that this resemblance is a high and noble state. And since the spheres are more perfect in state than humans in their sciences and knowledges and types of virtues, they are more entitled to obtain this resemblance, according to what befits their capacity. So they move with movements that include the ordering of this lower world. However, their primary intention is not care for these lowly things, but their primary intention is resemblance to God Almighty in that He is the principle of obtaining order, good, and mercy. Third aspect: We have explained that the sphere is an animate being. And it is obedient to its worshipped and its Creator. And worship is an expression of the sum of three things: belief in the heart, remembrance with the tongue, and service with the body. So when the universal soul is persistent in worshipping its Creator and Worshipped, and its body is the sphere, these movements are like bowing and prostrating to God, May He be exalted and glorified. This is the saying in enumerating the possible aspects that the purpose of the sphere from movement is: the acquisition of perfections and happiness from the high causes.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the third possible purpose for celestial motions: seeking perfection in intellectual power. It gives three sub-arguments. The first is from the astrologers: planets have specific exaltations, debilities, friendships, and enmities, and their motions seek increases in power. The second is from the concept of tashabbuh bi-l-ilāh (resemblance to God). The Necessary Existent is the source of all good, and the spheres imitate Him by being causes of order in the lower world, though their primary aim is not the lower world but resemblance. The third is from worship: the sphere is an animate body that obeys God; its motions are like the bodily acts of worship (bowing and prostrating). The conclusion summarizes that these are possible aspects for the sphere's purpose: acquiring perfections and happiness from high causes.

نظام
عالم سفلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The spheres' motions are solely for the benefit of the lower world.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that their primary intention is resemblance to God, and ordering the lower world is a secondary effect.

Checkpoint
According to the resemblance argument, what is the primary intention of the celestial spheres in their motions?
A) To provide for the needs of humans
B) To maintain the order of the lower world
C) To resemble God as a source of order and mercy
D) To increase their own physical power

Key Takeaway
The spheres' motions are primarily acts of imitating God's role as the source of order and goodness, not merely for the sake of the lower world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily activities, do you ever pause to ask whether your primary intention is to resemble God (in His goodness, mercy, and order) or simply to get things done? How would your day change if you consciously aimed at tashabbuh?
?
The passage says the spheres are ‘more perfect than humans’ and thus more entitled to resemblance. If you are less perfect than the spheres, what does that say about the sincerity of your efforts to imitate God? Are you satisfied with your current state?
?
You claim to believe God is the source of all good, yet how often do you take that truth into account when you act? If your actions do not ultimately aim at resembling Him, what are you really serving?

The Argument from Worship: Celestial Motions as Acts of Devotion
Third sub-argument: celestial motions as bodily worship (bowing and prostrating).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how celestial motions are interpreted as acts of worship (ʿibādah), specifically as bodily acts of service analogous to bowing and prostrating in human prayer.

Core Idea
The sphere's motion is its body's worship of the Creator, like bowing and prostrating.

This lesson presents the third sub-argument for the third probability. The author previously established that the sphere is a living being (ḥayawān). Now he argues that its motions constitute worship: just as human worship includes bodily service, the sphere's continuous motions are its form of bowing and prostrating to God. This ties the purpose of motion directly to spiritual devotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on worship
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note the definition of worship and the analogy to human prayer.
2
Recall the premise that the sphere is an animal
The author references an earlier proof that the sphere is a living being with a soul. This is essential for the argument.
See previous lesson for that proof.
3
Understand the three components of worship
Worship consists of belief in the heart, remembrance with the tongue, and service with the body. The sphere's motion corresponds to bodily service.
4
Connect motion to worship
The universal soul of the sphere is constant in worship; its body (the sphere) moves accordingly, analogous to the physical acts of bowing and prostrating in Islamic prayer.
5
Consider the concluding summary
The passage ends by stating that these three aspects are possible explanations for the sphere's purpose: acquiring perfections and happiness from high causes.

?

?

The Second Argument: Celestial Motion as Divine Imitation
The author presents the second argument: celestial motion as imitation of the Divine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second argument for celestial motion uses the concept of imitation of God to explain why the spheres move, and why their primary intention is divine imitation rather than care for the lower world.

Core Idea
The celestial spheres move primarily to imitate God, not just to order the lower world.

This lesson examines the second argument presented by the author for the motion of celestial spheres. It builds on the idea that all contingent beings seek perfection through imitating God, and since the spheres are more perfect than humans, they are even more entitled to this imitation. Let's explore how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two premises
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the first premise: the Necessary Existent is the source of all good and mercy. Second premise: celestial movements cause the order of this world.
These premises are taken as given without proof in this passage.
2
Understand the principle of imitation of God
The passage introduces a general principle: the perfection of contingent beings lies in imitating God to the extent of their capacity. This is supported by a prophetic saying and the definition of philosophy by early philosophers.
This principle is key to the argument.
3
Apply the principle to celestial spheres
Since the celestial spheres are more perfect than humans in knowledge and virtue, they are more entitled than humans to seek this imitation. Their movements are therefore acts of imitation.
Notice the hierarchy: spheres > humans in perfection.
4
Distinguish primary intention from apparent effect
The author clarifies that while celestial movements result in ordering the lower world, that is not their primary intention. Their primary intention is to imitate God in being a source of order, good, and mercy.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the teleology of celestial motion.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument ties celestial motion to divine attributes and provides a motive beyond mere physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : قالوا : لا شك أن واجب الوجود لذاته : المبدأ المفيض لجميع أقسام الخير والرحمة ، على جميع أجزاء العالم الروحاني والجسماني . ولا شك أن الحركات الفلكية : أسبابا لنظام هذا العالم . وعند هذا قالوا : « كمال حال الممكنات التشبه بالإله (۱) بقدر الطاقة البشرية ، ولهذا المعنى قال صاحب الشريعة : « تخلقوا بأخلاق الله تعالى ، ونقل عن أوائل الفلاسفة (٢) أنهم قالوا : الفلسفة [ عبارة عن التشبه بالإله بقدر الطاقة البشرية ، ولا شك أن هذا التشبه (۴) ] حالة عالية شريفة . ولما كانت الأفلاك أكمل حالاً من البشر ، في علومها ومعارفها ، وأنواع فضائلها ، كانت أولى بتحصيل هذا التشبه ، بقدر ما يليق بها من الطاقة . فهي تتحرك حركات مشتملة على تحصيل مناظم هذا العالم الأسفل . إلا أن مقصودها الأصلي ليس هو العناية بهذه السافلات ، بل مقصودها الأصلي : هو التشبه بالإله تعالى في كونه مبدأ الحصول النظام والخير والرحمة .
The second aspect: They said: There is no doubt that the Necessary Existent in essence is the principle effusing all kinds of good and mercy upon all parts of the spiritual and corporeal world. And there is no doubt that the celestial movements are causes for the order of this world. And upon this they said: 'The perfection of the state of possibles is the imitation of God (al-tashabbuh bi-l-ilāh) to the extent of human capacity, and for this meaning the possessor of the Law said: 'Acquire the character traits of God Almighty,' and it is transmitted from the early philosophers that they said: Philosophy is [an expression for imitation of God to the extent of human capacity, and there is no doubt that this imitation (al-tashabbuh)] is a lofty and noble state. And since the celestial spheres are more perfect in state than humans, in their sciences, knowledges, and types of virtues, they were more deserving of attaining this imitation, to the extent befitting their capacity. So they move with movements that include the arrangement of the systems of this lower world. However, their primary intention is not care for these lowly things, rather their primary intention is: imitation of God Almighty in His being the principle of obtaining order, good, and mercy.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) for why the celestial spheres move. It is a proof based on the concept of imitation of God (al-tashabbuh bi-l-ilāh). The argument begins with two premises: first, that the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) is the source of all good and mercy for all parts of the world, both spiritual and physical. Second, the movements of the celestial spheres are causes for the order of this world. From these, the author quotes a broader principle: the perfection of contingent beings lies in imitating God to the extent of their capacity. This is supported by a prophetic saying ('Acquire the character traits of God') and by a definition of philosophy from early philosophers as imitation of God. The author then argues that since the celestial spheres are more perfect than humans in knowledge and virtue, they are more entitled to seek this imitation. Their movements are indeed involved in ordering the lower world, but their primary goal is not care for the lower world; rather, it is to imitate God in being a source of order, good, and mercy. The passage thus distinguishes between the apparent effect (ordering the lower world) and the ultimate intention (divine imitation).

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
التشبه بالإله
الفلك
الطاقة البشرية
الخير والرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author denies that celestial motions benefit the lower world.
Correction: The passage clarifies that benefit is not the primary intention, though it occurs incidentally.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what is the primary intention of the celestial spheres' movements?
A) To care for the lower world
B) To imitate God in being a source of order and good
C) To acquire virtues from the Throne
D) To worship through prostration

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that celestial spheres move primarily to imitate God's generosity, not merely to arrange the lower world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of kindness or service, what is your primary intention? Is it to imitate God's generosity, or is it for recognition, habit, or personal satisfaction? How can you shift your intention to mirror the celestial spheres' example of putting imitation of God above all else?
?
The passage says that even the noble task of ordering the lower world is not the spheres' primary goal. In your life, are there good deeds you do that have become ends in themselves rather than acts of divine imitation? What specific action can you take today to reorient your purpose towards God?
?
If the celestial spheres, with their perfect knowledge and virtues, still make imitation of God their primary intention, what does that say about your own priorities? Are you content with merely performing religious duties, or do you actively strive to manifest God's attributes of mercy and order in your character? What is one attribute of God you will consciously practice this week?

Celestial Motion as Worship
Definition and proof: celestial motion as worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that celestial motion is an act of worship by identifying the celestial sphere as an animal and defining worship as a threefold act.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's motion is bowing and prostration to God.

The third argument presents a unique perspective: the celestial sphere is not just a physical object but a living animal obedient to its Creator. By defining worship as belief, remembrance, and bodily service, the author equates the sphere's motion to the ritual acts of bowing and prostration. This lesson explores this intriguing argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: it begins with an assertion, defines a term, and concludes.
2
Identify the three components of worship
Find where worship is defined. Note the three elements: belief in the heart, remembrance with the tongue, and service with the body.
These are the standard components of worship in Islamic theology.
3
Understand the analogy
Observe how the author connects the universal soul's constant worship to the celestial sphere's motion, calling it bowing and prostrating. Reflect on how this frames celestial motion as an act of devotion.
The analogy is meant to show purpose in motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إنا بينا : أن الفلك حيوان . وهو مطيع لمعبوده ولخالقه . والعبادة عبارة عن مجموع أمور ثلاثة : الاعتقاد بالقلب . والذكر باللسان. والخدمة بالبدن (4) . فالنفس الكلية لما كانت مواظبة على عبادة خالقها ومعبودها - ويـدنها هو الفلك - كانت هذه الحركات جارية مجرى الركوع والسجود الله سبحانه وتعالى . فهذا هو القول في تعديد الوجوه الممكنة في أن الغرض للفلك من الحركة : استفادة الكمالات والسعادات من الأسباب العالية
The third aspect: We have explained that the celestial sphere is an animal. And it is obedient to its worshipped and its Creator. And worship is an expression of a totality of three things: belief in the heart, remembrance with the tongue, and service with the body. So since the universal soul is constant in worship of its Creator and its worshipped – and its body is the celestial sphere – these movements run in the course of bowing and prostrating to God, glory be to Him and exalted. So this is the statement in enumerating the possible aspects concerning that the purpose for the celestial sphere from movement is: gaining perfections and felicities from the high causes.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thālith) for the purpose of celestial motion. It is a definition-and-proof passage: the author first asserts that the celestial sphere (al-falak) is an animal (ḥayawān) – meaning it has a soul – and that it is obedient to its Creator. Then worship (al-‘ibāda) is defined as the combination of three things: belief in the heart, remembrance with the tongue, and service with the body. The author identifies the universal soul (al-nafs al-kulliyya) as the one constant in worship, and its body is the celestial sphere. Therefore, the movements of the sphere are analogous to the bodily acts of worship in humans, specifically bowing (rukū‘) and prostration (sujūd) to God. The conclusion is that the purpose of the sphere's movement is to gain perfections and felicities from high causes. The passage thus integrates the concept of celestial motion into the framework of religious worship.

Key Terms
الفلك حيوان
العبادة
النفس الكلية
الركوع والسجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the celestial sphere is just a lifeless object and cannot engage in worship.
Correction: The author operates within a philosophical cosmology where the celestial sphere has a soul (universal soul) and is thus an animal capable of obedience and worship.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, the movements of the celestial sphere are comparable to which human acts?
A) Reading and writing
B) Bowing and prostrating
C) Fasting and charity
D) Thought and reflection

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere's motion is an act of worship, specifically bowing and prostration, because it is the bodily expression of the universal soul's constant devotion to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The universal soul is described as constant in worship. Your soul is not constant—it wavers. What is one distraction or attachment that pulls your soul away from that constancy, and what will you sacrifice to remove it starting tomorrow?

Celestial Motion for Care of the Lower World
The author presents the view that celestial motion aims to care for the lower world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view on celestial motion: that its purpose is to care for the lower world by conveying forces from the Throne and Footstool.

Core Idea
Celestial motion channels noble forces from the Throne to the lower world.

The author now presents the second major position on the purpose of planetary movements. Some thinkers held that the seven planets move in order to care for the sublunar realm. This lesson explains how they acquire forces from above and transmit them below.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
Notice the structure: a claim, then a mechanism, then an expansion.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by stating the second division: the purpose of celestial motions is care for the lower world. Identify who holds this view and what 'care' means in this context.
3
Understand the mechanism
The explanation involves ascent to apogees to acquire forces from the Throne and Footstool, then descent to perigees to convey those forces to the elemental world. Visualize this cycle.
The terms 'apogee' and 'perigee' refer to farthest and closest points in orbit relative to Earth.
4
Reflect on the inclusion
Note the final statement: the individuals of the elemental world are included in this division. Ask yourself why this addition is important.
It emphasizes that all sublunar beings benefit, not just the world as a whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : قول من يقول : غرضها من هذه الحركات : العناية بالسافلات. وهذا قول ذهب إليه عالم من الناس . الذين قالوا : إن الكواكب السبعة إذا صعدت إلى أوجاتها ، استفادت القوى الشريفة من العرش والكرسي ، فإذا نزلت إلى حضيضاتها أدت تلك القوى إلى العالم العنصري [ وأفراد العالم العنصري ) ] داخلون في هذا التقسيم
The second division: The saying of one who says: Their purpose from these movements is care for the lower things. And this is a saying held by a group of people. They said: When the seven planets ascend to their apogees, they acquire noble forces from the Throne and the Footstool, and when they descend to their perigees, they convey those forces to the elemental world. And the individuals of the elemental world are included in this division.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the second major position (al-qism al-thānī) on the purpose of celestial motions. It is a classification: the author identifies a group of people who hold that the purpose is care for the lower world (al-‘ināya bi-l-sāfilāt). The explanation provided is that the seven planets (al-kawākib al-sab‘a) ascend to their apogees (awjātihā) and acquire noble forces from the Throne (al-‘arsh) and the Footstool (al-kursī). Then when they descend to their perigees (ḥaḍīḍātihā), they convey those forces to the elemental world (al-‘ālam al-‘unṣurī). The author adds that the individuals of the elemental world are included in this division, meaning that the benefit extends to all sublunar beings. This is a straightforward statement of a teleological view based on planetary motion.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this view makes the heavens subordinate to the lower world, implying the lower is higher in purpose.
Correction: The author merely reports the view without endorsing it. The heavenly bodies are still considered noble, but their motion serves a caring function for the lower realm.

Checkpoint

B) From the Throne and the Footstool
C) From the universal soul
D) From their own essence

Key Takeaway
The second view attributes planetary motion to a caring function: acquiring forces from the Throne and Footstool at apogee and delivering them to the elemental world at perigee.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The planets ascend to acquire forces and descend to deliver them. In your daily life, when do you ascend (seek spiritual or noble forces) and when do you descend to serve those around you? Are you intentional about conveying what you receive, or do you keep it for yourself?
?
If the very movement of celestial bodies is for the care of the lower world, what does your own movement—your comings and goings, your daily routines—accomplish? Is your life oriented toward care for what is lower and needy, or merely toward your own elevation?
?
You are part of the 'individuals of the elemental world' that benefit from this celestial care. How does it change your sense of humility and gratitude to know that forces from the Throne and Footstool are channeled to sustain you? Will you live today as a grateful recipient or as an entitled consumer?

The Third View: Motion for Self-Perfection and Care for Lower World
The author presents a composite view on celestial motion: self-perfection by high things and care for lower world through directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third view on celestial motion, which combines self-perfection by high things and care for the lower world through the directions of motion.

Core Idea
Celestial motion serves both upward perfection and downward care.

After presenting two previous views on the purpose of celestial motion, the author now introduces a third, composite view. This view distinguishes between the movement's essential aim—self-perfection through higher realities—and its specific directions, which are arranged for the benefit of the sublunar realm. The passage also attributes this view to the philosopher Thāra Masṭīyūs, likely a historical figure. Let's examine how this synthesis works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the two distinct parts: what the movement is for, and what the directions are for.
The passage is a single sentence with two clauses.
2
Identify the two aspects
Identify the purpose of the movements themselves (self-perfection by high things) and the purpose of their directions and modalities (care for the lower world).
These are explicitly contrasted with 'أما' (as for).
3
Consider the attribution
Note that this view is attributed to Thāra Masṭīyūs. Think about why the author might include this attribution.
Thāra Masṭīyūs is likely a transliteration of a Greek philosopher, possibly Thales.
4
Reflect on the synthesis
Consider how this view combines both a higher aim (self-perfection) and a lower aim (care for the lower world) in one theory.
This is a composite view that reconciles two possible purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو [ قول من يقول (۲) ] : إن وصول الحركات ، لأجل الاستكمال بالعاليات. وأما جهاتها وكيفياتها ، فللعناية بالسافلات . وهذا قول اختاره ( ثار مسطيوس )
And as for the third division: It is [the saying of one who says]: That the arrival of the movements is for the sake of self-perfection by the high things. And as for their directions and modalities, they are for the care of the lower things. And this is a saying chosen by Thāra Masṭīyūs.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the third division (al-qism al-thālith) on the purpose of celestial motion. It is a classification: the author attributes to an unnamed source (or some people) a composite view. According to this view, the movement itself (wuṣūl al-ḥarakāt) is for the sake of self-perfection (al-istikmāl) by high things (al-‘āliyāt), meaning the celestial spheres seek perfection from higher causes. However, the specific directions (jihātuhā) and modalities (kayfiyyātuhā) of the movements are for the care of the lower world (al-‘ināya bi-l-sāfilāt). This view thus combines both purposes: the primary intention is self-perfection, but the detailed motion is arranged to benefit the sublunar realm. The author notes that this saying was chosen by someone named Thāra Masṭīyūs, likely a reference to a philosopher (possibly Thales of Miletus?). The passage is a brief report of a position.

Key Terms
الاستكمال بالعاليات
جهاتها وكيفياتها
العناية بالسافلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This view is the same as one of the previous views (e.g., motion only for self-perfection or only for care of lower world).
Correction: It is a distinct synthesis that assigns different aspects of motion to two different purposes: the movement itself to self-perfection, and its specific directions to care for the lower world.

Checkpoint
According to the third division, what is the purpose of the celestial movements themselves?
A) Care for the lower world
B) Self-perfection by high things
C) Worship of God
D) Random motion

Key Takeaway
The third view proposes that celestial motion aims at self-perfection by high things while its specific directions serve to care for the lower world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a passage that divides the purpose of celestial motion into self-perfection and care for the lower world. Now consider your own daily actions: do you ever consciously align the direction and quality of your actions (how you do them) with the intention of serving others, while also pursuing your own growth? Or do you see these as separate? Write down one action you will take today that combines both purposes.
?
The third view suggests that even the highest celestial motions are not solely for their own perfection but also for the benefit of the lower world. How does this challenge your assumption that your personal spiritual pursuits are private? Are you willing to examine whether your acts of worship or study are arranged (in their details) to care for those around you? Commit to a specific change.
?
If the arrival of your movements (the fact that you act at all) is for your own perfection, but how you move is for the care of others, then your entire life is meant to be integrated. Look at your schedule this week: are there any actions that serve only one purpose? How can you adjust them to be like the celestial motion described here? Be concrete.

An Odd View: Worlds of Light Outside the Outermost Sphere
The author reports an unusual theory about worlds of light beyond the outermost sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an unusual cosmological theory about luminous worlds beyond the outermost sphere and its proposed role in explaining celestial motion.

Core Idea
Even strange theories reflect serious attempts to explain celestial motion.

This lesson explores a curious theory recorded by the author. It suggests that beyond our visible universe lie worlds of light, and that the outermost sphere moves to draw perfection from them. While unusual, this view represents a genuine attempt to account for celestial motion by positing external luminous sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by calling this a 'strange saying' (قول عجيب).
The author introduces it as an additional view, not his own.
2
Identify the key components
Identify three parts: what lies outside the outermost sphere (worlds of light), the nature of those lights (different in perfection), and the purpose of the sphere's motion (to acquire those perfections).
3
Reflect on the cosmological implications
Consider why someone might propose such a view. How does it explain celestial motion? What does it imply about the cosmos beyond the visible spheres?
This theory expands the universe beyond the traditional nine spheres.
4
Consider the author's stance
Notice that the author simply reports this view without endorsing it. What does this tell you about the author's method?
He is documenting diverse opinions on the topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المسألة قول آخر عجيب : وهو أن خارج الفلك الأقصى عوالم مملوءة من النور ، وتلك الأنوار مختلفة في درجات الكمال. فالفلك الأقصى يتحرك ليستفيد الكمالات المختلفة ، والقوى المتباينة من تلك الأنوار .
And on the issue there is another strange saying: That outside the outermost sphere there are worlds filled with light, and those lights differ in degrees of perfection. So the outermost sphere moves to acquire the different perfections and diverse forces from those lights.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents an additional, unusual position (qawl ākhar ‘ajīb) on the purpose of celestial motion. It is a brief report. The view posits that outside the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aqṣā) there exist worlds (awālim) filled with light (nūr). These lights are not uniform but differ in degrees of perfection. The outermost sphere moves in order to acquire these various perfections and diverse forces from those external lights. This theory expands the cosmological framework beyond the familiar cosmos, suggesting a source of influence from beyond the visible universe. The author does not endorse it; he simply records it as one of the possible sayings on the topic.

Key Terms

عوالم مملوءة من النور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This theory is pure fantasy with no serious intent.
Correction: Although unusual, it reflects a serious attempt to explain celestial motion by positing external luminous sources as the cause of motion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The outermost sphere moves to acquire perfection from external lights. What is the ultimate source of perfection you are moving toward? Are you actively seeking it, or are you passively drifting? Name one concrete step you will take today to draw closer to that perfection.

The Second Division: Celestial Motions for Caring for the Lower World
Rāzī presents the second division's view on celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second division's explanation that celestial motions aim to care for the sublunary world by transferring noble powers from the Throne and Footstool.

Core Idea
Planets acquire powers at apogee and deliver them to earth at perigee.

Rāzī continues his classification of views on the purpose of celestial motions. The second division holds that the motions are solely for the benefit of the lower world. This view involves a specific mechanism: planets gain spiritual powers from the highest realms and then convey them downward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify key elements
Notice the claim: purpose is caring for the lower. Then the mechanism: planets ascend to acquire powers, descend to deliver.
Identify the terms: Throne and Footstool.
3
Reflect on the view
Consider why this view is classified as 'care for the lower'. How does it explain the observed motions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : قول من يقول : غرضها من هذه الحركات : العناية بالسافلات. وهذا قول ذهب إليه عالم من الناس . الذين قالوا : إن الكواكب السبعة إذا صعدت إلى أوجاتها ، استفادت القوى الشريفة من العرش والكرسي ، فإذا نزلت إلى حضيضاتها أدت تلك القوى إلى العالم العنصري [ وأفراد العالم العنصري ) ] داخلون في هذا التقسيم وأما القسم الثالث : وهو [ قول من يقول (۲) ] : إن وصول الحركات ، لأجل الاستكمال بالعاليات. وأما جهاتها وكيفياتها ، فللعناية بالسافلات . وهذا قول اختاره ( ثار مسطيوس ) وفي المسألة قول آخر عجيب : وهو أن خارج الفلك الأقصى عوالم مملوءة من النور ، وتلك الأنوار مختلفة في درجات الكمال. فالفلك الأقصى يتحرك ليستفيد الكمالات المختلفة ، والقوى المتباينة من تلك الأنوار . فهذا ضبط الأقوال الممكنة في هذا الباب
The second division: the saying of one who says: their purpose from these motions is caring for the lower things. And this is a saying held by a group of people who said: when the seven planets ascend to their apogees, they acquire noble powers from the Throne and the Footstool, and when they descend to their perigees, they convey those powers to the elemental world. And individuals of the elemental world are included in this division. As for the third division: it is the saying of one who says: the occurrence of motions is for the sake of perfecting through the higher things. And in this matter there is another strange saying: that outside the outermost sphere there are worlds filled with light, and those lights differ in degrees of perfection. So the outermost sphere moves to acquire different perfections and diverse powers from those lights. So this is a recording of the possible sayings in this chapter.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on the purpose of celestial motions. The text presents two main divisions and then a third odd opinion. The second division states that the purpose of these motions is caring for the lower (sublunary) world: the planets acquire noble powers from the Throne and Footstool when at apogee and then impart them to the elemental world when at perigee. The third division, attributed to Thar Masṭiyūs, combines two purposes: the motions themselves aim at perfection from higher beings, while their directions and qualities aim at caring for the lower. Finally, an additional strange opinion is mentioned: outside the outermost sphere there are luminous worlds varying in perfection, and the outermost sphere moves to acquire different perfections from them. The passage concludes that this is an exhaustive enumeration of possible views on the topic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you face hardship, do you ever feel that the universe is indifferent, or that celestial movements have abandoned you? How can the truth that even the planets are tasked with caring for the lower world reshape your certainty that Allah's care never ceases?

The Third Division: Combining Perfection and Care
Rāzī presents the third division with its dual purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division's combined view that celestial motions aim at perfection from higher beings while their directions care for the lower world.

Core Idea
Motions perfect the mover; directions care for the lower.

Rāzī now presents the third division, which attributes a dual purpose to celestial motions. The motion itself is for the sake of perfection from higher entities, while the specific directions and qualities of the motion are for the benefit of the lower world. This view is attributed to Thar Masṭiyūs.

Read the passage

3

The second division states that the purpose of these motions is caring for the lower (sublunary) world: the planets acquire noble powers from the Throne and Footstool when at apogee and then impart them to the elemental world when at perigee. The third division, attributed to Thar Masṭiyūs, combines two purposes: the motions themselves aim at perfection from higher beings, while their directions and qualities aim at caring for the lower. Finally, an additional strange opinion is mentioned: outside the outermost sphere there are luminous worlds varying in perfection, and the outermost sphere moves to acquire different perfections from them. The passage concludes that this is an exhaustive enumeration of possible views on the topic.

D) Neither.

The third division distinguishes between the purpose of motion (perfection) and its directions (care for lower).

The Strange Saying: Motion for Acquiring from Luminous Worlds
Rāzī presents an additional strange opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strange saying that the outermost sphere moves to acquire perfections from luminous worlds beyond it.

Core Idea
The outermost sphere moves to gain perfection from external luminous worlds.

Finally, Rāzī mentions an additional strange opinion. It posits that outside the outermost sphere there are worlds of light varying in perfection. The outermost sphere moves to acquire different perfections from those luminous worlds, explaining celestial motion as a quest for divine light and perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim: there are luminous worlds outside the outermost sphere.
2
Understand the mechanism
The outermost sphere moves to acquire perfections and powers from those luminous worlds. This is a form of self-perfection.
3
Reflect on why it is called 'strange'
Consider what makes this view unusual compared to the previous ones. It posits an external source of perfection beyond the known cosmos.

Source Text

Refutation of Purposeless Voluntary Motions
Razi refutes the fifth view with two logical arguments against purposeless acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two logical arguments against the claim that celestial motions are voluntary but purposeless, and why such motions cannot be perpetual.

Core Idea
Purposeless voluntary acts cannot be perpetual and are logically impossible.

Razi has already refuted four views that celestial motions are natural. Now he turns to the fifth view: that the motions are voluntary but without any purpose. This view depends on a problematic premise—that an agent can act without any purpose. Razi presents two powerful arguments to show why this view fails, drawing on the principle of preference without a preponderant and the nature of occasional events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: a premise, then two arguments (اول and ثاني), and the beginning of an objection.
The passage is incomplete; the objection continues on the next page.
2
Identify the two arguments
The first argument is about the impossibility of preferring one equal possibility without a preponderant. The second argument is about occasional events not being perpetual or even frequent.
These arguments target the idea that an agent can act without purpose.
3
Consider the objection
The objection tries to counter the second argument by pointing to trivial human actions (like fidgeting with a hair) that appear purposeless but occur. Think about how Razi might answer this.
The objection is not fully addressed in this passage; the answer is on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الأولى : اعلم : أن الأقوال الأربعة المبنية على أن هذه الحركات : طبعية ، قد سلف إبطالها . وأما القول الخامس : وهو أنها إرادية ، إلا أنه ليس للأفلاك في تلك الحركات غرض معين . فاعلم : أن هذا بناء على أن القادر يمكنه أن يفعل فعلاً معيناً ، لا لغرض أصلاً . والكلام المستقصى في إبطاله [ قد سبق (۱) ] في باب والدواعي والصوارف (۳) ، ولا بأس بإعادة بعضها فنقول : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : إنها إذا كانت متساوية بالنسبة إلى ذلك الفاعل ، كان إقدامه على البعض دون البعض : رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . وهو محال . الثاني : أن بتقدير أن يكون ذلك جائزاً ، إلا أنه أمر اتفاقي . والأمــور الاتفاقية ، لا تكون دائمة ولا أكثرية [ فوجب أن لا تكون هذه الحركات دائمة ولا أكثرية (۳) ] فإن قيل : الحركات العبثية كثيرة . مثل : عبث الإنسان بشعرة
The first question: Know that the four sayings based on these motions being natural have already been refuted. As for the fifth saying: that they are voluntary, except that the spheres have no specific purpose in those motions. So know that this is based on the premise that the capable agent can perform a specific act without any purpose at all. And the detailed discussion on its refutation [has already preceded] in the chapter on motives and deterrents, and it is not inappropriate to repeat some of it, so we say: This is invalid from two aspects: The first: that if they are equal with respect to that agent, then his undertaking of some rather than others would be a preference of one of the two possible alternatives over the other without a preponderant, which is impossible. The second: that even if that is allowed, it would be an occasional affair. And occasional matters are neither perpetual nor most of the time; [so it is necessary that these motions be neither perpetual nor most of the time]. If it is said: Trivial movements are numerous, such as a person's fidgeting with a hair
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the fifth view which claims that celestial motions are voluntary but without a specific purpose. The author states that the four previous views based on natural motions have already been refuted. The fifth view assumes that a capable agent can act without any purpose. The author refers to a previous detailed refutation but repeats part of it here. The proof consists of two arguments: First, if all possible actions are equal for the agent, then choosing one over another would be a preference without a preponderant, which is impossible. Second, even if such an act were possible, it would be an occasional occurrence, but occasional events are not perpetual or even frequent. Since celestial motions are perpetual, the fifth view fails. The passage then begins to address an objection about trivial human actions (like fidgeting with a hair), but the text cuts off. The objection is that such trivial actions are indeed voluntary and without purpose, yet they occur. The answer is not included in this page.

Two Proofs Against Purposeless Celestial Movements
Al-Razi states the fifth opinion and refutes it with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two kalam arguments that refute the claim that celestial movements are voluntary but without purpose.

Core Idea
Accidental movements cannot be perpetual; preference without cause is impossible.

Al-Razi has already refuted four opinions that celestial movements are natural. Now he tackles the fifth: that they are voluntary but purposeless. He offers two concise but powerful arguments, each grounded in the fundamental principles of kalam. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fifth opinion
Read the passage and note the claim being refuted: that celestial movements are voluntary but without any specific purpose.
This is the target of the refutation.
2
Understand the first proof: preference requires a preponderant
Al-Razi argues that if the agent has no reason to prefer one movement over another, choosing one is a preference without a preponderant, which is impossible.
This is a standard kalam principle.
3
Understand the second proof: accidental cannot be perpetual
Even if arbitrary choice were allowed, the result would be accidental, and accidental matters are not perpetual. Celestial movements are perpetual, so they cannot be accidental.
This is a direct observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الأولى : اعلم : أن الأقوال الأربعة المبنية على أن هذه الحركات : طبعية ، قد سلف إبطالها . وأما القول الخامس : وهو أنها إرادية ، إلا أنه ليس للأفلاك في تلك الحركات غرض معين . فاعلم : أن هذا بناء على أن القادر يمكنه أن يفعل فعلاً معيناً ، لا لغرض أصلاً . والكلام المستقصى في إبطاله [ قد سبق (۱) ] في باب و الدواعي والصوارف (۳) ، ولا بأس بإعادة بعضها فنقول : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : إنها إذا كانت متساوية بالنسبة إلى ذلك الفاعل ، كان إقدامه على البعض دون البعض : رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . وهو محال . الثاني : أن بتقدير أن يكون ذلك جائزاً ، إلا أنه أمر اتفاقي . والأمــور الاتفاقية ، لا تكون دائمة ولا أكثرية [ فوجب أن لا تكون هذه الحركات دائمة ولا أكثرية (۳) ]
The first question: Know that the four opinions based on that these movements are natural have already been refuted. As for the fifth opinion—that they are voluntary, except that the celestial spheres have no specific purpose in those movements—know that this is based on that the capable agent can perform a specific act with no purpose at all. The comprehensive discussion of its refutation has preceded in the chapter of motives and deterrents, and there is no harm in repeating some of it. So we say: This is invalid from two aspects. The first: If they are equal with respect to that agent, then his proceeding with some to the exclusion of others would be a preference of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderant, which is impossible. The second: Even if that were permissible, it would be an accidental matter. And accidental matters are not perpetual nor majority. So these movements must not be perpetual nor majority.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two refutations (the two wajhain) against the fifth opinion regarding the movements of the celestial spheres. The author, al-Razi, states the fifth opinion: that the movements are voluntary (irādiyyah) but without any specific purpose (gharaḍ) for the spheres. He explains that this opinion is based on the premise that a capable agent (al-qādir) can perform a specific act with no purpose at all. Al-Razi then gives two arguments to show this is false. First argument: if the movements are equally possible for the agent, then choosing one specific movement over another would be a preference (tarjīḥ) of one possibility without a preponderant (murjīḥ), which is impossible—this is a standard kalam principle that every preference requires a preponderant. Second argument: even if such arbitrary choice were possible, the resulting movement would be accidental (ittifāqī), and accidental occurrences cannot be perpetual (dā’imah) or even majority (akthariyyah). Since the celestial movements are observed to be perpetual (e.g., daily rotations), they cannot be accidental. Thus the fifth opinion is refuted.

Key Terms
إرادية
غرض
مرجح
اتفاقي
دائمة
أكثرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can act arbitrarily without any reason, but kalam holds that even for God, preference without a preponderant is impossible. Also, one might think accidental movements can be regular, but al-Razi denies this.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that purposeless voluntary action leads to either an impossible preference without cause or an accidental nature incompatible with the observed perpetual regularity.

Checkpoint

A) It would require a preference without a preponderant.
B) Such movements would be accidental, but accidental matters are not perpetual.
C) The celestial spheres are inanimate and cannot have will.
D) The movements are natural, not voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the fifth opinion by showing it leads to impossible consequences: preference without a preponderant and accidental movements that cannot be perpetual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever perform actions—like prayers, work, or conversations—without a conscious purpose, simply out of habit or impulse? Al-Razi argues that preference without a preponderant is impossible. Will you pause today to examine one action and ask yourself: 'What is my real purpose here, and is it worthy of a rational agent?'
?
The celestial spheres move perpetually with purpose, but your own life's movements—your daily routines, your studies, your interactions—are they driven by a consistent purpose or are they scattered and accidental? What one change can you make tomorrow to ensure your actions are not arbitrary but aligned with a higher goal?

Al-Razi refutes the idea that voluntary actions can be purposeless. When you worship Allah, do you sometimes do so mechanically, without a present heart or intention? Answering Objections on Vain Actions, Sleep, and Hunger
Al-Razi refutes objections that human vain actions disprove the need for purpose in voluntary acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that every voluntary act, even seemingly vain ones, necessarily has a specific purpose based on a belief in benefit.

Core Idea
Even vain actions have a purpose: the benefit of the act itself.

Al-Razi's argument that voluntary acts require a purpose faces three clever objections: vain actions, sleep movements, and a hungry person's choice of a side of bread. These seem to be voluntary yet purposeless. In this lesson, you will see how al-Razi refutes all three by analyzing the mechanism of voluntary action—showing that every movement is driven by a belief in some benefit, however trivial.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and three examples
Carefully read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objector presents three cases: vain play with a hair or straw, the sleeper's turning, and the hungry person's choice. Notice how each is claimed to be voluntary without a purpose.
The objector is trying to find counterexamples to al-Razi's general claim.
2
Identify al-Razi's answer structure
Al-Razi begins his answer by explaining the hierarchy of the moving power (quwwa muḥarrika). Trace his steps: proximate power, need for decisive inclination (mayl jāzim), and the role of belief (iʿtiqād) in a benefit.
He distinguishes between the power in muscles (equal opposites) and the added inclination that tips the balance.
3
Understand the role of belief and benefit
Al-Razi emphasizes that the belief does not have to be true—even a false or trivial belief can generate inclination. The benefit may be base (pleasure) or noble. Connect this to the vain action example.
This is key: the 'purpose' can be as simple as enjoyment of the act itself.
4
Apply the analysis to each objection
See how al-Razi's general explanation applies to each of the three objections. For vain actions, the benefit is the pleasure or satisfaction. For sleep movements, there is a minimal benefit (e.g., comfort). For the hungry person, the specific side is chosen due to a subtle belief (e.g., ease).
Al-Razi implicitly covers all three, though the text explicitly addresses only 'عبث' (vain actions). The analysis extends.
5
Grasp the conclusion: every movement has a specific goal
Al-Razi concludes that each voluntary act has a specific goal (ghāyah) determined by the specific belief and will. Hence the objections fail.
This reinforces his earlier argument that all voluntary actions require a purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الحركات العبثية كثيرة . مثل : عبث الإنسان بشعرة واحدة من شعرات لحيته ، وعبثه بتبنة يأخذها من الطريق من غير غرض صحيح ، ويعبث بها. فهذه حركات إرادية ، مع أنه ليس فيها أغراض صحيحة . وأيضاً : النائم ينقلب من أحد جنبيه إلى الثاني ، فتلك الحركة : حركة إرادية ، وليس فيها أغراض معينة . لأن النوم ضد العلم ، وعند عدم العلم ، لا يبقى الغرض . وأيضاً : الجائع إذا وضع عنده رغيف ، فإنه يبتدىء بكسر جانب معين منه ، دون سائر الجوانب . لا لغرض يتعلق بذلك الجانب بعينه . والجواب : أما العبث . فنقول : إن القوة المحركة لها مراتب : فالمرتبة القريبة هي القوة المركوزة في الأوتار والعضلات : للفعل ولضده ، وما دامت هذه الصلاحية من الجانبين باقية ، امتنع أن تصير مصدراً للفعل المعين . فإذا انضاف إليها ميل جازم ، حصل الرجحان . وذلك الميل مرتب على أن يعتقد الإنسان أن له في ذلك الفعل مصلحة ومنفعة . فإذا حصل هذا الاعتقاد ، حصل الميل . وليس من شرط ذلك الاعتقاد : أن يكون مطابقاً [ بل سواء كان مطابقاً (۱) ] أو لم يكن . فإنه إذا حصل الاعتقاد الجازم ، ترتب عليه الميل . وأيضاً : فتلك المنفعة قد تكون منفعة خسيسة ، وهي نفس التذاذه بذلك الفعل ، وقد تكون منفعة شريفة تحصل بها آثار كاملة ، ونتائج شريفة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المقدم على الفعل العبثي إنما يقدم عليه ، لو انضاف إلى القوة العضلية تلك الإرادة المترتبة على ذلك الاعتقاد . ومعلوم أن ذلك الاعتقاد يكون مقدراً بمقدار معين، وتلك الإرادة أيضاً محدودة بحد معين فمجموع تلك القدرة مع تلك الإرادة ، ومع ذلك التصور ، يوجب تلك الحركة المقدرة بالمقدار المخصوص . فذلك المجموع له في ذلك الفعل غاية معينة [ وهي الانتهاء إلى ذلك الحد المعين . فالحركة حصلت لها ههنا غاية معينة ](٢)
If it is said: Vain actions are many. Like a person playing with a single hair of his beard, and his playing with a straw he takes from the road without a true purpose, and he fiddles with it. These are voluntary movements, yet they have no true purposes. Also, the sleeper turns from one side to the other, so that movement is a voluntary movement, and it has no specific purposes, because sleep is opposite to knowledge, and in the absence of knowledge, purpose does not remain. Also, the hungry person when a loaf is placed before him, he begins to break a specific side of it, not the other sides, with no purpose related to that particular side. The answer: As for vain action, we say: The moving power has ranks. The proximate rank is the power embedded in the tendons and muscles, for the act and its opposite. As long as this capability from both sides remains, it is impossible for it to become a source of the specific act. When a decisive inclination is added to it, preference occurs. That inclination is consequent upon the person believing that he has a benefit and advantage in that act. When this belief occurs, the inclination occurs. It is not a condition of that belief that it be true; rather, whether it is true or not. For when the decisive belief occurs, the inclination follows. Also, that benefit may be a base benefit, which is his very pleasure in that act, or it may be a noble benefit by which perfect effects and noble results are obtained. If you know this, we say: The one who undertakes a vain action only undertakes it if that will which is consequent upon that belief is added to the muscular power. It is known that that belief is measured by a specific measure, and that will is also limited by a specific limit. So the total of that power with that will and that conception necessitates that movement measured by that specific measure. So that totality has in that act a specific goal, which is reaching that specific limit. So the movement has here a specific goal.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage addresses objections to the earlier argument that voluntary acts require a purpose. The objector gives three examples: vain actions like playing with a hair or straw, the movements of a sleeper, and a hungry person's act of breaking a specific side of a loaf. The objector claims these are voluntary without any specific purpose. Al-Razi answers by analyzing the structure of voluntary action. He explains that the moving power (al-quwwah al-muḥarrikah) has ranks. The proximate rank is the power in tendons and muscles, which is equally capable of the act and its opposite. For a specific act to occur, a decisive inclination (mayl jāzim) must be added, which brings about preference (tarjīḥ). This inclination depends on the agent's belief (i‘tiqād) that there is some benefit (maṣlaḥah) or advantage in the act. Importantly, the belief does not have to be true; even a false belief can produce inclination. The benefit may be base (like mere pleasure) or noble. In vain actions, the benefit is the pleasure or satisfaction of the act itself, or merely the cessation of some internal state. Thus each voluntary act has a specific goal (ghāyah), namely reaching the specific limit determined by the belief and will. Hence the objections are refuted: even seeming vain actions have purposes, albeit trivial ones. Al-Razi also implies that the sleeper's movement is not fully voluntary without belief, but the response focuses on establishing a purpose for each case.

Key Terms
عبث
قوة محركة
ميل جازم
اعتقاد
مصلحة
غاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vain actions like playing with a hair or straw have absolutely no purpose or benefit at all.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that such actions always involve at least the benefit of pleasure or satisfaction, even if trivial, and thus have a specific goal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's answer, what condition must be met for a voluntary movement to occur?
A) The agent must have a true belief about a benefit.
B) The agent must have a decisive inclination based on a belief in some benefit.
C) The agent must have a specific purpose that is noble.
D) The agent must be fully conscious and aware of the goal.

Key Takeaway
Every voluntary act, even those appearing vain, necessarily has a specific purpose grounded in the agent's belief in some benefit, however trivial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you regularly do that you consider trivial or pointless—like fidgeting, scrolling social media, or repeatedly checking your phone. According to this lesson, what belief about benefit (even if false) is driving that action? Are you willing to honestly examine that belief today and choose a more noble purpose instead?
?
Al-Razi states that the belief behind an action does not have to be true for it to produce an inclination. How many of your daily decisions are based on beliefs that you have never questioned? Pick one area of your life (finances, relationships, worship) and commit to investigating whether the belief you act on is truly benefiting you or just comfortable.
?
This lesson shows that even base pleasures count as benefits that give purpose to actions. When you look back on your last week, what proportion of your voluntary acts were aimed at base pleasures versus noble, lasting benefits? If the balance is skewed, what concrete change can you make tomorrow to tip it toward the noble?

Answer Regarding the Sleeper's Movements
The author explains that the sleeper acts based on subconscious desire or aversion, but forgets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even a sleeper's movements are based on an imagined purpose, though forgotten after waking.

Core Idea
A sleeper's actions are not purposeless; they stem from subconscious desire or aversion.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: if all actions require a purpose, how can sleep movements be explained? He argues that even sleepers act based on an imagined good or evil, but the imagination is forgotten upon waking. Thus, all voluntary movements, even in sleep, have a causal basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's claim about the sleeper's imagination.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that unless the sleeper imagines something desirable or undesirable, he does nothing. This is the core causal link.
3
Understand the objection and reply
The objection is that we don't remember after waking. The author replies that this does not prove the imagination didn't occur at the time.
Memory failure is not evidence of absence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Even unconscious or forgotten purposes can be real causes. This strengthens the principle that all voluntary actions have a purpose.
This aligns with the Ash'ari view on causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النائم فنقول : إنه ما لم يتخيل النائم مكروهاً أو مطلوباً [ لم يترك شيئاً ولم يفعل شيئاً . وأقصى ما في الباب : إنا بعد الانتباه لا نتذكر ذلك المطلوب وذلك المهروب ] إلا أنه لا يلزم من حصول ذلك التخيل في ذلك الوقت ، علمنا بعد النوم بأنه كان ذلك التخيل حاصلا في ذلك الوقت
As for the sleeper, we say: Unless the sleeper imagines something undesirable or desirable, [he does not leave anything nor do anything. The most that can be said is that after awakening we do not remember that desired thing or that fled thing] but it does not follow from the occurrence of that imagination at that time that we know after sleep that that imagination was occurring at that time.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
In response to the objection that sleep movements have no purpose, the author argues that even the sleeper acts only after imagining something desirable or undesirable. The fact that we do not remember after waking does not prove that no such imagination occurred. Thus, the sleeper's movement is also based on an imagined purpose, albeit unconscious.

?
