?
When you wake up in the morning, do you ever reflect on why you moved or turned during the night? This passage suggests that even those movements were tied to a desire or aversion. How does this change your view of your own subconscious—do you trust that even your 'random' actions are following a purposeful pattern set by your deeper inclinations?

The Hungry Person's Subconscious Reason
Author argues for a subconscious reason behind an apparently random choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even seemingly random choices have a subconscious cause according to the author.

Core Idea
Every choice has a reason, even if it disappears instantly from the mind.

We often make choices that seem arbitrary, like picking a side of a loaf of bread. The author argues that such choices are not truly random; there is a fleeting mental reason. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states the objection, then gives his response.
The objection claims the choice has no specific purpose.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the author says that without imagining something suitable, the particular side would not be chosen. This is the core of the argument.
The key term is 'ترجيح' (weighting/preference).
3
Consider the examples given
The author lists three possible reasons: tastiness, suitability, or closeness. Notice these are all plausible but often unconscious.
These examples show that a reason exists even if not articulated.
4
Reflect on the reason's disappearance
The author explains why the person cannot express the reason: it vanishes instantly. Consider how this fits with our daily experience of quick decisions.
Think of a time you made a quick choice without knowing why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : « الجائع يبتدىء بكسر الرغيف من أحد الجوانب ، لا لغرض معين ، فنقول : إنه ما لم يتخيل أمراً صالحاً لذلك الترجيح ، لم يحصل ذلك التعين . وهو كونه ألذ وأوفق أو كونه أقرب . أو شيء آخر ، يكون حاصلا في الذهن إلا أنه يزول عن الذهن في الحال ، فلا يمكن التعبير عنه لهذا السبب .
As for his statement: 'The hungry person begins to break the loaf from one of the sides, without a specific purpose,' we say: Unless he imagines something suitable for that weighting, that specificity does not occur. It is being more delicious, more suitable, or closer, or something else that occurs in the mind but disappears from the mind instantly, so it cannot be expressed for this reason.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
The author responds to the example of the hungry person who picks a particular side of the bread. He argues that there must be some imagined reason—such as that side being tastier, more appropriate, or closer—that tips the balance. This reason occurs in the mind but vanishes instantly, so the person cannot articulate it. Thus, the action still has a purpose, however fleeting.

Key Takeaway
Even instantaneous choices have a mental cause, though it may be too fleeting to verbalize.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What does that imply about your responsibility to examine your own mind? Will you start asking Allah to help you uncover the hidden motives behind your actions?
?
The author says reasons can 'disappear from the mind instantly.' How many of your beliefs and actions are driven by forgotten reasons? Are you willing to slow down and question the 'automatic' parts of your life, knowing that they are not random but reflect something real within you? Take one habit and try to recall its original cause today.

First Argument: Celestial Bodies Cannot Serve Lower Beings
The author presents the first argument against the purpose of celestial motions being to improve lower beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument against the claim that celestial motions exist solely to benefit lower beings, and the response to an objection based on prophets and rulers.

Core Idea
Serving the lowly makes the servant lowlier, but celestial bodies are too noble for that.

This passage presents the first argument in a series refuting the idea that the motions of celestial bodies are only for the benefit of earthly beings. The author uses a logical analogy about servitude to show the absurdity of such a claim, and then addresses a common objection using the example of prophets and rulers. By the end, you'll see how the author affirms that celestial bodies have their own higher benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and objection
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a clear argument followed by an objection and a response.
2
Understand the servant analogy
The author claims that if someone's actions are mostly for others, that person is like a servant. If the served are lowly, the servant is even lower. This sets up the premise.
Celestial bodies are noble, lower beings are lowly.
3
See the objection from prophets
An interlocutor asks: Don't prophets serve their communities without being lower? The author replies that prophets have higher benefits besides serving, so the analogy doesn't hold.
4
Apply the principle to celestial bodies
The author then applies the same logic: celestial bodies have their own higher benefits (delight, happiness) and also benefit lower beings as a secondary consequence.
The author endorses Themistius's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في الرد على من يقول : المقصود من تلك الحركات : إصلاح هذه السافلات : فنقول : الذي يدل على فساد هذا القول وجهين) : الحجة الأولى : إن كل من كان أكثر أفعاله لرعاية حال الغير ، كان كالخادم لذلك الغير. والخادم أخس من المخدوم . ثم إذا كان المخدوم خسيساً ، كان خادمه بالغاً إلى النهاية القصوى في الخساسة . وأشرف موجودات هذا العالم العنصري، هو في نهاية الضعف، في علومه ، وفي أفعاله الفاضلة فجعل تلك الأجسام العالية المقدسة الشريفة خادمة لهذه الأجسام الخسيسة ، مما لا يقبله العقل . فإن قيل : أليس المقصود من النبي رعاية مصالح الأمة [ ومن السلطان رعاية مصلحة الرعية (۱) ] ومن الراعي رعاية الغنم . ولم يلزم كون النبي أدون حالاً . من الأمة . فكذا ههنا ؟ قلنا : لا نسلم أنه لا فائدة في وجود النبي إلا رعاية مصالح الأمة ، بل في وجوده فوائد عالية رفيعة (٢) فإنه لأجل نبوته تستكمل ذاته بالمعارف القدسية، والأنوار الإلهية . من جملتها : إرشاد الخلق إلى الحق. ونظيره في مسألتنا : أن يقال : إن للأفلاك في حركاتها : فوائد كثيرة . ومن جملتها : مصالح السافلات. وبهذا التقدير [ سيندفع السؤال) ] وهذا هو القول الذي اختاره : ثامسطيوس : وهو أن الفلك يستفيد من المبادىء العالية أنواعاً من البهجة والسعادة ، ثم إنه يختار الجهة المعينة ، لكونها أنفع للمسافلات . وذلك عين (4) المذهب الذي نحن فيه الآن
The second issue in refuting those who say: The purpose of those motions is the improvement of these lower beings. So we say: What indicates the corruption of this statement is two aspects:
The first argument: Whoever's most of his actions are for tending to the condition of others is like a servant to that other. And the servant is lower than the served. Then if the served is lowly, his servant reaches the utmost extreme in lowliness. And the most noble existent of this elemental world is at the utmost level of weakness in its sciences and its virtuous actions. So making those high, sacred, noble bodies servants to these lowly bodies is something reason does not accept.
If it is said: Is not the purpose of a prophet tending to the interests of the community, and of a sultan tending to the interests of the subjects, and of a shepherd tending to the sheep? And it does not follow that the prophet is lower in station than the community. So likewise here?
We say: We do not accept that there is no benefit in the existence of the prophet except tending to the interests of the community; rather, in his existence there are high, elevated benefits. For by his prophecy his self is perfected with sacred knowledges and divine lights. Among them is guiding creation to the truth. The analogue in our issue is: It is said that for the spheres, in their motions, there are many benefits; among them are the interests of the lower beings. With this estimation the question is repelled. This is the view chosen by Themistius: that the sphere benefits from the high principles types of delight and happiness, then it chooses the specific direction because it is more beneficial to the lower beings. And that is the very doctrine we are now in.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument in the refutation of the claim that celestial bodies move only to improve lower beings (sāfilāt). The author begins by stating the second issue (al-masʾala al-thāniya) and then gives two lines of proof. The first hujja is: anyone whose actions are mostly for the sake of others becomes like a servant, and a servant is inferior to the served. If the served is lowly, the servant is extremely low. The noblest things in this elemental world (i.e., humans) are already weak; to make the celestial bodies (high, sacred, noble) servants to lowly earthly bodies is rationally unacceptable. An objection is raised: prophets, sultans, and shepherds serve the interests of others yet are not considered inferior. The answer (qulnā) is that the benefit of a prophet is not limited to serving the community; his own self is perfected by sacred knowledge and divine lights. Similarly, celestial motions have many higher benefits for themselves (delight and happiness) and, as a consequence, also benefit lower beings. The author endorses the view of Themistius: the sphere gains joy from high principles and then chooses directions that are useful for lower beings, which is exactly the position being advocated.

Key Terms
سافلات
خادم
مخدوم
ثامسطيوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any action for another's benefit implies inferiority of the agent.
Correction: The author clarifies that if the agent also has higher benefits (like prophets' own perfection or celestial bodies' delight), then the service does not imply inferiority.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the analogy of the prophet and the community?
A) He denies that prophets care for the community.
B) He argues that prophets have higher benefits beyond serving others, and so do celestial bodies.
C) He states that prophets are actually inferior to their communities.
D) He claims the analogy is invalid because prophets are not celestial.

Key Takeaway
The celestial bodies move primarily for their own perfection, not solely to serve the lower world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you serve others—whether family, community, or in your profession—do you also seek your own spiritual perfection, or have you reduced yourself to a mere servant, neglecting your soul's growth? How can you emulate the prophet in combining service with self-elevation?
?
The author argues that celestial bodies move for their own delight and happiness, not merely to serve the lowly. Examine your daily routines: Are your actions driven primarily by a desire for your own closeness to God, or are you enslaved by the endless demands of the world? What one change can you make to reclaim your higher purpose?
?
If the noblest beings in the cosmos are not degraded by also benefiting lower beings, what does that say about your own acts of service? Are you willing to serve others from a place of strength and spiritual abundance, or do you serve from weakness, hoping to be validated? Begin today to root your service in your own connection with the Divine.

Second Argument: The Perfect Cannot Be Perfected by the Imperfect
Second argument and its objection with persuasive qualifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that the more perfect cannot be completed by the less perfect, and the objection raised against it, along with the author's note that the evidence is persuasive.

Core Idea
The perfect cannot be completed by the imperfect.

The author presents a second argument against the claim that celestial bodies act for the benefit of lower beings. He argues that if they did, they would be seeking completion from what is inferior, which is impossible. However, he acknowledges that this evidence is only persuasive, and he also records an objection that uses everyday analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage: 'The second argument: Whoever does an act for a purpose is completed by that purpose...' Understand the logical structure.
The argument is: agent acts for purpose → agent is completed by purpose. If celestial bodies act for lower beings, they are completed by inferior things, which is impossible.
2
Examine the objection
Read the objection: 'And one may say: Why is it not permissible...' Notice the examples of the sultan, master, and human benefiting from inferiors.
The objection challenges the universality of the principle, citing cases where the nobler benefits from the lower.
3
Note the author's remark
Read the final part: 'And know: These two proofs are without doubt from the category of persuasive...' Understand that the author considers the argument non-demonstrative.
The remark qualifies the force of the argument; it is not certain proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن كل من فعل فعلاً لغرض . فهو مستكمل بذلك الغرض . فلو فعلت الأجرام العالية أفعالها ، لمصالح السافلات [ لكانت مستكملة بهذه السافلات. ومعلوم أنها أشرف وأعلى من هذه السافلات (٥) ] فيلزم كون الأكمل مستكملاً بالأخس والأنقص . وهو محال . ولقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الكامل الشريف العالي، قد يستكمل في بعض الأحوال بالخسيس النازل ؟ ألا ترى أن السلطان قد ينتفع برعيته ، والمولى قد ينتفع بعبده ، والإنسان قد ينتفع بالخبز والماء ، مع أنه أشرف منهما .
واعلم : أن هذين الدليلين ، لا شك أنهما من باب الإقناعيات . إلا أنه ثبت أن [ الأرض (٦) ] بالنسبة إلى الأفلاك والكواكب [ العلوية ) ] كالمركز في ...
The second argument: Whoever does an act for a purpose is completed (mustakmil) by that purpose. So if the high bodies do their actions for the interests of the lower beings, [they would be completed by these lower beings. And it is known that they are nobler and higher than these lower beings.] Then it follows that the more complete is completed by the more inferior and deficient. And that is impossible.
And one may say: Why is it not permissible to say that the perfect, noble, high (thing) may in some states be completed by the lowly, descending (thing)? Do you not see that the sultan may benefit from his subjects, the master may benefit from his slave, and the human may benefit from bread and water, even though he is nobler than them?
And know: These two proofs are without doubt from the category of persuasive (iqnāʿiyyāt). However, it is established that the [earth] relative to the spheres and [celestial] stars is like the center in...
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) and a potential objection. The argument states: whoever does an act for a purpose is 'completed' (mustakmil) by that purpose. If celestial bodies acted for the benefit of lower beings, they would be seeking completion from what is inferior, implying the more complete is completed by the less complete, which is impossible (muḥāl). The objection (li-qāʾilin an yaqūl) asks why this is not permissible, noting examples like a sultan benefiting from subjects, a master from a slave, or a human from bread and water — where the nobler receives benefit from the lowly. The author then makes a general remark (waʿlam) that these two proofs are only persuasive (iqnāʿiyyāt), not demonstrative, and then begins to add a further point about the earth being like a center relative to the spheres, but the text cuts off. The objection is left unanswered in the preserved text, though the 'waʿlam' section may hint at the author's assessment.

Key Terms
مستكمل
أكمل
أنقص
إقناعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument proves that celestial bodies cannot benefit lower beings at all, or that the objection is answered completely in this passage.
Correction: The author acknowledges the argument is only persuasive, and the objection is recorded without an explicit answer in the preserved text.

Checkpoint
What is the core claim of the second argument?
A) Celestial bodies are not perfected by anything.
B) If celestial bodies acted for lower beings, they would be completed by inferior things, which is impossible.
C) Lower beings cannot receive benefit from celestial bodies.
D) The sultan and his subjects prove that the nobler can be perfected by the inferior.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that the perfect cannot be perfected by the imperfect, but the author considers it persuasive rather than demonstrative, and the objection remains unanswered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek benefit from something or someone 'below' you, do you sense that you are being completed? What does that reveal about your view of your own perfection?
?
The author calls his own argument 'persuasive' not certain. Are you ever honest enough with yourself to admit that some of your most cherished beliefs might only be persuasive, not certain? How would that change your humility?
?
If you truly believed that the perfect cannot be perfected by the imperfect, how would that transform your pursuit of status, approval, or achievement in this world?

The Argument from the Weakness of the Elemental World and Its Refutation
Razi presents an argument against celestial bodies acting for the sublunary world and replies to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that celestial bodies serving the weak elemental world makes them inferior, by drawing an analogy with the prophet and invoking Themistius' view.

Core Idea
Service does not diminish dignity; celestial bodies serve lower beings while pursuing their own perfection.

Razi presents an argument against the idea that noble celestial bodies act solely for the benefit of the weak elemental world. He then anticipates an objection based on the prophet analogy and provides a robust reply. This passage shows how philosophical theology handles apparent paradoxes of service and nobility.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial argument
Read the first sentence: the elemental world is weak, so making celestial bodies serve it is unreasonable. Notice the premise.
The argument assumes that servants should not be nobler than what they serve.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection using the prophet analogy. Understand the parallel being drawn: a prophet serves the community but is not inferior.
The objection challenges the assumption that serving implies inferiority.
3
Understand the reply
Read Razi's answer: the prophet's existence has higher benefits (his own perfection), similarly celestial movements have multiple benefits. Note the refutation.
The reply shows that the service is only one aspect of a greater good.
4
Consider Themistius' view
Themistius' view is invoked: the sphere benefits itself and then chooses directions beneficial for lower beings. Reflect on how this resolves the objection.
This view integrates self-perfection with service to others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا العالم العنصري، هو في نهاية الضعف، في علومه، وفي أفعاله الفاضلة فجعل تلك الأجسام العالية المقدسة الشريفة خادمة لهذه الأجسام الخسيسة، مما لا يقبله العقل. فإن قيل: أليس المقصود من النبي رعاية مصالح الأمة [ومن السلطان رعاية مصلحة الرعية] ومن الراعي رعاية الغنم. ولم يلزم كون النبي أدون حالاً من الأمة. فكذا ههنا؟ قلنا: لا نسلم أنه لا فائدة في وجود النبي إلا رعاية مصالح الأمة، بل في وجوده فوائد عالية رفيعة فإنه لأجل نبوته تستكمل ذاته بالمعارف القدسية، والأنوار الإلهية. من جملتها: إرشاد الخلق إلى الحق. ونظيره في مسألتنا: أن يقال: إن للأفلاك في حركاتها: فوائد كثيرة. ومن جملتها: مصالح السافلات. وبهذا التقدير سيندفع السؤال وهذا هو القول الذي اختاره ثامسطيوس: وهو أن الفلك يستفيد من المبادئ العالية أنواعاً من البهجة والسعادة، ثم إنه يختار الجهة المعينة، لكونها أنفع للمسافلات. وذلك عين المذهب الذي نحن فيه الآن.
This elemental world is in the utmost weakness in its sciences and its virtuous actions, so making those high, sacred, noble bodies servants to these base bodies is something that reason does not accept. If it is said: Is not the purpose of the prophet the welfare of the community [and of the sultan the welfare of the subjects] and of the shepherd the welfare of the sheep? And it does not necessitate the prophet being in a lower state than the community. So too here? We say: We do not concede that there is no benefit in the existence of the prophet except the welfare of the community; rather, in his existence there are high, lofty benefits—for by virtue of his prophethood his essence is perfected by holy knowledges and divine lights. Among them is guiding creation to the truth. The analogy in our issue is to say that the celestial spheres in their movements have many benefits, and among them are the interests of the lower beings. By this estimation the question is repelled. And this is the view chosen by Themistius: that the sphere benefits from the high principles in types of delight and happiness, then it chooses the particular direction because it is more beneficial for the lower beings. And that is exactly the doctrine we are now in.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and its refutation regarding whether celestial bodies act for the sake of the sublunary world. It begins by stating that the elemental world is weak, so it is unreasonable for noble celestial bodies to serve base ones. An objection is then raised using an analogy: a prophet benefits the community without being lesser than them. The answer explains that the prophet's existence has benefits beyond mere welfare, including his own perfection through divine knowledge. Similarly, celestial movements have multiple benefits, including but not limited to sublunary interests. Themistius' view is invoked: the sphere seeks its own perfection while also benefiting lower beings. Thus the objection is resolved.

Key Terms
العالم العنصري
الأجسام العالية
السافلات
ثامسطيوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If celestial bodies serve the lower world, they must be inferior.
Correction: Service does not imply inferiority; they serve while pursuing their own perfection, which is their primary goal.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the first argument that celestial bodies serving the weak elemental world is unreasonable?
A) The prophet analogy shows that a superior can serve an inferior without being inferior.
B) The celestial bodies are not actually serving.
C) The elemental world is not weak.
D) Themistius disagreed.

Key Takeaway
The celestial bodies' service to the lower world does not diminish their nobility, as they act for their own perfection while also providing benefits below.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of service that seem beneath your dignity—whether for family, community, or work—do you seek the higher spiritual benefits that God may be granting you through that service, as the prophet and celestial bodies did? What prevents you from seeing those benefits, and how can you change your perspective today?
?
This lesson teaches that even the noblest beings serve lower ones while pursuing their own perfection. Is there a relationship or duty in your life where you feel diminished by serving? Will you trust that God has hidden higher purposes in that role, and ask Him to show you how that service can perfect your soul?
?
The celestial spheres choose their direction because it benefits lower beings, yet they delight in their own perfection. Have you ever chosen to do something difficult for others while finding joy in your own growth? If not, what would it take for you to intentionally combine service with self-refinement, seeking both Allah's pleasure and your own elevation?

The Second Argument: The Agent Perfected by Its Purpose
Razi's second argument with an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against celestial bodies acting for lower purposes, and the objection that a superior being can benefit from an inferior one without contradiction.

Core Idea
If the perfect were perfected by the imperfect, that would be impossible—but is it always impossible?

Razi presents a second argument: any agent acting for a purpose is perfected by that purpose. If celestial bodies act for lower beings, they would be perfected by the lower—which contradicts their superiority. However, an objection arises using everyday examples like a sultan benefiting from subjects. This lesson unpacks both the argument and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the proof, then the objection introduced by 'ولقائل أن يقول'.
2
Trace the logical steps of the argument
Identify the premise (every agent acting for a purpose is perfected), the application (high bodies acting for lower interests), the conclusion (they would be perfected by lower), and the contradiction (they are superior, so the perfect is perfected by the imperfect).
The argument concludes that this is impossible (محال).
3
Analyze the objection
Note how the objection challenges the argument by giving counterexamples (sultan, master, man). Ask yourself whether these examples truly parallel the celestial case. Consider how the author might respond.
The answer to the objection is not given in this passage but appears in the course later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن كل من فعل فعلاً لغرض، فهو مستكمل بذلك الغرض. فلو فعلت الأجرام العالية أفعالها، لمصالح السافلات لكانت مستكملة بهذه السافلات. ومعلوم أنها أشرف وأعلى من هذه السافلات فيلزم كون الأكمل مستكملاً بالأخس والأنقص. وهو محال. ولقائل أن يقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الكامل الشريف العالي، قد يستكمل في بعض الأحوال بالخسيس النازل؟ ألا ترى أن السلطان قد ينتفع برعيته، والمولى قد ينتفع بعبده، والإنسان قد ينتفع بالخبز والماء، مع أنه أشرف منهما.
The second proof: Everyone who does an act for a purpose is perfected by that purpose. So if the high bodies did their actions for the interests of the lower beings, they would be perfected by these lower beings. And it is known that they are nobler and higher than these lower beings, so it follows that the more perfect is perfected by the baser and defective. That is impossible. And one may say: Why is it not permissible to say that the perfect, noble, high being may be perfected in some states by the base, low? Do you not see that the sultan may benefit from his subjects, and the master from his slave, and man from bread and water, even though he is nobler than them?
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against celestial bodies acting for sublunary interests. It states that any agent that acts for a purpose is perfected by achieving that purpose. If celestial bodies act for the sake of lower beings, they would be perfected by those lower beings, yet they are superior, making the perfect perfected by the inferior—an impossibility. An objection follows, citing examples like a sultan benefiting from subjects or humans from bread, where a nobler being benefits from a baser one without becoming base. The argument here only presents the objection; the answer is not given in the immediate text but is addressed later in the course.

الأخس
الأجرام العالية
السافلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that the second argument is false or invalid.
Correction: The objection is a challenge that will be addressed; it does not necessarily refute the argument. The examples may not be analogous because in those cases the superior's action is not solely for the inferior's benefit but also for its own.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the second argument if the high bodies acted for lower beings?
A) They would be perfected by lower beings, which is impossible.
B) They would become lower themselves.
C) They would not be perfected at all.
D) They would benefit without perfection.

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses the principle of perfection through purpose to show that celestial bodies cannot act for lower purposes, but the objection with everyday examples sets up a need for further refinement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument states that a perfect being cannot be perfected by a base purpose. In your own life, what purposes are you pursuing that you consider beneath your dignity as a human? How does realizing that these purposes cannot truly perfect you change your motivation? Will you abandon them for something higher?
?
The objection gives examples of sultan benefiting from subjects and human from bread—noble benefiting from base without losing nobility. Are there people or things you consider 'beneath' you from whom you could benefit without being diminished? What pride prevents you from receiving from them? How can you practice humility today by acknowledging your need for what is 'lower'?
?
This argument forces you to examine the ultimate goal of your actions. Is your life oriented toward a purpose that perfects you, or are you seeking completion in temporary, base things? If you had to die tonight, would your purpose have perfected you? What change must you make starting now?

Razi's Disproportion Argument
Razi critiques previous proofs and offers an argument from disproportion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument from disproportion against the idea that all celestial motions exist for the sake of the sublunary world.

Core Idea
The heavens are too majestic to serve man's meager accomplishments.

Razi acknowledges that the previous proofs are rhetorical, not demonstrative. He then presents a stronger argument based on the sheer disproportion between the grandeur of the celestial spheres and the insignificance of the earth and its inhabitants. This argument appeals to rational intuition rather than formal logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the shift from evaluating previous proofs to presenting a new argument.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has two clear parts: first, the admission that the previous proofs are rhetorical (إقناعيات); second, the new argument from disproportion. Mark where the shift occurs.
3
Analyze the new argument
List the celestial entities mentioned: the greatest sphere, sphere of fixed stars, fixed stars, seven spheres with planets, the sun. Note their described attributes (majesty, greatness). Then contrast with the earth (like a center point, negligible) and man (little knowledge and virtues). Ask yourself: what is the point of contrast?
The argument highlights a mismatch between cause and effect.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi says the mind strongly recoils (نفرة شديدة) from the claim that all this motion is for the interests of this world. Why does he find it so obviously unreasonable? Consider the principle of proportionality in rational thinking.
This is not a formal proof but an appeal to rational intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن هذين الدليلين، لا شك أنهما من باب الإقناعيات. إلا أنه ثبت أن الأرض بالنسبة إلى الأفلاك والكواكب العلوية كالمركز في الدائرة. وأنه لا يبقى لها وجود بالنسبة إليها. ويبعد في العقل: أن يقال: الفلك الأعظم على جلالته، وفلك الثوابت على عظمته، وجملة الثوابت على غاية جلالتها، وجميع الأفلاك السبعة مع ما فيها من السيارات الرفيعة، والشمس على غاية جلالتها، كلها دائمة الحركة والفعل لأجل مصالح هذا العالم، الذي أشرف أقسامه هو الإنسان. ثم إن حاصل الإنسان من العلم والفضائل ليس إلا القليل. فهذا في العقل نفرة شديدة عنه. والله أعلم بالحقائق.
And know that these two proofs are without doubt of the rhetorical type. However, it is established that the earth in relation to the celestial spheres and the higher planets is like the center in a circle. And that it has no existence in relation to them. And it is far from reason to say: The greatest sphere in its majesty, and the sphere of the fixed stars in its greatness, and the totality of the fixed stars in their utmost majesty, and all the seven spheres with what they contain of the high planets, and the sun in its utmost majesty—all of them are constantly in motion and action for the sake of the interests of this world, whose noblest part is man. Then the sum of what man attains of knowledge and virtues is only little. So the mind strongly recoils from this. And God knows best the realities.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage serves as a commentary on the two preceding proofs, acknowledging that they are rhetorical (إقناعيات) rather than demonstrative. The author then presents a stronger argument: the earth is insignificant relative to the celestial spheres, like a center point in a circle—it has virtually no existence compared to them. Given the immense grandeur of the spheres (the greatest sphere, the sphere of fixed stars, the seven planets, the sun), it is absurd to think that all of their constant motion is for the sake of a world whose highest part, man, attains only a small amount of knowledge and virtue. This disproportion makes the claim irrational.

Key Terms
الإقناعيات
الفلك الأعظم
فلك الثوابت
السيارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi dismisses the previous proofs as false or worthless.
Correction: Razi explicitly says they are 'لا شك أنهما من باب الإقناعيات' (without doubt of the rhetorical type), meaning they are persuasive but not demonstrative. He does not reject them outright but offers a more compelling argument.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the claim that all celestial motions are for sublunary interests to be far from reason?
A) Because the celestial bodies are too noble to serve the base world.
B) Because the earth is insignificant compared to the heavens, and man's achievements are meager.
C) Because the proofs are only rhetorical.
D) Because the earth is the center of the universe.

Key Takeaway
Razi's disproportion argument questions the rationality of geocentric teleology by pointing out the immense size and majesty of the heavens relative to the earth and man's limited achievements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the universe revolves around you, or do you acknowledge your tiny place in God's creation? How does this affect your humility?
?
When you consider the immense heavens constantly in motion, and that your own knowledge and virtue are so little, does your heart feel a 'recoil'? What does that recoil tell you about your need for God?
?
The passage says the mind strongly recoils from the idea that all this is for man's meager attainment. Does your own life reflect a belief that you deserve all of God's attention? How can you change that?

The First Proof: If the Goal Were Actualization, Motions Would Be Fastest
Razi introduces the third issue and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof against the view that celestial motions aim to actualize forms, and the beginning of a possible objection based on material limitation.

Core Idea
If the sphere's purpose were to actualize forms, it would do so instantly.

Razi considers the view that celestial motions aim to bring forms from potentiality to actuality as the weakest. He presents a logical proof: a capable agent would achieve its goal as quickly as possible. Since the sphere's motions are slow, the goal cannot be mere actualization. However, an objection arises: perhaps the sphere's matter only accepts a certain slow motion. The text cuts off before Razi's reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Note that the text is cut off at the end.
The passage is incomplete due to OCR issues; the full objection and answer are missing.
2
Identify Razi's main claim and proof
Razi introduces the third issue and gives the first proof. Understand the conditional argument: if the goal were actualization, the motions would occur in the fastest way; they don't, so the goal is not actualization.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
3
Examine the objection about material limitation
The objection suggests that the sphere's matter (hyle) only accepts a specific type of motion, which explains the slowness. Consider how this challenges the proof.
The text cuts off before Razi's response; we can infer he likely refutes this by noting the agent's power overcomes material limits.

The missing portion would complete the objection or provide the answer. Consider what a complete defense might look like.
This lesson is based on an incomplete text; future revisions may provide the full argument.

Commentary
This passage introduces the third issue of the chapter: refuting the view that celestial motions aim to actualize forms and positions. The author considers this view the weakest and most irrational. The first proof against it uses the principle of efficiency: a capable agent would actualize its purpose as quickly as possible. If the sphere's goal were to actualize forms, it would do so in a short time (e.g., one day), but in reality the motions take longer. Therefore, their purpose cannot be mere actualization. The objection suggests that the sphere's material nature limits it to slow motion, so the delay is not due to a different purpose but to material constraints.

Razi's First Argument Against Actualization in Celestial Motions
Razi introduces the issue and presents the first argument against the actualization view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument against the view that celestial motions aim to actualize potential positions, and how he counters a potential objection about the intrinsic nature of celestial matter.

Core Idea
The fastest path is proof of intention; celestial motions are slow, so actualization is not the goal.

Razi begins the third issue by declaring the view that celestial motions are for actualizing positions as the weakest and most irrational. He then presents a sharp logical argument: if actualization were the purpose, the motions would occur as quickly as possible, since a capable agent would not delay. But celestial motions are not the fastest — indeed they take eons. He anticipates an objection about the inherent limitation of celestial matter and turns it against the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument: a conditional proof followed by an objection and reply.
Notice the strong language: 'أضعف الوجوه وأبعدها عن العقل'.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Find the conditional statement starting with 'إنه لو كان مقصود الفلك...' and its conclusion. Understand why the author says the motion would need to be as fast as possible.
The key premise: a capable agent (qādir) does not delay achieving his purpose.
3
Examine the objection and reply
Read the objection about hyle (هيولى) only accepting a specific motion. Then see how Razi turns it into a counter-argument: if hyle necessitates that motion, then the motion is not intentional.
This shows Razi's skill in refutation: accepting the opponent's premise leads to their own downfall.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة في إبطال قول من يقول: المقصود للأفلاك من الحركات إخراج الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل: اعلم: أن هذا الوجه كأنه أضعف الوجوه وأبعدها عن العقل ثم الذي يدل على فساده وجوه: الأول: إنه لو كان مقصود الفلك من الحركة إخراج تلك الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل، لوجب أن تكون تلك الحركات واقعة على أسرع الوجوه. وهذا باطل فذاك باطل. وبيان الملازمة: أنه إذا كان مقصود فلك الثوابت من الحركة: إخراج الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل فلم لا يخرجها فلك الثوابت في مقدار اليوم الواحد. فإن القادر إذا أمكنه تحصيل مقصوده في زمان قليل، فإنه لا يعدل عنه ولا يوقف تحصيله على زمان طويل؟ فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هيولى كل فلك لا يقبل إلا ذلك النوع من الحركة. ولأجل أن تلك الهيولى لا تقبل إلا ذلك النوع من الحركة وجد ذلك النوع دون غيره؟ فتقول: فإذا جاز هذا، فقولوا: إن هيولى كل فلك مستلزمة لذاتها حصول ذلك النوع من الحركة. فلهذا السبب وقعت تلك الحركة، دون سائر الحركات. وعلى هذا التقدير، فيبطل كل ما ذكرتموه.
The third issue: In refuting the statement of those who say: The intended purpose for the celestial spheres from motions is the bringing out of the active entities (ayan) and positions (awda') from potentiality (quwwa) to actuality (fi'l). Know: That this aspect is as if it is the weakest of aspects and furthest from reason. Then that which indicates its corruption are aspects: The first: If the purpose of the celestial sphere from motion were the bringing out of those entities and positions from potentiality to actuality, then it would be necessary that those motions occur in the fastest manner. This is false, so that is false. And the explanation of the implication: If the purpose of the sphere of fixed stars from motion is the bringing out of the entities and positions from potentiality to actuality, then why does the sphere of fixed stars not bring them out within the amount of one day? For the capabble (qādir) if he can achieve his purpose in a short time, he does not turn away from it nor delay its achievement to a long time. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the hyle (hayūlā) of each sphere accepts only that type of motion? And because that hyle accepts only that type of motion, that type occurred to the exclusion of others? Then you say: If this is permissible, then say: The hyle of each sphere is, by its very essence, necessitating the occurrence of that type of motion. For this reason that motion occurred, to the exclusion of other motions. And on this assumption, everything you mentioned becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (ibṭāl) of a specific claim about the purpose of celestial motions. The author first states the issue and declares this view the weakest. He then presents the first proof, which is a conditional argument: if the purpose were actualization, motions would be fastest, but they are not, so the claim is false. He explains the implication by pointing out that a capable agent would achieve his purpose quickly. A possible objection is raised: the hyle of each sphere may only accept a specific type of motion, preventing speed. The answer is that if that is allowed, one could simply say the hyle necessitates that motion, which would eliminate the need for an intentional purpose, thus refuting the original claim.

Key Terms
إبطال
مقصود

حركات
إخراج الأيون والأوضاع
القوة إلى الفعل
هيولى
مستلزمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection about hyle is a valid defense of the actualization view.
Correction: Razi shows that if hyle necessitates a specific motion, then the motion is not intentional, which actually refutes the claim that the motion has a purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if the purpose of the celestial sphere's motion is to actualize positions, why should the motion be as fast as possible?
A) Because the sphere is powerful and can move quickly.
B) Because a capable agent would not delay achieving his purpose.
C) Because the hyle forces slow motion.
D) Because the sphere has no purpose.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument shows that if celestial motions were for actualization, they would be fastest; since they are not, the claim fails, and the hyle objection only supports a necessitarian view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pursue a goal, do you take the fastest route, or do you find yourself delaying? What does your delay say about the sincerity of your intention, especially in light of Razi's argument that a capable agent does not delay achieving his purpose?
?
Consider an area of your life where you make excuses like 'I can't do it faster because of my circumstances'—similar to the hyle objection. Are you hiding a lack of real intention behind perceived limitations? How would your life change if you truly intended the goal?
?
Razi's argument strips away excuses and focuses on the gap between claim and action. Where in your worship or obedience is there a gap between claiming to seek Allah's pleasure and the speed of your response? Will you commit to closing that gap today?

The Second Proof: Opposite Motions and the Necessity of No Motion
Razi argues that if opposite motions both actualize positions, no motion is preferred, contradicting observed motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's second proof uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that the opponent's claim about the purpose of motion leads to the negation of motion.

Core Idea
If all motions equally achieve the same goal, then no motion is preferred, contradicting observed motion.

In this short but powerful argument, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a second proof against the claim that the purpose of celestial motion is to actualize positions. He shows that if opposite motions both achieve the same goal, then no particular motion is superior, and they cannot be combined. The only conclusion is that motion would be abandoned altogether, which contradicts the undeniable fact that motion occurs. Let's see how this reductio works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow settle.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the key claim and its implications
Razi assumes that both east-west and west-east motions equally bring positions from potentiality to actuality. From this equality he deduces that no motion is better, and that combining them is impossible. The only logical outcome is abandoning all motion.
This is a classic reductio: assuming the opponent's premise leads to a contradiction with observed reality.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the opponent's own reasoning actually negates motion, which is absurd because motion exists. Therefore the premise that the purpose is actualization must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن الحركة من المشرق إلى المغرب كما أنها تفيد إخراج الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل، فكذلك الحركة المضادة لها، تفيد هذا المقصود. وإذا كان جميع أنواع الحركات يفيد هذا المقصود، لم يكن بعضها أولى من بعض، ولا يمكن الجمع بينهما. فلم يبق إلا تركها بأسرها فيا ذكرتموه يوجب نفي الحركة، لا حصولها.
The second proof: Motion from east to west, just as it benefits the bringing out of the entities and positions from potentiality to actuality, likewise the motion opposite to it benefits this purpose. And if all types of motions benefit this purpose, then none of them is more deserving than another, and it is not possible to combine them. So nothing remains except to abandon them altogether. Thus what you mentioned necessitates the negation of motion, not its occurrence.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is a reductio ad absurdum. The author states that both east-west motion and its opposite (west-east) equally achieve the goal of actualizing positions. If all types of motion serve the same purpose, then no particular motion is preferred over another, and they cannot be performed simultaneously. Therefore, the only logical outcome is to have no motion at all. However, celestial motion is observed to occur, so the premise that the purpose is actualization must be false. The argument shows that the claim actually entails the denial of motion, contradicting the obvious fact that motion exists.

Key Terms
حركة من المشرق إلى المغرب
الحركة المضادة
جميع أنواع الحركات
لا بعض أولى من بعض
نفي الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that having multiple types of motion achieving the same goal is fine, and that no problem arises.
Correction: The argument shows that if all motions equally achieve the same purpose, then none is preferred, and they cannot be combined, so logically all motion would cease. This contradicts observable motion, thus the premise is false.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude about the claim that the purpose of motion is actualization?
A) It proves the purpose is valid.
B) It shows the claim leads to the negation of motion.
C) It argues for faster motion.
D) It supports the idea of multiple purposes.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof demonstrates that the opponent's view that motion's purpose is to actualize positions leads to the absurd conclusion that no motion can occur, contradicting the evident fact that celestial motion exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often face multiple equally good options. Do you ever end up doing nothing because you cannot decide which is best? How does Razi's argument reveal the flaw in assuming all options are equal? True repentance demands specific action—what if every good deed looks the same to you? Would that paralyze your soul?
?
The proof shows that if all motions achieve the same purpose, none is preferred. Consider your worship: do you sometimes perform acts of devotion mechanically, assuming they all earn the same reward? If so, are you actually performing them with genuine intention, or are you just going through motions that lead to spiritual stagnation?
?
Razi's opponent believed motion's purpose is to actualize positions. What do you believe is the ultimate purpose of your daily movements—work, family, leisure? Could your own reasoning about purpose lead to a contradiction that leaves you motionless? Pry open that assumption today: what would be left if you abandoned all motion that had no deeper purpose?

The Third Proof: The Analogy of a Purposeless Runner
Razi uses an analogy of a man running without purpose to refute the actualization claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses an analogy to refute the claim that the celestial sphere's motion only aims at actualizing potentialities.

Core Idea
An aimless runner is judged insane—so why not the celestial sphere?

Razi's third proof is a powerful analogy that challenges the opponent's claim. By comparing the celestial sphere's motion to a man running without purpose, he forces a critical question. But the opponent objects, saying the sphere is different. Let's see how Razi responds with a logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and conclusion
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that Razi supposes a man running aimlessly and claims any rational person would call him insane.
This sets up the analogy to the celestial sphere.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that the man lacks perfections, so his motion is a waste, while the sphere is already perfect except for the actualizations, so pursuing them is a perfection.
Understand the difference they claim.
3
Analyze Razi's refutation
Razi points out that the opponent has no certain proof that the sphere is perfect in all other attributes. Without proof, the objection fails.
Also note that other purposes are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا لو فرضنا إنساناً أخذ يعدو في جميع مشارق الأرض ومغاربها. ويقول: لا غرض لي في هذه الحركة: إلا إخراج الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل، لقضى كل عاقل عليه بالجنون. فإن قالوا: الفرق بين البابين: أن هذا الإنسان خالي عن أكثر الكمالات المهمة، فكان اشتغاله باستخراج الأيون والأوضاع مانعاً له من تلك الكمالات، بخلاف الفلك فإنه كامل في جميع الصفات، إلا في هذا المعنى، فكان اشتغاله بتحصيلها كمالاً، بالنسبة إليه. قلنا: أنتم ما ذكرتم برهاناً يقينياً في أن نفس الفلك وجسده كاملة في الصفات، إلا في هذا المعنى. فكان كلامكم باطلا. بل قد بينا: أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون مقصوده من هذه الحركة: سائر الوجوه التي عددناها.
The third proof: If we suppose a man who takes to running in all the eastern and western regions of the earth and says: I have no purpose in this motion except the bringing out of the entities and positions from potentiality to actuality, every rational person would judge him to be insane. If they say: The difference between the two cases is that this man is devoid of most important perfections, so his occupation with extracting the entities and positions prevents him from those perfections, unlike the celestial sphere, for it is perfect in all attributes except in this meaning, so its occupation with acquiring them is a perfection relative to it. We say: You have not presented a certain proof that the essence of the celestial sphere and its body are perfect in attributes except in this meaning. So your speech is false. Rather, we have shown that it is not impossible that its purpose from this motion is the other aspects which we have enumerated.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which is an analogy (tamthīl). The author imagines a man running aimlessly across the earth, claiming his only purpose is to actualize positions; any rational person would consider him insane. This is compared to the celestial sphere. An objection is raised: the man lacks other perfections, so his action is a waste, whereas the celestial sphere is perfect except for lacking those actualizations, so pursuing them is a perfection for it. The author refutes this objection by stating that the opponent has not proven that the celestial sphere is perfect in all other attributes. Without such proof, the objection is baseless. Moreover, the author has already shown that other purposes (previously enumerated) are possible, so the claim that actualization is the sole purpose is not established.

Key Terms
إنسان يعدو
مشارق الأرض ومغاربها
جنون
كمالات
برهان يقيني
سائر الوجوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy is unfair because the celestial sphere is not like a man.
Correction: Razi shows that the opponent's claimed difference lacks proof, so the analogy stands until proven otherwise.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the third proof's objection and answer?
A) The celestial sphere is irrational.
B) The objector has no proof that the celestial sphere is perfect in all attributes except actualization.
C) The man's motion is faster than the sphere's.
D) The celestial sphere cannot have other purposes.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof uses analogy to challenge the opponent, and his refutation demands proof for any claimed difference between the analogy and the real case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in an activity without clear purpose, are you not like the aimless runner? What in your life is motion without intention, and how can you bring purpose to it?
?
Do you assume perfection in yourself or others without proof, as the opponents assumed about the celestial sphere? Where might you be accepting claims without certain proof, and how can you challenge that?
?
Razi demands proof for differences between analogy and reality. Where in your religious practice do you accept claims without certain proof? Will you commit to seeking evidence for your beliefs?

Second Argument: Both Directions Yield Same Result
Second argument refuting purpose of celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaw in using the extraction of positions from potentiality to actuality as the purpose of celestial motion, as presented in the second argument.

Core Idea
If opposite motions achieve the same purpose, neither is preferable — so motion becomes unnecessary.

This lesson continues the critique of the claim that celestial motion's purpose is to actualize potential positions. The second argument shows that this purpose would be equally achieved by motion in either direction, leading to a contradiction with observed motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: premise, analogy, conclusion.
The word 'فيا' may be a scribal error for 'فبما'.
2
Identify the logical structure
Identify the three parts: (1) both motions achieve the same purpose, (2) therefore no direction is preferable and they cannot be combined, (3) so motion must be abandoned entirely.
3
Connect to the larger debate
Understand how this argument refutes the opponent's claim about the purpose of motion. The opponent's premise leads to the negation of motion, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن الحركة من المشرق إلى المغرب كما أنها تفيد إخراج الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل ، فكذلك الحركة المضادة لها ، تفيد هذا المقصود . وإذا كان جميع أنواع الحركات يفيد هذا المقصود ، لم يكن بعضها أولى من بعض ، ولا يمكن الجمع بينهما . فلم يبق إلا تركها [بأسرها] فيا ذكرتموه يوجب نفي الحركة ، لا حصولها
The second argument: The motion from east to west, just as it brings about the extraction of places and positions from potentiality to actuality, likewise the opposite motion brings about this purpose. And when all types of motions bring about this purpose, no one of them is more fitting than another, and it is not possible to combine them. So nothing remains except leaving them entirely, and your statement necessitates the negation of motion, not its occurrence.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the claim that the purpose of celestial motion is to extract places and positions from potentiality to actuality. The author argues that if both east-to-west motion and its opposite (west-to-east) equally achieve this extraction, then no motion is more appropriate than another; yet they cannot be combined simultaneously. Therefore, the only logical conclusion is to abandon motion altogether, which contradicts the observed fact of motion. Thus the argument fails to explain motion.

Key Terms
أين
وضع
قوة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One motion is preferred
B) They can be combined
C) No motion would occur
D) Motion is eternal

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that if a purpose is equally achieved by opposite motions, then that purpose cannot explain why motion occurs in one direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a goal in your life that you seek through actions. Are there opposite courses of action that could both achieve the same goal? If so, does this mean your specific action is unnecessary? What would it take for your choice to be truly meaningful?
?
This argument says that if opposite motions both lead to the same outcome, neither is preferable and motion is negated. In your worship, do you sometimes assume that a particular act is essential, when the opposite (e.g., remaining silent vs. speaking) might achieve the same spiritual benefit? How can you discern what is truly required of you?
?
The passage shows that a premise can self-destruct when taken to its logical conclusion. Examine one of your core beliefs about why you act. If opposites could equally satisfy that belief, would your actions still be justified? Are you willing to abandon actions that lack a compelling reason?

The Manic Man Analogy
Third argument uses a manic man analogy to challenge motion purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of a man running aimlessly refutes the claim that the celestial sphere's only purpose is the extraction of places.

Core Idea
Exclusive focus on extraction is irrational, undermining the celestial sphere's supposed purpose.

Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a third argument against the idea that the celestial sphere's motion is solely for extracting places and positions. The author uses a vivid analogy: a man running frantically across the earth with no other goal. The opponent objects that the celestial sphere is perfect in all else, but the response cuts to the core—there is no proof of that perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the hypothetical scenario: a man running east-west claiming his only purpose is to actualize places. Why would any rational person deem him insane?
Notice the absurdity of a single-minded, endless pursuit.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
Find the opponent's response: the celestial sphere is perfect except in this one lacking aspect, so the motion is a perfection for it.
The opponent tries to differentiate the two cases.
3
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author denies the opponent's premise: we have no certain proof that the celestial sphere is perfect in all attributes except this one. Therefore, the analogy holds and the argument fails.
This is a logical refutation that shifts the burden of proof.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument fits into the broader discussion of celestial motion and causality. Why is it important to challenge assumptions about purpose?
The lesson highlights the need for certain proof in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا لو فرضنا إنساناً أخذ يعدو في [جميع] مشارق الأرض ومغاربها . ويقول : لا غرض لي في هذه الحركة : إلا إخراج الأيون والأوضاع من القوة إلى الفعل، لقضى كل عاقل عليه بالجنون . فإن قالوا : الفرق بين البابين : أن هذا الإنسان خالي عن أكثر الكمالات المهمة ، فكان اشتغاله باستخراج الأيون والأوضاع مانعاً له من تلك الكمالات ، بخلاف الفلك فإنه كامل في جميع الصفات، إلا في هذا المعنى ، فكان اشتغاله بتحصيلها كمالاً بالنسبة إليه . [جميع]، قلنا : أنتم ما ذكرتم برهاناً يقينياً في أن نفس الفلك وجسده كاملة في الصفات ، إلا في هذا المعنى . فكان كلامكم باطلا . بل قد بينا : أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون مقصوده من هذه الحركة : سائر الوجوه التي عددناها
The third argument: If we suppose a man who starts running through all the east and west of the earth and says: I have no purpose in this motion except to extract places and positions from potentiality to actuality, every rational person would judge him to be insane. If they say: The difference between the two cases is that this man is devoid of most important perfections, so his occupation with extracting places and positions prevents him from those perfections, unlike the celestial sphere which is perfect in all attributes except in this meaning, so its occupation with acquiring them is a perfection relative to it. [All?], We say: You have not provided a certain proof that the very soul and body of the celestial sphere are perfect in all attributes except in this meaning. So your statement is invalid. Rather, we have shown that it is not impossible that its purpose in this motion is the other aspects which we have enumerated.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer argument. The author poses a hypothetical: a man running around the earth with the sole purpose of extracting places and positions would be called insane. The opponent might respond that the celestial sphere is perfect in all other respects, so such activity is a perfection for it. The author rebuts by denying that we have certain proof that the sphere is perfect except in this one lacking aspect. Thus the analogy stands and the opponent's position is weakened.

Key Takeaway
The manic man analogy exposes the flaw in claiming that the celestial sphere's sole purpose is extraction, as no certain proof exists for its supposed perfection in all other respects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Will you open yourself to the 'other aspects' that God might want to reveal through your circumstances?

Fourth Argument: Extraction as a Perfection or Not
A dilemma argument regarding extraction as a perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dilemma argument refutes the claim that the celestial sphere's motion aims to imitate the separate intellect.

Core Idea
A dilemma can refute an opponent with two alternatives — both defeat their position.

The fourth argument presents a dilemma to challenge the idea that the sphere's motion is for imitating the separate intellect. It examines whether extraction of positions is a perfection worthy of pursuit. By showing that both possibilities lead to contradictions, the argument invalidates the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the dilemma structure.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Note the two horns of the dilemma: extraction is a perfection or it is not.
3
Follow the implications
What follows if it is a perfection? What follows if it is not? Both lead to refutation.
4
Understand the conclusion
The opponent's claim is invalidated regardless of which horn is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : وهي إن استخراج الأيون والأوضاع . إما أن يكون كمالاً لا يصلح أن يكون مقصوداً للعاقل ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول وجب أن يكون مقصود الفلك من الحركة : نفس الحركة . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لبطل قولكم : إن مقصود الفلك من الحركة التشبه بالعقل المفارق . وإن كان الثاني ، وهو أن هذه الاستخراجات أمور لا تصلح أن تكون مطلوبة للعقلاء . فهذا يقدح في كون الفلك طالباً لها . وكل ذلك يبطل قولهم
The fourth argument: The extraction of places and positions is either a perfection that is not suitable to be intended by a rational being, or it is not. If the first, then the purpose of the celestial sphere in motion must be the motion itself. And if that were the case, your statement that the purpose of the celestial sphere in motion is to imitate the separate intellect would become invalid. And if the second, that these extractions are things not suitable to be sought by rational beings, then this detracts from the sphere being a seeker of them. And all of this invalidates their statement.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This is a dilemma argument. The author presents two possibilities regarding the extraction of places and positions: either it is a perfection suitable for a rational being to intend, or it is not. If it is a perfection, then the sphere's purpose in motion would be the motion itself (since the extraction is the motion), contradicting the opponent's claim that the purpose is to imitate the separate intellect. If it is not a perfection, then it is not a proper goal for a rational sphere, which undermines the claim that the sphere seeks it. Both alternatives refute the opponent's position.

Key Terms
كمال
عاقل
عقل مفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only refutes the opponent if extraction is not a perfection.
Correction: The argument shows that both alternatives refute the opponent, so regardless of which is true, the opponent's position is invalidated.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude about extraction being a perfection?
A) It must be a perfection
B) It cannot be a perfection
C) Both alternatives refute the opponent
D) The sphere imitates the intellect anyway

Key Takeaway
The dilemma argument shows that regardless of whether extraction is a perfection, the opponent's claim that the sphere imitates the separate intellect is defeated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit or action you do regularly. Is its true purpose merely the action itself (like the motion being its own purpose), or do you tell yourself it serves a higher goal? If the former, what does this lesson reveal about your own self-deception? Will you honestly reassess that action today?
?
When you face a dilemma in your faith—two conflicting possibilities—do you really consider both horns fully, or do you jump to the one that feels comfortable? What is at stake if you ignore the alternative that refutes your preferred position? Commit to examining one such dilemma this week with the same rigor as this argument.
